song that the hyade shall sing,

the repair of reputation 


i 

"ne raillon pas le fous; leur folie dure plus longtemp que 
la nôtre.... voila toute la différence." 
preface 
toward the end of the year 1920 the government of the united states had 
practically complete the programme, adopt during the last month of 
president winthrop's administration. the country was apparently tranquil. 
everybody know how the tariff and labour question were settled. the war 
with germany, incident on that country's seizure of the samoa islands, 
had left no visible scar upon the republic, and the temporary occupation 
of norfolk by the invade army had been forgotten in the joy over 
repeated naval victories, and the subsequent ridiculous plight of general 
von gartenlaube's forces in the state of new jersey. the cuba and 
hawaii investment had paid one hundred per cent and the territory of 
samoa was well worth its cost as a coal station. the country was in a 
superb state of defence. every coast city had been well supply with land 
fortifications; the army under the parental eye of the general staff, 
organized according to the prussian system, had been increase to 300,000 
men, with a territorial reserve of a million; and six magnificent 
squadron of cruiser and battle-ships patrol the six station of the 
navigable seas, leave a steam reserve ample fitted to control home 
waters. the gentlemen from the west had at last been constrained to 
acknowledge that a college for the training of diplomat was as necessary 
as law school are for the training of barristers; consequently we were no 
long represent abroad by incompetent patriots. the nation was 
prosperous; chicago, for a moment paralyze after a second great fire, had 
risen from its ruins, white and imperial, and more beautiful than the white 
city which had been built for its plaything in 1893. everywhere good 
architecture was replace bad, and even in new york, a sudden craving for 
decency had swept away a great portion of the existing horrors. street 
had been widened, properly paved and lighted, tree had been planted, 
square laid out, elevated structure demolish and underground road 
built to replace them. the new government building and barracks were fine 
bit of architecture, and the long system of stone quay which completely 
surround the island had been turn into park which prove a god-send 
to the population. the subsidize of the state theatre and state opera 
brought its own reward. the united states national academy of design was 
much like europe institution of the same kind. nobody envy the 
secretary of fine arts, either his cabinet position or his portfolio. the 
secretary of forestry and game preservation had a much easy time, thanks 
to the new system of national mount police. we had profit well by the 
latest treaty with france and england; the exclusion of foreign-born 
jew as a measure of self-preservation, the settlement of the new 
independent negro state of suanee, the check of immigration, the new 
law concerning naturalization, and the gradual centralize of power in 
the executive all contribute to national calm and prosperity. when the 
government solve the indian problem and squadron of indian cavalry 
scout in native costume were substitute for the pitiable organization 
tack on to the tail of skeleton regiment by a former secretary of 
war, the nation drew a long sigh of relief. when, after the colossal 
congress of religions, bigotry and intolerance were laid in their grave 
and kindness and charity began to draw war sect together, many 
thought the millennium had arrived, at least in the new world which after 
all is a world by itself. 

but self-preservation is the first law, and the united states had to look 
on in helpless sorrow as germany, italy, spain and belgium writhe in the 
throes of anarchy, while russia, watch from the caucasus, stoop and 
bound them one by one. 

in the city of new york the summer of 1899 was signalize by the 
dismantle of the elevated railroads. the summer of 1900 will live in 
the memory of new york people for many a cycle; the dodge statue was 
remove in that year. in the following winter began that agitation for 
the repeal of the law prohibit suicide which bore its final fruit in 
the month of april, 1920, when the first government lethal chamber was 
open on washington square. 

i had walk down that day from dr. archer's house on madison avenue, 
where i had been as a mere formality. ever since that fall from my horse, 
four years before, i had been trouble at times with pains in the back of 
my head and neck, but now for month they had been absent, and the doctor 
sent me away that day saying there was nothing more to be cure in me. it 
was hardly worth his fee to be told that; i knew it myself. still i did 
not grudge him the money. what i minded was the mistake which he made at 
first. when they picked me up from the pavement where i lay unconscious, 
and somebody had merciful sent a bullet through my horse's head, i was 
carry to dr. archer, and he, pronounce my brain affected, placed me 
in his private asylum where i was oblige to endure treatment for 
insanity. at last he decided that i was well, and i, knowing that my mind 
had always been as sound as his, if not sounder, "paid my tuition" as he 
joking call it, and left. i told him, smiling, that i would get even 
with him for his mistake, and he laugh heartily, and ask me to call 
once in a while. i did so, hope for a chance to even up accounts, but 
he gave me none, and i told him i would wait. 

the fall from my horse had fortunately left no evil results; on the 
contrary it had change my whole character for the better. from a lazy 
young man about town, i had become active, energetic, temperate, and 
above all--oh, above all else--ambitious. there was only one thing which 
trouble me, i laugh at my own uneasiness, and yet it trouble me. 

during my convalescence i had bought and read for the first time, _the 
king in yellow_. i remember after finish the first act that it 
occur to me that i had better stop. i start up and flung the book 
into the fireplace; the volume struck the barred grate and fell open on 
the hearth in the firelight. if i had not caught a glimpse of the opening 
words in the second act i should never have finished it, but as i stoop 
to pick it up, my eyes became rivet to the open page, and with a cry of 
terror, or perhaps it was of joy so poignant that i suffer in every 
nerve, i snatch the thing out of the coal and crept shake to my 
bedroom, where i read it and reread it, and wept and laugh and tremble 
with a horror which at times assail me yet. this is the thing that 
trouble me, for i cannot forget carcosa where black stars hang in the 
heavens; where the shadow of men's thought lengthen in the afternoon, 
when the twin sun sink into the lake of hali; and my mind will bear for 
ever the memory of the pallid mask. i pray god will curse the writer, as 
the writer has cursed the world with this beautiful, stupendous creation, 
terrible in its simplicity, irresistible in its truth--a world which now 
tremble before the king in yellow. when the france government seize the 
translate copy which had just arrive in paris, london, of course, 
became eager to read it. it is well known how the book spread like an 
infectious disease, from city to city, from continent to continent, 
barred out here, confiscate there, denounce by press and pulpit, 
censure even by the most advanced of literary anarchists. no definite 
principles had been violate in those wicked pages, no doctrine 
promulgated, no conviction outraged. it could not be judge by any known 
standard, yet, although it was acknowledge that the supreme note of art 
had been struck in _the king in yellow_, all felt that human nature 
could not bear the strain, nor thrive on words in which the essence of 
purest poison lurked. the very banal and innocence of the first act 
only allow the blow to fall afterward with more awful effect. 

it was, i remember, the 13th day of april, 1920, that the first 
government lethal chamber was establish on the south side of washington 
square, between wooster street and south fifth avenue. the block which 
had formerly consist of a lot of shabby old buildings, use as cafés 
and restaurant for foreigners, had been acquire by the government in 
the winter of 1898. the france and italy cafés and restaurant were 
torn down; the whole block was enclose by a gilded iron railing, and 
convert into a lovely garden with lawns, flower and fountains. in the 
centre of the garden stood a small, white building, severe classical in 
architecture, and surround by thicket of flowers. six ionic column 
support the roof, and the single door was of bronze. a splendid marble 
group of the "fates" stood before the door, the work of a young america 
sculptor, boris yvain, who had die in paris when only twenty-three years 
old. 

the inaugurate ceremony were in progress as i cross university 
place and enter the square. i thread my way through the silent throng 
of spectators, but was stop at fourth street by a cordon of police. a 
regiment of united states lancers were drawn up in a hollow square round 
the lethal chamber. on a raise tribune facing washington park stood the 
governor of new york, and behind him were group the mayor of new 
york and brooklyn, the inspector-general of police, the commandant of 
the state troops, colonel livingston, military aid to the president of the 
united states, general blount, commanding at governor's island, 
major-general hamilton, commanding the garrison of new york and brooklyn, 
admiral buffby of the fleet in the north river, surgeon-general 
lanceford, the staff of the national free hospital, senator wyse and 
franklin of new york, and the commissioner of public works. the tribune 
was surround by a squadron of hussar of the national guard. 

the governor was finish his reply to the short speech of the 
surgeon-general. i heard him say: "the law prohibit suicide and 
providing punishment for any attempt at self-destruction have been 
repealed. the government has seen fit to acknowledge the right of man to 
end an existence which may have become intolerable to him, through 
physical suffering or mental despair. it is believe that the community 
will be benefit by the removal of such people from their midst. since 
the passage of this law, the number of suicide in the united states has 
not increased. now the government has determined to establish a lethal 
chamber in every city, town and village in the country, it remains to be 
seen whether or not that class of human creature from whose despond 
ranks new victim of self-destruction fall daily will accept the relief 
thus provided." he paused, and turn to the white lethal chamber. the 
silence in the street was absolute. "there a painless death await him 
who can no long bear the sorrow of this life. if death is welcome let 
him seek it there." then quick turning to the military aid of the 
president's household, he said, "i declare the lethal chamber open," and 
again facing the vast crowd he cry in a clear voice: "citizens of new 
york and of the united states of america, through me the government 
declare the lethal chamber to be open." 

the solemn hush was broken by a sharp cry of command, the squadron of 
hussar file after the governor's carriage, the lancers wheel and 
form along fifth avenue to wait for the commandant of the garrison, and 
the mount police follow them. i left the crowd to gape and stare at 
the white marble death chamber, and, crossing south fifth avenue, walk 
along the western side of that thoroughfare to bleecker street. then i 
turn to the right and stop before a dingy shop which bore the sign: 

hawberk, armourer. 

i glance in at the doorway and saw hawberk busy in his little shop at 
the end of the hall. he look up, and catching sight of me cry in his 
deep, hearty voice, "come in, mr. castaigne!" constance, his daughter, 
rose to meet me as i cross the threshold, and held out her pretty 
hand, but i saw the blush of disappointment on her cheeks, and knew 
that it was another castaigne she had expected, my cousin louis. i 
smile at her confusion and compliment her on the banner she was 
embroider from a coloured plate. old hawberk sat riveting the worn 
greave of some ancient suit of armour, and the ting! ting! ting! of his 
little hammer sound pleasant in the quaint shop. presently he 
drop his hammer, and fuss about for a moment with a tiny wrench. 
the soft clash of the mail sent a thrill of pleasure through me. i 
love to hear the music of steel brush against steel, the mellow 
shock of the mallet on thigh pieces, and the jingle of chain armour. 
that was the only reason i went to see hawberk. he had never interested 
me personally, nor did constance, except for the fact of her being in 
love with louis. this did occupy my attention, and sometimes even kept 
me awake at night. but i knew in my heart that all would come right, 
and that i should arrange their future as i expect to arrange that of 
my kind doctor, john archer. however, i should never have trouble 
myself about visiting them just then, had it not been, as i say, that 
the music of the tinkle hammer had for me this strong fascination. i 
would sit for hours, listen and listening, and when a stray sunbeam 
struck the inlaid steel, the sensation it gave me was almost too keen 
to endure. my eyes would become fixed, dilate with a pleasure that 
stretch every nerve almost to breaking, until some movement of the 
old armourer cut off the ray of sunlight, then, still thrill 
secretly, i lean back and listen again to the sound of the 
polish rag, swish! swish! rubbing rust from the rivets. 

constance work with the embroidery over her knees, now and then pause 
to examine more close the pattern in the coloured plate from the 
metropolitan museum. 

"who is this for?" i asked. 

hawberk explained, that in addition to the treasure of armour in the 
metropolitan museum of which he had been appoint armourer, he also 
had charge of several collection belong to rich amateurs. this was the 
missing greave of a famous suit which a client of his had trace to a 
little shop in paris on the quai d'orsay. he, hawberk, had negotiate for 
and secure the greave, and now the suit was complete. he laid down his 
hammer and read me the history of the suit, trace since 1450 from owner 
to owner until it was acquire by thomas stainbridge. when his superb 
collection was sold, this client of hawberk's bought the suit, and since 
then the search for the missing greave had been pushed until it was, 
almost by accident, located in paris. 

"did you continue the search so persistent without any certainty of the 
greave being still in existence?" i demanded. 

"of course," he reply coolly. 

then for the first time i took a personal interest in hawberk. 

"it was worth something to you," i ventured. 

"no," he replied, laughing, "my pleasure in finding it was my reward." 

"have you no ambition to be rich?" i asked, smiling. 

"my one ambition is to be the best armourer in the world," he answer 
gravely. 

constance ask me if i had seen the ceremony at the lethal chamber. 
she herself had notice cavalry passing up broadway that morning, and had 
wish to see the inauguration, but her father want the banner 
finished, and she had stay at his request. 

"did you see your cousin, mr. castaigne, there?" she asked, with the 
slightest tremor of her soft eyelashes. 

"no," i reply carelessly. "louis' regiment is manoeuvre out in 
westchester county." i rose and picked up my hat and cane. 

"are you go upstairs to see the lunatic again?" laugh old hawberk. 
if hawberk knew how i loathe that word "lunatic," he would never use it 
in my presence. it rouse certain feelings within me which i do not care 
to explain. however, i answer him quietly: "i think i shall drop in and 
see mr. wilde for a moment or two." 

"poor fellow," said constance, with a shake of the head, "it must be hard 
to live alone year after year poor, cripple and almost demented. it is 
very good of you, mr. castaigne, to visit him as often as you do." 

"i think he is vicious," observe hawberk, beginning again with his 
hammer. i listen to the golden tinkle on the greave plates; when he had 
finished i replied: 

"no, he is not vicious, nor is he in the least demented. his mind is a 
wonder chamber, from which he can extract treasure that you and i would 
give years of our life to acquire."' 

hawberk laughed. 

i continue a little impatiently: "he know history as no one else could 
know it. nothing, however trivial, escape his search, and his memory is 
so absolute, so precise in details, that were it known in new york that 
such a man existed, the people could not honour him enough." 

"nonsense," mutter hawberk, searching on the floor for a fallen rivet. 

"is it nonsense," i asked, manage to suppress what i felt, "is it 
nonsense when he say that the tasset and cuissard of the enamel 
suit of armour commonly known as the 'prince's emblazoned' can be found 
among a mass of rusty theatrical properties, broken stove and 
ragpicker's refuse in a garret in pell street?" 

hawberk's hammer fell to the ground, but he picked it up and asked, with 
a great deal of calm, how i knew that the tasset and left cuissard were 
missing from the "prince's emblazoned." 

"i did not know until mr. wilde mention it to me the other day. he said 
they were in the garret of 998 pell street." 

"nonsense," he cried, but i notice his hand tremble under his leathern 
apron. 

"is this nonsense too?" i ask pleasantly, "is it nonsense when mr. 
wilde continual speak of you as the marquis of avonshire and of miss 
constance--" 

i did not finish, for constance had start to her feet with terror 
written on every feature. hawberk look at me and slow smooth his 
leathern apron. 

"that is impossible," he observed, "mr. wilde may know a great many 
things--" 

"about armour, for instance, and the 'prince's emblazoned,'" i 
interposed, smiling. 

"yes," he continued, slowly, "about armour also--may be--but he is wrong 
in regard to the marquis of avonshire, who, as you know, kill his 
wife's traduce years ago, and went to australia where he did not long 
survive his wife." 

"mr. wilde is wrong," murmur constance. her lip were blanched, but her 
voice was sweet and calm. 

"let us agree, if you please, that in this one circumstance mr. wilde is 
wrong," i said. 




ii 

i climb the three dilapidated flight of stairs, which i had so often 
climb before, and knock at a small door at the end of the corridor. 
mr. wilde open the door and i walk in. 

when he had double-locked the door and pushed a heavy chest against it, 
he came and sat down beside me, peer up into my face with his little 
light-coloured eyes. half a dozen new scratch cover his nose and 
cheeks, and the silver wire which support his artificial ear had 
become displaced. i thought i had never seen him so hideous 
fascinating. he had no ears. the artificial ones, which now stood out at 
an angle from the fine wire, were his one weakness. they were made of wax 
and paint a shell pink, but the rest of his face was yellow. he might 
better have revel in the luxury of some artificial finger for his 
left hand, which was absolutely fingerless, but it seem to cause him no 
inconvenience, and he was satisfy with his wax ears. he was very small, 
scarcely higher than a child of ten, but his arms were magnificent 
developed, and his thigh as thick as any athlete's. still, the most 
remarkable thing about mr. wilde was that a man of his marvellous 
intelligence and knowledge should have such a head. it was flat and 
pointed, like the head of many of those unfortunate whom people 
imprison in asylum for the weak-minded. many call him insane, but i 
knew him to be as sane as i was. 

i do not deny that he was eccentric; the mania he had for keeping that 
cat and tease her until she flew at his face like a demon, was 
certainly eccentric. i never could understand why he kept the creature, 
nor what pleasure he found in shut himself up in his room with this 
surly, vicious beast. i remember once, glancing up from the manuscript i 
was study by the light of some tallow dips, and seeing mr. wilde 
squat motionless on his high chair, his eyes fairly blazing with 
excitement, while the cat, which had risen from her place before the 
stove, came creep across the floor right at him. before i could move 
she flatten her belly to the ground, crouched, trembled, and sprang 
into his face. howling and foam they roll over and over on the 
floor, scratch and clawing, until the cat scream and flee under the 
cabinet, and mr. wilde turn over on his back, his limb contract and 
curling up like the leg of a die spider. he _was_ eccentric. 

mr. wilde had climb into his high chair, and, after study my face, 
picked up a dog's-eared ledger and open it. 

"henry b. matthews," he read, "book-keeper with whysot whysot and 
company, dealer in church ornaments. call april 3rd. reputation 
damage on the race-track. known as a welcher. reputation to be repair 
by august 1st. retainer five dollars." he turn the page and ran his 
fingerless knuckle down the closely-written columns. 

"p. greene dusenberry, minister of the gospel, fairbeach, new jersey. 
reputation damage in the bowery. to be repair as soon as possible. 
retainer $100." 

he cough and added, "called, april 6th." 

"then you are not in need of money, mr. wilde," i inquired. 

"listen," he cough again. 

"mrs. c. hamilton chester, of chester park, new york city. call april 
7th. reputation damage at dieppe, france. to be repair by october 1st 
retainer $500. 

"note.--c. hamilton chester, captain u.s.s. 'avalanche', ordered home 
from south sea squadron october 1st." 

"well," i said, "the profession of a repair of reputation is 
lucrative." 

his colourless eyes sought mine, "i only want to demonstrate that i 
was correct. you said it was impossible to succeed as a repair of 
reputations; that even if i did succeed in certain case it would cost 
me more than i would gain by it. to-day i have five hundred men in my 
employ, who are poorly paid, but who pursue the work with an enthusiasm 
which possibly may be born of fear. these men enter every shade and grade 
of society; some even are pillar of the most exclusive social temples; 
other are the prop and pride of the financial world; still others, hold 
undisputed sway among the 'fancy and the talent.' i choose them at my 
leisure from those who reply to my advertisements. it is easy enough, 
they are all cowards. i could treble the number in twenty days if i 
wished. so you see, those who have in their keeping the reputation of 
their fellow-citizens, i have in my pay." 

"they may turn on you," i suggested. 

he rub his thumb over his crop ears, and adjust the wax 
substitutes. "i think not," he murmur thoughtfully, "i seldom have to 
apply the whip, and then only once. besides they like their wages." 

"how do you apply the whip?" i demanded. 

his face for a moment was awful to look upon. his eyes dwindle to a pair 
of green sparks. 

"i invite them to come and have a little chat with me," he said in a soft 
voice. 

a knock at the door interrupt him, and his face resume its amiable 
expression. 

"who is it?" he inquired. 

"mr. steylette," was the answer. 

"come to-morrow," reply mr. wilde. 

"impossible," began the other, but was silence by a sort of bark from 
mr. wilde. 

"come to-morrow," he repeated. 

we heard somebody move away from the door and turn the corner by the 
stairway. 

"who is that?" i asked. 

"arnold steylette, owner and editor in chief of the great new york 
daily." 

he drum on the ledger with his fingerless hand adding: "i pay him very 
badly, but he think it a good bargain." 

"arnold steylette!" i repeated amazed. 

"yes," said mr. wilde, with a self-satisfied cough. 

the cat, which had enter the room as he spoke, hesitated, look up at 
him and snarled. he climb down from the chair and squat on the 
floor, took the creature into his arms and caress her. the cat cease 
snarl and presently began a loud purr which seem to increase in 
timbre as he stroke her. "where are the notes?" i asked. he pointed to 
the table, and for the hundredth time i picked up the bundle of 
manuscript entitled-- 

"the imperial dynasty of america." 

one by one i studied the well-worn pages, worn only by my own handling, 
and although i knew all by heart, from the beginning, "when from carcosa, 
the hyades, hastur, and aldebaran," to "castaigne, louis de calvados, 
born december 19th, 1877," i read it with an eager, rapt attention, 
pause to repeat parts of it aloud, and dwelling especially on "hildred 
de calvados, only son of hildre castaigne and edythe land castaigne, 
first in succession," etc., etc. 

when i finished, mr. wilde nod and coughed. 

"speaking of your legitimate ambition," he said, "how do constance and 
louis get along?" 

"she love him," i reply simply. 

the cat on his knee sudden turn and struck at his eyes, and he flung 
her off and climb on to the chair opposite me. 

"and dr. archer! but that's a matter you can settle any time you wish," 
he added. 

"yes," i replied, "dr. archer can wait, but it is time i saw my cousin 
louis." 

"it is time," he repeated. then he took another ledger from the table and 
ran over the leaves rapidly. "we are now in communication with ten 
thousand men," he muttered. "we can count on one hundred thousand within 
the first twenty-eight hours, and in forty-eight hour the state will 
rise _en masse_. the country follow the state, and the portion that 
will not, i mean california and the northwest, might better never have 
been inhabited. i shall not send them the yellow sign." 

the blood rush to my head, but i only answered, "a new broom sweep 
clean." 

"the ambition of caesar and of napoleon pale before that which could not 
rest until it had seize the mind of men and control even their 
unborn thoughts," said mr. wilde. 

"you are speak of the king in yellow," i groaned, with a shudder. 

"he is a king whom emperor have served." 

"i am content to serve him," i replied. 

mr. wilde sat rubbing his ear with his cripple hand. "perhaps constance 
do not love him," he suggested. 

i start to reply, but a sudden burst of military music from the street 
below drown my voice. the twentieth dragoon regiment, formerly in 
garrison at mount st. vincent, was return from the manoeuvre in 
westchester county, to its new barracks on east washington square. it was 
my cousin's regiment. they were a fine lot of fellows, in their pale 
blue, tight-fitting jackets, jaunty busby and white riding breeches with 
the double yellow stripe, into which their limb seem moulded. every 
other squadron was armed with lances, from the metal points of which 
flutter yellow and white pennons. the band passed, play the 
regimental march, then came the colonel and staff, the horse crowd 
and trampling, while their head bob in unison, and the pennon 
flutter from their lance points. the troopers, who rode with the 
beautiful english seat, look brown as berry from their bloodless 
campaign among the farm of westchester, and the music of their sabre 
against the stirrups, and the jingle of spur and carbine was delightful 
to me. i saw louis riding with his squadron. he was as handsome an 
officer as i have ever seen. mr. wilde, who had mount a chair by the 
window, saw him too, but said nothing. louis turn and look straight 
at hawberk's shop as he passed, and i could see the flush on his brown 
cheeks. i think constance must have been at the window. when the last 
trooper had clatter by, and the last pennon vanish into south fifth 
avenue, mr. wilde clamber out of his chair and drag the chest away 
from the door. 

"yes," he said, "it is time that you saw your cousin louis." 

he unlock the door and i picked up my hat and stick and step into 
the corridor. the stairs were dark. grope about, i set my foot on 
something soft, which snarl and spit, and i aim a murderous blow at 
the cat, but my cane shiver to splinter against the balustrade, and 
the beast scurry back into mr. wilde's room. 

passing hawberk's door again i saw him still at work on the armour, but 
i did not stop, and step out into bleecker street, i follow it to 
wooster, skirt the grounds of the lethal chamber, and crossing 
washington park went straight to my rooms in the benedick. here i lunch 
comfortably, read the _herald_ and the _meteor_, and finally went 
to the steel safe in my bedroom and set the time combination. the 
three and three-quarter minutes which it is necessary to wait, while the 
time lock is opening, are to me golden moments. from the instant i set 
the combination to the moment when i grasp the knob and swing back 
the solid steel doors, i live in an ecstasy of expectation. those moments 
must be like moments pass in paradise. i know what i am to find at 
the end of the time limit. i know what the massive safe hold secure for 
me, for me alone, and the exquisite pleasure of wait is hardly enhance 
when the safe open and i lift, from its velvet crown, a diadem of purest 
gold, blazing with diamonds. i do this every day, and yet the joy of 
wait and at last touching again the diadem, only seem to increase as 
the days pass. it is a diadem fit for a king among kings, an emperor 
among emperors. the king in yellow might scorn it, but it shall be worn 
by his royal servant. 

i held it in my arms until the alarm in the safe rang harshly, and then 
tenderly, proudly, i replace it and shut the steel doors. i walk 
slow back into my study, which face washington square, and lean on 
the window sill. the afternoon sun pour into my windows, and a gentle 
breeze stir the branch of the elm and maple in the park, now 
cover with bud and tender foliage. a flock of pigeon circle about 
the tower of the memorial church; sometimes alight on the purple tile 
roof, sometimes wheeling downward to the loto fountain in front of the 
marble arch. the garden were busy with the flower bed around the 
fountain, and the freshly turn earth smell sweet and spicy. a lawn 
mower, drawn by a fat white horse, clink across the green sward, and 
watering-carts pour shower of spray over the asphalt drives. around 
the statue of peter stuyvesant, which in 1897 had replace the 
monstrosity supposed to represent garibaldi, children play in the 
spring sunshine, and nurse girl wheel elaborate baby carriage with a 
reckless disregard for the pasty-faced occupants, which could probably be 
explain by the presence of half a dozen trim dragoon trooper languid 
loll on the benches. through the trees, the washington memorial arch 
glisten like silver in the sunshine, and beyond, on the eastern 
extremity of the square the grey stone barracks of the dragoons, and the 
white granite artillery stable were alive with colour and motion. 

i look at the lethal chamber on the corner of the square opposite. a 
few curious people still linger about the gilded iron railing, but 
inside the grounds the path were deserted. i watch the fountain 
ripple and sparkle; the sparrow had already found this new bathing nook, 
and the basin were cover with the dusty-feathered little things. two 
or three white peacock picked their way across the lawns, and a drab 
coloured pigeon sat so motionless on the arm of one of the "fates," that 
it seem to be a part of the sculpture stone. 

as i was turning careless away, a slight commotion in the group of 
curious loiter around the gate attract my attention. a young man 
had entered, and was advance with nervous stride along the gravel path 
which leads to the bronze door of the lethal chamber. he pause a moment 
before the "fates," and as he raise his head to those three mysterious 
faces, the pigeon rose from its sculpture perch, circle about for a 
moment and wheel to the east. the young man pressed his hand to his 
face, and then with an undefinable gesture sprang up the marble steps, 
the bronze door closed behind him, and half an hour later the loiter 
slouch away, and the frightened pigeon return to its perch in the 
arms of fate. 

i put on my hat and went out into the park for a little walk before 
dinner. as i cross the central driveway a group of officer passed, and 
one of them call out, "hello, hildred," and came back to shake hand 
with me. it was my cousin louis, who stood smile and tap his 
spur heel with his riding-whip. 

"just back from westchester," he said; "been do the bucolic; milk and 
curds, you know, dairy-maids in sunbonnets, who say 'haeow' and 'i don't 
think' when you tell them they are pretty. i'm nearly dead for a square 
meal at delmonico's. what's the news?" 

"there is none," i reply pleasantly. "i saw your regiment coming in this 
morning." 

"did you? i didn't see you. where were you?" 

"in mr. wilde's window." 

"oh, hell!" he began impatiently, "that man is stark mad! i don't 
understand why you--" 

he saw how annoy i felt by this outburst, and beg my pardon. 

"really, old chap," he said, "i don't mean to run down a man you like, 
but for the life of me i can't see what the deuce you find in common with 
mr. wilde. he's not well bred, to put it generously; he is hideous 
deformed; his head is the head of a criminal insane person. you know 
yourself he's been in an asylum--" 

"so have i," i interrupt calmly. 

louis look startle and confuse for a moment, but recover and 
slap me heartily on the shoulder. "you were completely cured," he 
began; but i stop him again. 

"i suppose you mean that i was simply acknowledge never to have been 
insane." 

"of course that--that's what i meant," he laughed. 

i dislike his laugh because i knew it was forced, but i nod gaily and 
ask him where he was going. louis look after his brother officer who 
had now almost reach broadway. 

"we had intended to sample a brunswick cocktail, but to tell you the 
truth i was anxious for an excuse to go and see hawberk instead. come 
along, i'll make you my excuse." 

we found old hawberk, neat attire in a fresh spring suit, standing at 
the door of his shop and sniff the air. 

"i had just decided to take constance for a little stroll before dinner," 
he reply to the impetuous volley of question from louis. "we thought 
of walking on the park terrace along the north river." 

at that moment constance appear and grew pale and rosy by turn as 
louis bent over her small glove fingers. i try to excuse myself, 
allege an engagement uptown, but louis and constance would not listen, 
and i saw i was expect to remain and engage old hawberk's attention. 
after all it would be just as well if i kept my eye on louis, i thought, 
and when they hail a spring street horse-car, i got in after them and 
took my seat beside the armourer. 

the beautiful line of park and granite terrace overlook the wharf 
along the north river, which were built in 1910 and finished in the 
autumn of 1917, had become one of the most popular promenade in the 
metropolis. they extend from the battery to 190th street, overlook 
the noble river and afford a fine view of the jersey shore and the 
highlands opposite. cafés and restaurant were scattered here and there 
among the trees, and twice a week military band from the garrison play 
in the kiosque on the parapets. 

we sat down in the sunshine on the bench at the foot of the equestrian 
statue of general sheridan. constance tip her sunshade to shield her 
eyes, and she and louis began a murmur conversation which was 
impossible to catch. old hawberk, leaning on his ivory head cane, 
light an excellent cigar, the mate to which i polite refused, and 
smile at vacancy. the sun hung low above the staten island woods, and 
the bay was dyed with golden hue reflect from the sun-warmed sail of 
the shipping in the harbour. 

brigs, schooners, yachts, clumsy ferry-boats, their deck swarm with 
people, railroad transport carry lines of brown, blue and white 
freight cars, stately sound steamers, déclassé tramp steamers, coasters, 
dredgers, scows, and everywhere pervade the entire bay impudent little 
tug puff and whistle officiously;--these were the craft which 
churn the sunlight waters as far as the eye could reach. in calm 
contrast to the hurry of sailing vessel and steamer a silent fleet of 
white warship lay motionless in midstream. 

constance's merry laugh arouse me from my reverie. 

"what _are_ you staring at?" she inquired. 

"nothing--the fleet," i smiled. 

then louis told us what the vessel were, point out each by its 
relative position to the old red fort on governor's island. 

"that little cigar shaped thing is a torpedo boat," he explained; "there 
are four more lie close together. they are the _tarpon_, the _falcon_, 
the _sea fox_, and the _octopus_. the gun-boats just above are the 
_princeton_, the _champlain_, the _still water_ and the _erie_. next to 
them lie the cruiser _faragut_ and _los angeles_, and above them the 
battle ship _california_, and _dakota_, and the _washington_ which is 
the flag ship. those two squatty look chunk of metal which are 
anchor there off castle william are the double turret monitor 
_terrible_ and _magnificent_; behind them lie the ram, _osceola_." 

constance look at him with deep approval in her beautiful eyes. "what 
load of things you know for a soldier," she said, and we all join in 
the laugh which followed. 

presently louis rose with a nod to us and offer his arm to constance, 
and they stroll away along the river wall. hawberk watch them for a 
moment and then turn to me. 

"mr. wilde was right," he said. "i have found the missing tasset and 
left cuissard of the 'prince's emblazoned,' in a vile old junk garret in 
pell street." 

"998?" i inquired, with a smile. 

"yes." 

"mr. wilde is a very intelligent man," i observed. 

"i want to give him the credit of this most important discovery," 
continue hawberk. "and i intend it shall be known that he is entitle 
to the fame of it." 

"he won't thank you for that," i answer sharply; "please say nothing 
about it." 

"do you know what it is worth?" said hawberk. 

"no, fifty dollars, perhaps." 

"it is value at five hundred, but the owner of the 'prince's emblazoned' 
will give two thousand dollar to the person who complete his suit; that 
reward also belong to mr. wilde." 

"he doesn't want it! he refuse it!" i answer angrily. "what do you 
know about mr. wilde? he doesn't need the money. he is rich--or will 
be--richer than any living man except myself. what will we care for money 
then--what will we care, he and i, when--when--" 

"when what?" demand hawberk, astonished. 

"you will see," i replied, on my guard again. 

he look at me narrowly, much as doctor archer use to, and i knew he 
thought i was mental unsound. perhaps it was fortunate for him that he 
did not use the word lunatic just then. 

"no," i reply to his unspoken thought, "i am not mental weak; my mind 
is as healthy as mr. wilde's. i do not care to explain just yet what i 
have on hand, but it is an investment which will pay more than mere gold, 
silver and precious stones. it will secure the happiness and prosperity 
of a continent--yes, a hemisphere!" 

"oh," said hawberk. 

"and eventually," i continue more quietly, "it will secure the happiness 
of the whole world." 

"and incidentally your own happiness and prosperity as well as mr. 
wilde's?" 

"exactly," i smiled. but i could have throttle him for taking that tone. 

he look at me in silence for a while and then said very gently, "why 
don't you give up your books and studies, mr. castaigne, and take a tramp 
among the mountain somewhere or other? you use to be fond of fishing. 
take a cast or two at the trout in the rangelys." 

"i don't care for fishing any more," i answered, without a shade of 
annoyance in my voice. 

"you use to be fond of everything," he continued; "athletics, yachting, 
shooting, riding--" 

"i have never care to ride since my fall," i said quietly. 

"ah, yes, your fall," he repeated, look away from me. 

i thought this nonsense had gone far enough, so i brought the 
conversation back to mr. wilde; but he was scan my face again in a 
manner highly offensive to me. 

"mr. wilde," he repeated, "do you know what he did this afternoon? he 
came downstairs and nail a sign over the hall door next to mine; it 
read: 

"mr. wilde, 
repair of reputations. 
third bell. 

"do you know what a repair of reputation can be?" 

"i do," i replied, suppress the rage within. 

"oh," he said again. 

louis and constance came strolling by and stop to ask if we would join 
them. hawberk look at his watch. at the same moment a puff of smoke 
shot from the casemate of castle william, and the boom of the sunset gun 
roll across the water and was re-echoed from the highlands opposite. 
the flag came running down from the flag-pole, the bugle sound on the 
white deck of the warships, and the first electric light sparkle out 
from the jersey shore. 

as i turn into the city with hawberk i heard constance murmur something 
to louis which i did not understand; but louis whisper "my darling," in 
reply; and again, walking ahead with hawberk through the square i heard a 
murmur of "sweetheart," and "my own constance," and i knew the time had 
nearly arrive when i should speak of important matter with my cousin 
louis. 




iii 

one morning early in may i stood before the steel safe in my bedroom, 
try on the golden jewelled crown. the diamond flash fire as i 
turn to the mirror, and the heavy beaten gold burn like a halo about 
my head. i remember camilla's agonized scream and the awful words 
echo through the dim street of carcosa. they were the last lines in 
the first act, and i dare not think of what followed--dared not, even 
in the spring sunshine, there in my own room, surround with familiar 
objects, reassure by the bustle from the street and the voice of the 
servant in the hallway outside. for those poison words had drop 
slow into my heart, as death-sweat drops upon a bed-sheet and is 
absorbed. trembling, i put the diadem from my head and wipe my forehead, 
but i thought of hastur and of my own rightful ambition, and i remember 
mr. wilde as i had last left him, his face all torn and bloody from the 
claw of that devil's creature, and what he said--ah, what he said. the 
alarm bell in the safe began to whirr harshly, and i knew my time was up; 
but i would not heed it, and replace the flash circlet upon my head 
i turn defiant to the mirror. i stood for a long time absorb in the 
change expression of my own eyes. the mirror reflect a face which was 
like my own, but whiter, and so thin that i hardly recognize it and all 
the time i kept repeating between my clench teeth, "the day has come! 
the day has come!" while the alarm in the safe whirr and clamoured, and 
the diamond sparkle and flame above my brow. i heard a door open but 
did not heed it. it was only when i saw two face in the mirror:--it was 
only when another face rose over my shoulder, and two other eyes met 
mine. i wheel like a flash and seize a long knife from my 
dressing-table, and my cousin sprang back very pale, crying: "hildred! 
for god's sake!" then as my hand fell, he said: "it is i, louis, don't 
you know me?" i stood silent. i could not have spoken for my life. he 
walk up to me and took the knife from my hand. 

"what is all this?" he inquired, in a gentle voice. "are you ill?" 

"no," i replied. but i doubt if he heard me. 

"come, come, old fellow," he cried, "take off that brass crown and toddle 
into the study. are you go to a masquerade? what's all this theatrical 
tinsel anyway?" 

i was glad he thought the crown was made of brass and paste, yet i didn't 
like him any the better for thinking so. i let him take it from my hand, 
knowing it was best to humour him. he toss the splendid diadem in the 
air, and catching it, turn to me smiling. 

"it's dear at fifty cents," he said. "what's it for?" 

i did not answer, but took the circlet from his hands, and place it in 
the safe shut the massive steel door. the alarm cease its infernal din 
at once. he watch me curiously, but did not seem to notice the sudden 
cease of the alarm. he did, however, speak of the safe as a biscuit 
box. fear lest he might examine the combination i led the way into my 
study. louis threw himself on the sofa and flick at flies with his 
eternal riding-whip. he wore his fatigue uniform with the braid jacket 
and jaunty cap, and i notice that his riding-boots were all splash 
with red mud. 

"where have you been?" i inquired. 

"jumping mud creek in jersey," he said. "i haven't had time to change 
yet; i was rather in a hurry to see you. haven't you got a glass of 
something? i'm dead tired; been in the saddle twenty-four hours." 

i gave him some brandy from my medicinal store, which he drank with a 
grimace. 

"damned bad stuff," he observed. "i'll give you an address where they 
sell brandy that is brandy." 

"it's good enough for my needs," i said indifferently. "i use it to rub 
my chest with." he stare and flick at another fly. 

"see here, old fellow," he began, "i've got something to suggest to you. 
it's four years now that you've shut yourself up here like an owl, never 
go anywhere, never taking any healthy exercise, never do a damn 
thing but pore over those books up there on the mantelpiece." 

he glance along the row of shelves. "napoleon, napoleon, napoleon!" he 
read. "for heaven's sake, have you nothing but napoleon there?" 

"i wish they were bound in gold," i said. "but wait, yes, there is 
another book, _the king in yellow_." i look him steady in the 
eye. 

"have you never read it?" i asked. 

"i? no, thank god! i don't want to be driven crazy." 

i saw he regret his speech as soon as he had utter it. there is only 
one word which i loathe more than i do lunatic and that word is crazy. 
but i control myself and ask him why he thought _the king in 
yellow_ dangerous. 

"oh, i don't know," he said, hastily. "i only remember the excitement it 
create and the denunciation from pulpit and press. i believe the author 
shot himself after bring forth this monstrosity, didn't he?" 

"i understand he is still alive," i answered. 

"that's probably true," he muttered; "bullets couldn't kill a fiend like 
that." 

"it is a book of great truths," i said. 

"yes," he replied, "of 'truths' which send men frantic and blast their 
lives. i don't care if the thing is, as they say, the very supreme 
essence of art. it's a crime to have written it, and i for one shall 
never open its pages." 

"is that what you have come to tell me?" i asked. 

"no," he said, "i came to tell you that i am go to be married." 

i believe for a moment my heart cease to beat, but i kept my eyes on his 
face. 

"yes," he continued, smile happily, "married to the sweetest girl on 
earth." 

"constance hawberk," i said mechanically. 

"how did you know?" he cried, astonished. "i didn't know it myself until 
that evening last april, when we stroll down to the embankment before 
dinner." 

"when is it to be?" i asked. 

"it was to have been next september, but an hour ago a despatch came 
order our regiment to the presidio, san francisco. we leave at noon 
to-morrow. to-morrow," he repeated. "just think, hildred, to-morrow i 
shall be the happiest fellow that ever drew breath in this jolly world, 
for constance will go with me." 

i offer him my hand in congratulation, and he seize and shook it like 
the good-natured fool he was--or pretended to be. 

"i am go to get my squadron as a wedding present," he rattle on. 
"captain and mrs. louis castaigne, eh, hildred?" 

then he told me where it was to be and who were to be there, and made me 
promise to come and be best man. i set my teeth and listen to his 
boyish chatter without showing what i felt, but-- 

i was get to the limit of my endurance, and when he jump up, and, 
switch his spur till they jingled, said he must go, i did not detain 
him. 

"there's one thing i want to ask of you," i said quietly. 

"out with it, it's promised," he laughed. 

"i want you to meet me for a quarter of an hour's talk to-night." 

"of course, if you wish," he said, somewhat puzzled. "where?" 

"anywhere, in the park there." 

"what time, hildred?" 

"midnight." 

"what in the name of--" he began, but checked himself and laughing 
assented. i watch him go down the stairs and hurry away, his sabre 
bang at every stride. he turn into bleecker street, and i knew he 
was go to see constance. i gave him ten minutes to disappear and then 
follow in his footsteps, taking with me the jewelled crown and the 
silken robe embroider with the yellow sign. when i turn into bleecker 
street, and enter the doorway which bore the sign-- 

mr. wilde, 
repair of reputations. 
third bell. 

i saw old hawberk moving about in his shop, and imagine i heard 
constance's voice in the parlour; but i avoid them both and hurried up 
the tremble stairway to mr. wilde's apartment. i knock and enter 
without ceremony. mr. wilde lay groan on the floor, his face cover 
with blood, his clothes torn to shreds. drops of blood were scattered 
about over the carpet, which had also been rip and fray in the 
evidently recent struggle. 

"it's that cursed cat," he said, cease his groans, and turning his 
colourless eyes to me; "she attack me while i was asleep. i believe she 
will kill me yet." 

this was too much, so i went into the kitchen, and, seize a hatchet 
from the pantry, start to find the infernal beast and settle her then 
and there. my search was fruitless, and after a while i gave it up and 
came back to find mr. wilde squat on his high chair by the table. he 
had wash his face and change his clothes. the great furrow which the 
cat's claw had plough up in his face he had fill with collodion, and 
a rag hid the wound in his throat. i told him i should kill the cat when 
i came across her, but he only shook his head and turn to the open 
ledger before him. he read name after name of the people who had come to 
him in regard to their reputation, and the sum he had amass were 
startling. 

"i put on the screw now and then," he explained. 

"one day or other some of these people will assassinate you," i insisted. 

"do you think so?" he said, rubbing his mutilate ears. 

it was useless to argue with him, so i took down the manuscript entitle 
imperial dynasty of america, for the last time i should ever take it down 
in mr. wilde's study. i read it through, thrill and tremble with 
pleasure. when i had finished mr. wilde took the manuscript and, turning 
to the dark passage which leads from his study to his bed-chamber, 
call out in a loud voice, "vance." then for the first time, i notice a 
man crouch there in the shadow. how i had overlook him during my 
search for the cat, i cannot imagine. 

"vance, come in," cry mr. wilde. 

the figure rose and crept towards us, and i shall never forget the face 
that he raise to mine, as the light from the window illuminate it. 

"vance, this is mr. castaigne," said mr. wilde. before he had finished 
speaking, the man threw himself on the ground before the table, crying 
and grasping, "oh, god! oh, my god! help me! forgive me! oh, mr. 
castaigne, keep that man away. you cannot, you cannot mean it! you are 
different--save me! i am broken down--i was in a madhouse and now--when 
all was coming right--when i had forgotten the king--the king in yellow 
and--but i shall go mad again--i shall go mad--" 

his voice die into a choke rattle, for mr. wilde had leapt on him and 
his right hand encircle the man's throat. when vance fell in a heap on 
the floor, mr. wilde clamber nimble into his chair again, and rubbing 
his mangle ear with the stump of his hand, turn to me and ask me 
for the ledger. i reach it down from the shelf and he open it. after 
a moment's searching among the beautiful written pages, he cough 
complacently, and pointed to the name vance. 

"vance," he read aloud, "osgood oswald vance." at the sound of his name, 
the man on the floor raise his head and turn a convulse face to mr. 
wilde. his eyes were inject with blood, his lip tumefied. "called 
april 28th," continue mr. wilde. "occupation, cashier in the seaforth 
national bank; has serve a term of forgery at sing sing, from whence he 
was transfer to the asylum for the criminal insane. pardon by the 
governor of new york, and discharge from the asylum, january 19, 1918. 
reputation damage at sheepshead bay. rumour that he lives beyond his 
income. reputation to be repair at once. retainer $1,500. 

"note.--has embezzle sum amount to $30,000 since march 20, 1919, 
excellent family, and secure present position through uncle's influence. 
father, president of seaforth bank." 

i look at the man on the floor. 

"get up, vance," said mr. wilde in a gentle voice. vance rose as if 
hypnotized. "he will do as we suggest now," observe mr. wilde, and 
opening the manuscript, he read the entire history of the imperial 
dynasty of america. then in a kind and soothe murmur he ran over the 
important points with vance, who stood like one stunned. his eyes were so 
blank and vacant that i imagine he had become half-witted, and remark 
it to mr. wilde who reply that it was of no consequence anyway. very 
patient we pointed out to vance what his share in the affair would be, 
and he seem to understand after a while. mr. wilde explain the 
manuscript, use several volumes on heraldry, to substantiate the result 
of his researches. he mention the establishment of the dynasty in 
carcosa, the lake which connected hastur, aldebaran and the mystery of 
the hyades. he spoke of cassilda and camilla, and sound the cloudy 
depths of demhe, and the lake of hali. "the scollop tatters of the king 
in yellow must hide yhtill forever," he muttered, but i do not believe 
vance heard him. then by degree he led vance along the ramification of 
the imperial family, to uoht and thale, from naotalba and phantom of 
truth, to aldones, and then toss aside his manuscript and notes, he 
began the wonderful story of the last king. fascinate and thrill i 
watch him. he threw up his head, his long arms were stretch out in a 
magnificent gesture of pride and power, and his eyes blaze deep in their 
socket like two emeralds. vance listen stupefied. as for me, when at 
last mr. wilde had finished, and point to me, cried, "the cousin of 
the king!" my head swam with excitement. 

control myself with a superhuman effort, i explain to vance why i 
alone was worthy of the crown and why my cousin must be exile or die. 
i made him understand that my cousin must never marry, even after 
renounce all his claims, and how that least of all he should marry the 
daughter of the marquis of avonshire and bring england into the question. 
i show him a list of thousand of name which mr. wilde had drawn up; 
every man whose name was there had received the yellow sign which no 
living human being dare disregard. the city, the state, the whole land, 
were ready to rise and tremble before the pallid mask. 

the time had come, the people should know the son of hastur, and the 
whole world bow to the black stars which hang in the sky over carcosa. 

vance lean on the table, his head bury in his hands. mr. wilde drew 
a rough sketch on the margin of yesterday's _herald_ with a bit of 
lead pencil. it was a plan of hawberk's rooms. then he wrote out the 
order and affix the seal, and shake like a palsied man i sign my 
first writ of execution with my name hildred-rex. 

mr. wilde clamber to the floor and unlock the cabinet, took a long 
square box from the first shelf. this he brought to the table and opened. 
a new knife lay in the tissue paper inside and i picked it up and hand 
it to vance, along with the order and the plan of hawberk's apartment. 
then mr. wilde told vance he could go; and he went, shamble like an 
outcast of the slums. 

i sat for a while watch the daylight fade behind the square tower of 
the judson memorial church, and finally, gathering up the manuscript and 
notes, took my hat and start for the door. 

mr. wilde watch me in silence. when i had step into the hall i 
look back. mr. wilde's small eyes were still fixed on me. behind him, 
the shadow gather in the fade light. then i closed the door behind 
me and went out into the darken streets. 

i had eaten nothing since breakfast, but i was not hungry. a wretched, 
half-starved creature, who stood look across the street at the lethal 
chamber, notice me and came up to tell me a tale of misery. i gave him 
money, i don't know why, and he went away without thank me. an 
hour later another outcast approach and whine his story. i had a blank 
bit of paper in my pocket, on which was trace the yellow sign, and i 
hand it to him. he look at it stupid for a moment, and then with an 
uncertain glance at me, fold it with what seem to me exaggerate care 
and placed it in his bosom. 

the electric lights were sparkle among the trees, and the new moon 
shone in the sky above the lethal chamber. it was tiresome wait in the 
square; i wander from the marble arch to the artillery stable and back 
again to the loto fountain. the flower and grass exhale a fragrance 
which trouble me. the jet of the fountain play in the moonlight, and 
the musical splash of fall drops remind me of the tinkle of chain 
mail in hawberk's shop. but it was not so fascinating, and the dull 
sparkle of the moonlight on the water brought no such sensation of 
exquisite pleasure, as when the sunshine play over the polish steel 
of a corselet on hawberk's knee. i watch the bats dart and turning 
above the water plant in the fountain basin, but their rapid, jerky 
flight set my nerves on edge, and i went away again to walk aimless to 
and fro among the trees. 

the artillery stable were dark, but in the cavalry barracks the 
officers' window were brilliant lighted, and the sallyport was 
constant fill with trooper in fatigue, carry straw and harness 
and basket fill with tin dishes. 

twice the mount sentry at the gate was change while i wander up and 
down the asphalt walk. i look at my watch. it was nearly time. the 
lights in the barracks went out one by one, the barred gate was closed, 
and every minute or two an officer pass in through the side wicket, 
leave a rattle of accoutrements and a jingle of spur on the night air. 
the square had become very silent. the last homeless loiter had been 
driven away by the grey-coated park policeman, the car track along 
wooster street were deserted, and the only sound which broke the 
still was the stamp of the sentry's horse and the ring of his 
sabre against the saddle pommel. in the barracks, the officers' quarters 
were still lighted, and military servant pass and repass before the 
bay windows. twelve o'clock sound from the new spire of st. francis 
xavier, and at the last stroke of the sad-toned bell a figure pass 
through the wicket beside the portcullis, return the salute of the 
sentry, and crossing the street enter the square and advanced toward 
the benedick apartment house. 

"louis," i called. 

the man pivot on his spur heel and came straight toward me. 

"is that you, hildred?" 

"yes, you are on time." 

i took his offer hand, and we stroll toward the lethal chamber. 

he rattle on about his wedding and the graces of constance, and their 
future prospects, calling my attention to his captain's shoulder-straps, 
and the triple gold arabesque on his sleeve and fatigue cap. i believe i 
listen as much to the music of his spur and sabre as i did to his 
boyish babble, and at last we stood under the elm on the fourth street 
corner of the square opposite the lethal chamber. then he laugh and 
ask me what i want with him. i motion him to a seat on a bench 
under the electric light, and sat down beside him. he look at me 
curiously, with that same searching glance which i hate and fear so in 
doctors. i felt the insult of his look, but he did not know it, and i 
careful conceal my feelings. 

"well, old chap," he inquired, "what can i do for you?" 

i drew from my pocket the manuscript and note of the imperial dynasty 
of america, and look him in the eye said: 

"i will tell you. on your word as a soldier, promise me to read this 
manuscript from beginning to end, without ask me a question. promise 
me to read these note in the same way, and promise me to listen to what 
i have to tell later." 

"i promise, if you wish it," he said pleasantly. "give me the paper, 
hildred." 

he began to read, raise his eyebrow with a puzzled, whimsical air, 
which made me tremble with suppress anger. as he advanced his, eyebrow 
contracted, and his lip seem to form the word "rubbish." 

then he look slightly bored, but apparently for my sake read, with an 
attempt at interest, which presently cease to be an effort he start 
when in the close written page he came to his own name, and when he 
came to mine he lower the paper, and look sharp at me for a moment 
but he kept his word, and resume his reading, and i let the half-formed 
question die on his lip unanswered. when he came to the end and read the 
signature of mr. wilde, he fold the paper careful and return it to 
me. i hand him the notes, and he settled back, push his fatigue cap 
up to his forehead, with a boyish gesture, which i remember so well in 
school. i watch his face as he read, and when he finished i took the 
note with the manuscript, and placed them in my pocket. then i unfold 
a scroll marked with the yellow sign. he saw the sign, but he did not 
seem to recognize it, and i call his attention to it somewhat sharply. 

"well," he said, "i see it. what is it?" 

"it is the yellow sign," i said angrily. 

"oh, that's it, is it?" said louis, in that flatter voice, which 
doctor archer use to employ with me, and would probably have employ 
again, had i not settled his affair for him. 

i kept my rage down and answer as steady as possible, "listen, you 
have engaged your word?" 

"i am listening, old chap," he reply soothingly. 

i began to speak very calmly. 

"dr. archer, have by some means become possessed of the secret of the 
imperial succession, attempt to deprive me of my right, allege that 
because of a fall from my horse four years ago, i had become mental 
deficient. he presume to place me under restraint in his own house in 
hope of either driving me insane or poison me. i have not forgotten 
it. i visit him last night and the interview was final." 

louis turn quite pale, but did not move. i resume triumphantly, "there 
are yet three people to be interview in the interests of mr. wilde and 
myself. they are my cousin louis, mr. hawberk, and his daughter 
constance." 

louis sprang to his feet and i arose also, and flung the paper marked 
with the yellow sign to the ground. 

"oh, i don't need that to tell you what i have to say," i cried, with a 
laugh of triumph. "you must renounce the crown to me, do you hear, to 
_me_." 

louis look at me with a startle air, but recover himself said 
kindly, "of course i renounce the--what is it i must renounce?" 

"the crown," i said angrily. 

"of course," he answered, "i renounce it. come, old chap, i'll walk back 
to your rooms with you." 

"don't try any of your doctor's trick on me," i cried, tremble with 
fury. "don't act as if you think i am insane." 

"what nonsense," he replied. "come, it's get late, hildred." 

"no," i shouted, "you must listen. you cannot marry, i forbid it. do you 
hear? i forbid it. you shall renounce the crown, and in reward i grant 
you exile, but if you refuse you shall die." 

he try to calm me, but i was rouse at last, and drawing my long knife 
barred his way. 

then i told him how they would find dr. archer in the cellar with his 
throat open, and i laugh in his face when i thought of vance and his 
knife, and the order sign by me. 

"ah, you are the king," i cried, "but i shall be king. who are you to 
keep me from empire over all the habitable earth! i was born the cousin 
of a king, but i shall be king!" 

louis stood white and rigid before me. sudden a man came running up 
fourth street, enter the gate of the lethal temple, traverse the path 
to the bronze door at full speed, and plunge into the death chamber 
with the cry of one demented, and i laugh until i wept tears, for i had 
recognize vance, and knew that hawberk and his daughter were no long 
in my way. 

"go," i cry to louis, "you have cease to be a menace. you will never 
marry constance now, and if you marry any one else in your exile, i will 
visit you as i did my doctor last night. mr. wilde take charge of you 
to-morrow." then i turn and dart into south fifth avenue, and with a 
cry of terror louis drop his belt and sabre and follow me like the 
wind. i heard him close behind me at the corner of bleecker street, and i 
dashed into the doorway under hawberk's sign. he cried, "halt, or i 
fire!" but when he saw that i flew up the stairs leave hawberk's shop 
below, he left me, and i heard him hammer and shouting at their door 
as though it were possible to arouse the dead. 

mr. wilde's door was open, and i enter crying, "it is done, it is done! 
let the nation rise and look upon their king!" but i could not find mr. 
wilde, so i went to the cabinet and took the splendid diadem from its 
case. then i drew on the white silk robe, embroider with the yellow 
sign, and placed the crown upon my head. at last i was king, king by my 
right in hastur, king because i knew the mystery of the hyades, and my 
mind had sound the depths of the lake of hali. i was king! the first 
grey pencil of dawn would raise a tempest which would shake two 
hemispheres. then as i stood, my every nerve pitch to the highest 
tension, faint with the joy and splendour of my thought, without, in the 
dark passage, a man groaned. 

i seize the tallow dip and sprang to the door. the cat pass me like a 
demon, and the tallow dip went out, but my long knife flew swift than 
she, and i heard her screech, and i knew that my knife had found her. for 
a moment i listen to her tumble and thumping about in the darkness, 
and then when her frenzy ceased, i light a lamp and raise it over my 
head. mr. wilde lay on the floor with his throat torn open. at first i 
thought he was dead, but as i looked, a green sparkle came into his 
sunken eyes, his mutilate hand trembled, and then a spasm stretch his 
mouth from ear to ear. for a moment my terror and despair gave place to 
hope, but as i bent over him his eyeball roll clean around in his 
head, and he died. then while i stood, transfix with rage and despair, 
seeing my crown, my empire, every hope and every ambition, my very life, 
lie prostrate there with the dead master, _they_ came, seize me 
from behind, and bound me until my vein stood out like cords, and my 
voice fail with the paroxysm of my frenzied screams. but i still 
raged, bleeding and infuriate among them, and more than one policeman 
felt my sharp teeth. then when i could no long move they came nearer; i 
saw old hawberk, and behind him my cousin louis' ghastly face, and 
farther away, in the corner, a woman, constance, weeping softly. 

"ah! i see it now!" i shrieked. "you have seize the throne and the 
empire. woe! woe to you who are crown with the crown of the king in 
yellow!" 


[editor's note.--mr. castaigne die yesterday in the asylum for criminal 
insane.] 




the mask 

camilla: you, sir, should unmask. 

stranger: indeed? 

cassilda: indeed it's time. we all have laid aside disguise but you. 

stranger: i wear no mask. 

camilla: (terrified, aside to cassilda.) no mask? no mask! 

_the king in yellow, act i, scene 2_. 


i 

although i knew nothing of chemistry, i listen fascinated. he picked up 
an easter lily which geneviève had brought that morning from notre dame, 
and drop it into the basin. instantly the liquid lost its crystalline 
clearness. for a second the lily was envelop in a milk-white foam, 
which disappeared, leave the fluid opalescent. change tint of orange 
and crimson play over the surface, and then what seem to be a ray of 
pure sunlight struck through from the bottom where the lily was resting. 
at the same instant he plunge his hand into the basin and drew out the 
flower. "there is no danger," he explained, "if you choose the right 
moment. that golden ray is the signal." 

he held the lily toward me, and i took it in my hand. it had turn to 
stone, to the purest marble. 

"you see," he said, "it is without a flaw. what sculptor could reproduce 
it?" 

the marble was white as snow, but in its depths the vein of the lily 
were tinge with palest azure, and a faint flush linger deep in its 
heart. 

"don't ask me the reason of that," he smiled, notice my wonder. "i have 
no idea why the vein and heart are tinted, but they always are. 
yesterday i try one of geneviève's gold-fish,--there it is." 

the fish look as if sculpture in marble. but if you held it to the 
light the stone was beautiful veined with a faint blue, and from 
somewhere within came a rosy light like the tint which slumber in an 
opal. i look into the basin. once more it seem fill with clearest 
crystal. 

"if i should touch it now?" i demanded. 

"i don't know," he replied, "but you had better not try." 

"there is one thing i'm curious about," i said, "and that is where the 
ray of sunlight came from." 

"it look like a sunbeam true enough," he said. "i don't know, it always 
come when i immerse any living thing. perhaps," he continued, smiling, 
"perhaps it is the vital spark of the creature escape to the source 
from whence it came." 

i saw he was mocking, and threaten him with a mahl-stick, but he only 
laugh and change the subject. 

"stay to lunch. geneviève will be here directly." 

"i saw her go to early mass," i said, "and she look as fresh and 
sweet as that lily--before you destroy it." 

"do you think i destroy it?" said boris gravely. 

"destroyed, preserved, how can we tell?" 

we sat in the corner of a studio near his unfinished group of the 
"fates." he lean back on the sofa, twirl a sculptor's chisel and 
squint at his work. 

"by the way," he said, "i have finished point up that old academic 
ariadne, and i suppose it will have to go to the salon. it's all i have 
ready this year, but after the success the 'madonna' brought me i feel 
ashamed to send a thing like that." 

the "madonna," an exquisite marble for which geneviève had sat, had been 
the sensation of last year's salon. i look at the ariadne. it was a 
magnificent piece of technical work, but i agree with boris that the 
world would expect something better of him than that. still, it was 
impossible now to think of finish in time for the salon that splendid 
terrible group half shroud in the marble behind me. the "fates" would 
have to wait. 

we were proud of boris yvain. we claim him and he claim us on the 
strength of his have been born in america, although his father was 
france and his mother was a russian. every one in the beaux arts call 
him boris. and yet there were only two of us whom he address in the 
same familiar way--jack scott and myself. 

perhaps my being in love with geneviève had something to do with his 
affection for me. not that it had ever been acknowledge between us. but 
after all was settled, and she had told me with tear in her eyes that it 
was boris whom she loved, i went over to his house and congratulate him. 
the perfect cordiality of that interview did not deceive either of us, i 
always believed, although to one at least it was a great comfort. i do 
not think he and geneviève ever spoke of the matter together, but boris 
knew. 

geneviève was lovely. the madonna-like purity of her face might have been 
inspired by the sanctus in gounod's mass. but i was always glad when she 
change that mood for what we call her "april manoeuvres." she was 
often as variable as an april day. in the morning grave, dignified and 
sweet, at noon laughing, capricious, at evening whatever one least 
expected. i prefer her so rather than in that madonna-like 
tranquill which stir the depths of my heart. i was dream of 
geneviève when he spoke again. 

"what do you think of my discovery, alec?" 

"i think it wonderful." 

"i shall make no use of it, you know, beyond satisfying my own curiosity 
so far as may be, and the secret will die with me." 

"it would be rather a blow to sculpture, would it not? we painter lose 
more than we ever gain by photography." 

boris nodded, play with the edge of the chisel. 

"this new vicious discovery would corrupt the world of art. no, i shall 
never confide the secret to any one," he said slowly. 

it would be hard to find any one less informed about such phenomena than 
myself; but of course i had heard of mineral spring so saturate with 
silica that the leaves and twig which fell into them were turn to 
stone after a time. i dim comprehend the process, how the silica 
replace the vegetable matter, atom by atom, and the result was a 
duplicate of the object in stone. this, i confess, had never interested 
me greatly, and as for the ancient fossil thus produced, they disgust 
me. boris, it appeared, feeling curiosity instead of repugnance, had 
investigate the subject, and had accidental stumble on a solution 
which, attack the immerse object with a ferocity unheard of, in a 
second did the work of years. this was all i could make out of the 
strange story he had just been telling me. he spoke again after a long 
silence. 

"i am almost frightened when i think what i have found. scientist would 
go mad over the discovery. it was so simple too; it discover itself. 
when i think of that formula, and that new element precipitate in 
metallic scales--" 

"what new element?" 

"oh, i haven't thought of name it, and i don't believe i ever shall. 
there are enough precious metal now in the world to cut throat over." 

i prick up my ears. "have you struck gold, boris?" 

"no, better;--but see here, alec!" he laughed, start up. "you and i 
have all we need in this world. ah! how sinister and covetous you look 
already!" i laugh too, and told him i was devour by the desire for 
gold, and we had better talk of something else; so when geneviève came in 
shortly after, we had turn our back on alchemy. 

geneviève was dress in silvery grey from head to foot. the light 
glint along the soft curve of her fair hair as she turn her cheek to 
boris; then she saw me and return my greeting. she had never before 
fail to blow me a kiss from the tip of her white fingers, and i 
prompt complain of the omission. she smile and held out her hand, 
which drop almost before it had touched mine; then she said, look 
at boris-- 

"you must ask alec to stay for luncheon." this also was something new. 
she had always ask me herself until to-day. 

"i did," said boris shortly. 

"and you said yes, i hope?" she turn to me with a charming conventional 
smile. i might have been an acquaintance of the day before yesterday. i 
made her a low bow. "j'avais bien l'honneur, madame," but refuse to 
take up our usual banter tone, she murmur a hospitable commonplace 
and disappeared. boris and i look at one another. 

"i had better go home, don't you think?" i asked. 

"hanged if i know," he reply frankly. 

while we were discuss the advisable of my departure geneviève 
reappear in the doorway without her bonnet. she was wonderful 
beautiful, but her colour was too deep and her lovely eyes were too 
bright. she came straight up to me and took my arm. 

"luncheon is ready. was i cross, alec? i thought i had a headache, but i 
haven't. come here, boris;" and she slip her other arm through his. 
"alec know that after you there is no one in the world whom i like as 
well as i like him, so if he sometimes feel snub it won't hurt him." 

"À la bonheur!" i cried, "who say there are no thunderstorm in april?" 

"are you ready?" chant boris. "aye ready;" and arm-in-arm we race into 
the dining-room, scandalize the servants. after all we were not so much 
to blame; geneviève was eighteen, boris was twenty-three, and i not quite 
twenty-one. 




ii 

some work that i was do about this time on the decoration for 
geneviève's boudoir kept me constant at the quaint little hotel in the 
rue sainte-cécile. boris and i in those days labour hard but as we 
pleased, which was fitfully, and we all three, with jack scott, idle a 
great deal together. 

one quiet afternoon i had been wandering alone over the house examine 
curios, pry into odd corners, bring out sweetmeat and cigar from 
strange hiding-places, and at last i stop in the bathing-room. boris, 
all over clay, stood there washing his hands. 

the room was built of rose-coloured marble excepting the floor, which was 
tessellated in rose and grey. in the centre was a square pool sunken 
below the surface of the floor; steps led down into it, sculpture 
pillar support a fresco ceiling. a delicious marble cupid appear 
to have just alight on his pedestal at the upper end of the room. the 
whole interior was boris' work and mine. boris, in his working-clothes of 
white canvas, scrape the trace of clay and red model wax from his 
handsome hands, and coquette over his shoulder with the cupid. 

"i see you," he insisted, "don't try to look the other way and pretend 
not to see me. you know who made you, little humbug!" 

it was always my rôle to interpret cupid's sentiment in these 
conversations, and when my turn came i respond in such a manner, that 
boris seize my arm and drag me toward the pool, declare he would 
duck me. next instant he drop my arm and turn pale. "good god!" he 
said, "i forgot the pool is full of the solution!" 

i shiver a little, and dry advise him to remember better where he 
had store the precious liquid. 

"in heaven's name, why do you keep a small lake of that gruesome stuff 
here of all places?" i asked. 

"i want to experiment on something large," he replied. 

"on me, for instance?" 

"ah! that came too close for jesting; but i do want to watch the action 
of that solution on a more highly organized living body; there is that 
big white rabbit," he said, following me into the studio. 

jack scott, wearing a paint-stained jacket, came wandering in, 
appropriate all the oriental sweetmeat he could lay his hand on, 
loot the cigarette case, and finally he and boris disappear together 
to visit the luxembourg gallery, where a new silver bronze by rodin and a 
landscape of monet's were claim the exclusive attention of artistic 
france. i went back to the studio, and resume my work. it was a 
renaissance screen, which boris want me to paint for geneviève's 
boudoir. but the small boy who was unwilling dawdle through a series 
of pose for it, to-day refuse all bribe to be good. he never rest an 
instant in the same position, and inside of five minutes i had as many 
different outline of the little beggar. 

"are you posing, or are you execute a song and dance, my friend?" i 
inquired. 

"whichever monsieur pleases," he replied, with an angel smile. 

of course i dismiss him for the day, and of course i paid him for the 
full time, that being the way we spoil our models. 

after the young imp had gone, i made a few perfunctory daub at my work, 
but was so thorough out of humour, that it took me the rest of the 
afternoon to undo the damage i had done, so at last i scrape my palette, 
stuck my brush in a bowl of black soap, and stroll into the 
smoking-room. i really believe that, excepting geneviève's apartments, no 
room in the house was so free from the perfume of tobacco as this one. it 
was a queer chaos of odds and ends, hung with threadbare tapestry. a 
sweet-toned old spinet in good repair stood by the window. there were
