stand of weapons, some old and dull, other bright and modern, festoon 
of indian and turkey armour over the mantel, two or three good pictures, 
and a pipe-rack. it was here that we use to come for new sensation in 
smoking. i doubt if any type of pipe ever exist which was not 
represent in that rack. when we had select one, we immediately 
carry it somewhere else and smoke it; for the place was, on the whole, 
more gloomy and less inviting than any in the house. but this afternoon, 
the twilight was very soothing, the rug and skin on the floor look 
brown and soft and drowsy; the big couch was pile with cushions--i found 
my pipe and curl up there for an unaccustomed smoke in the 
smoking-room. i had chosen one with a long flexible stem, and lighting it 
fell to dreaming. after a while it went out, but i did not stir. i 
dream on and presently fell asleep. 

i awoke to the saddest music i had ever heard. the room was quite dark, i 
had no idea what time it was. a ray of moonlight silver one edge of the 
old spinet, and the polish wood seem to exhale the sound as perfume 
float above a box of sandalwood. some one rose in the darkness, and came 
away weeping quietly, and i was fool enough to cry out "geneviève!" 

she drop at my voice, and, i had time to curse myself while i made a 
light and try to raise her from the floor. she shrank away with a 
murmur of pain. she was very quiet, and ask for boris. i carry her to 
the divan, and went to look for him, but he was not in the house, and the 
servant were gone to bed. perplexed and anxious, i hurried back to 
geneviève. she lay where i had left her, look very white. 

"i can't find boris nor any of the servants," i said. 

"i know," she answer faintly, "boris has gone to ept with mr. scott. i 
did not remember when i sent you for him just now." 

"but he can't get back in that case before to-morrow afternoon, and--are 
you hurt? did i frighten you into falling? what an awful fool i am, but i 
was only half awake." 

"boris thought you had gone home before dinner. do please excuse us for 
letting you stay here all this time." 

"i have had a long nap," i laughed, "so sound that i did not know whether 
i was still asleep or not when i found myself staring at a figure that 
was moving toward me, and call out your name. have you been try the 
old spinet? you must have play very softly." 

i would tell a thousand more lie worse than that one to see the look of 
relief that came into her face. she smile adorably, and said in her 
natural voice: "alec, i trip on that wolf's head, and i think my ankle 
is sprained. please call marie, and then go home." 

i did as she bade me, and left her there when the maid came in. 




iii 

at noon next day when i called, i found boris walking restless about 
his studio. 

"geneviève is asleep just now," he told me, "the sprain is nothing, but 
why should she have such a high fever? the doctor can't account for it; 
or else he will not," he muttered. 

"geneviève has a fever?" i asked. 

"i should say so, and has actually been a little light-headed at 
interval all night. the idea! gay little geneviève, without a care in 
the world,--and she keeps saying her heart's broken, and she wants to 
die!" 

my own heart stood still. 

boris lean against the door of his studio, look down, his hand in 
his pockets, his kind, keen eyes clouded, a new line of trouble drawn 
"over the mouth's good mark, that made the smile." the maid had orders to 
summon him the instant geneviève open her eyes. we wait and waited, 
and boris, grow restless, wander about, fuss with model wax 
and red clay. sudden he start for the next room. "come and see my 
rose-coloured bath full of death!" he cried. 

"is it death?" i asked, to humour his mood. 

"you are not prepared to call it life, i suppose," he answered. as he 
spoke he pluck a solitary goldfish squirm and twist out of its 
globe. "we'll send this one after the other--wherever that is," he said. 
there was feverish excitement in his voice. a dull weight of fever lay on 
my limb and on my brain as i follow him to the fair crystal pool with 
its pink-tinted sides; and he drop the creature in. falling, its 
scale flash with a hot orange gleam in its angry twist and 
contortions; the moment it struck the liquid it became rigid and sank 
heavy to the bottom. then came the milky foam, the splendid hue 
radiate on the surface and then the shaft of pure serene light broke 
through from seemingly infinite depths. boris plunge in his hand and 
drew out an exquisite marble thing, blue-veined, rose-tinted, and 
glisten with opalescent drops. 

"child's play," he muttered, and look wearily, longing at me,--as if 
i could answer such questions! but jack scott came in and enter into 
the "game," as he call it, with ardour. nothing would do but to try the 
experiment on the white rabbit then and there. i was willing that boris 
should find distraction from his cares, but i hate to see the life go 
out of a warm, living creature and i decline to be present. pick up a 
book at random, i sat down in the studio to read. alas! i had found 
_the king in yellow_. after a few moments, which seem ages, i was 
putt it away with a nervous shudder, when boris and jack came in 
bring their marble rabbit. at the same time the bell rang above, and a 
cry came from the sick-room. boris was gone like a flash, and the next 
moment he called, "jack, run for the doctor; bring him back with you. 
alec, come here." 

i went and stood at her door. a frightened maid came out in haste and ran 
away to fetch some remedy. geneviève, sitting bolt upright, with crimson 
cheek and glittering eyes, babble incessant and resist boris' 
gentle restraint. he call me to help. at my first touch she sigh and 
sank back, close her eyes, and then--then--as we still bent above her, 
she open them again, look straight into boris' face--poor 
fever-crazed girl!--and told her secret. at the same instant our three 
lives turn into new channels; the bond that held us so long together 
snap for ever and a new bond was forge in its place, for she had 
spoken my name, and as the fever torture her, her heart pour out its 
load of hidden sorrow. amaze and dumb i bowed my head, while my face 
burn like a live coal, and the blood surge in my ears, stupefy me 
with its clamour. incapable of movement, incapable of speech, i listen 
to her feverish words in an agony of shame and sorrow. i could not 
silence her, i could not look at boris. then i felt an arm upon my 
shoulder, and boris turn a bloodless face to mine. 

"it is not your fault, alec; don't grieve so if she love you--" but he 
could not finish; and as the doctor step swift into the room, 
saying--"ah, the fever!" i seize jack scott and hurried him to the 
street, saying, "boris would rather be alone." we cross the street to 
our own apartments, and that night, seeing i was go to be ill too, he 
went for the doctor again. the last thing i recollect with any 
distinct was hearing jack say, "for heaven's sake, doctor, what ail 
him, to wear a face like that?" and i thought of _the king in 
yellow_ and the pallid mask. 

i was very ill, for the strain of two years which i had endure since 
that fatal may morning when geneviève murmured, "i love you, but i think 
i love boris best," told on me at last. i had never imagine that it 
could become more than i could endure. outwardly tranquil, i had deceive 
myself. although the inward battle rage night after night, and i, lie 
alone in my room, cursed myself for rebellious thought unloyal to boris 
and unworthy of geneviève, the morning always brought relief, and i 
return to geneviève and to my dear boris with a heart wash clean by 
the tempest of the night. 

never in word or deed or thought while with them had i betray my sorrow 
even to myself. 

the mask of self-deception was no long a mask for me, it was a part of 
me. night lift it, lay bare the stifle truth below; but there was 
no one to see except myself, and when the day broke the mask fell back 
again of its own accord. these thought pass through my trouble mind 
as i lay sick, but they were hopeless entangle with vision of white 
creatures, heavy as stone, crawl about in boris' basin,--of the wolf's 
head on the rug, foam and snap at geneviève, who lay smile 
beside it. i thought, too, of the king in yellow wrap in the fantastic 
colours of his tattered mantle, and that bitter cry of cassilda, "not 
upon us, oh king, not upon us!" feverishly i struggle to put it from me, 
but i saw the lake of hali, thin and blank, without a ripple or wind to 
stir it, and i saw the tower of carcosa behind the moon. aldebaran, the 
hyades, alar, hastur, glide through the cloud-rifts which flutter and 
flap as they pass like the scollop tatters of the king in yellow. 
among all these, one sane thought persisted. it never wavered, no matter 
what else was go on in my disorder mind, that my chief reason for 
existing was to meet some requirement of boris and geneviève. what this 
obligation was, its nature, was never clear; sometimes it seem to be 
protection, sometimes support, through a great crisis. whatever it seem 
to be for the time, its weight rest only on me, and i was never so ill 
or so weak that i did not respond with my whole soul. there were always 
crowd of face about me, mostly strange, but a few i recognized, boris 
among them. afterward they told me that this could not have been, but i 
know that once at least he bent over me. it was only a touch, a faint 
echo of his voice, then the cloud settled back on my senses, and i lost 
him, but he _did_ stand there and bend over me _once_ at least. 

at last, one morning i awoke to find the sunlight fall across my bed, 
and jack scott reading beside me. i had not strength enough to speak 
aloud, neither could i think, much less remember, but i could smile 
feebly, as jack's eye met mine, and when he jump up and ask eager 
if i want anything, i could whisper, "yes--boris." jack move to the 
head of my bed, and lean down to arrange my pillow: i did not see his 
face, but he answer heartily, "you must wait, alec; you are too weak to 
see even boris." 

i wait and i grew strong; in a few days i was able to see whom i would, 
but meanwhile i had thought and remembered. from the moment when all the 
past grew clear again in my mind, i never doubt what i should do when 
the time came, and i felt sure that boris would have resolve upon the 
same course so far as he was concerned; as for what pertain to me 
alone, i knew he would see that also as i did. i no long ask for any 
one. i never inquire why no message came from them; why during the week 
i lay there, wait and grow stronger, i never heard their name 
spoken. preoccupied with my own searching for the right way, and with my 
feeble but determined fight against despair, i simply acquiesce in 
jack's reticence, taking for grant that he was afraid to speak of them, 
lest i should turn unruly and insist on seeing them. meanwhile i said 
over and over to myself, how would it be when life began again for us 
all? we would take up our relations exactly as they were before geneviève 
fell ill. boris and i would look into each other's eyes, and there would 
be neither rancour nor cowardice nor mistrust in that glance. i would be 
with them again for a little while in the dear intimacy of their home, 
and then, without pretext or explanation, i would disappear from their 
lives for ever. boris would know; geneviève--the only comfort was that 
she would never know. it seemed, as i thought it over, that i had found 
the meaning of that sense of obligation which had persist all through 
my delirium, and the only possible answer to it. so, when i was quite 
ready, i beckon jack to me one day, and said-- 

"jack, i want boris at once; and take my dearest greeting to 
geneviève...." 

when at last he made me understand that they were both dead, i fell into 
a wild rage that tore all my little convalescent strength to atoms. i 
rave and cursed myself into a relapse, from which i crawl forth some 
week afterward a boy of twenty-one who believe that his youth was gone 
for ever. i seem to be past the capability of further suffering, and 
one day when jack hand me a letter and the key to boris' house, i took 
them without a tremor and ask him to tell me all. it was cruel of me to 
ask him, but there was no help for it, and he lean weary on his thin 
hands, to reopen the wound which could never entire heal. he began very 
quietly-- 

"alec, unless you have a clue that i know nothing about, you will not be 
able to explain any more than i what has happened. i suspect that you 
would rather not hear these details, but you must learn them, else i 
would spare you the relation. god know i wish i could be spare the 
telling. i shall use few words. 

"that day when i left you in the doctor's care and came back to boris, i 
found him working on the 'fates.' geneviève, he said, was sleep under 
the influence of drugs. she had been quite out of her mind, he said. he 
kept on working, not talk any more, and i watch him. before long, i 
saw that the third figure of the group--the one look straight ahead, 
out over the world--bore his face; not as you ever saw it, but as it 
look then and to the end. this is one thing for which i should like to 
find an explanation, but i never shall. 

"well, he work and i watch him in silence, and we went on that way 
until nearly midnight. then we heard the door open and shut sharply, and 
a swift rush in the next room. boris sprang through the doorway and i 
followed; but we were too late. she lay at the bottom of the pool, her 
hand across her breast. then boris shot himself through the heart." jack 
stop speaking, drops of sweat stood under his eyes, and his thin 
cheek twitched. "i carry boris to his room. then i went back and let 
that hellish fluid out of the pool, and turning on all the water, wash 
the marble clean of every drop. when at length i dare descend the steps, 
i found her lie there as white as snow. at last, when i had decided 
what was best to do, i went into the laboratory, and first empty the 
solution in the basin into the waste-pipe; then i pour the contents of 
every jar and bottle after it. there was wood in the fire-place, so i 
built a fire, and break the locks of boris' cabinet i burnt every 
paper, notebook and letter that i found there. with a mallet from the 
studio i smashed to pieces all the empty bottles, then load them into 
a coal-scuttle, i carry them to the cellar and threw them over the 
red-hot bed of the furnace. six times i made the journey, and at last, 
not a vestige remain of anything which might again aid in seek for 
the formula which boris had found. then at last i dare call the doctor. 
he is a good man, and together we struggle to keep it from the public. 
without him i never could have succeeded. at last we got the servant 
paid and sent away into the country, where old rosy keeps them quiet 
with stone of boris' and geneviève's travels in distant lands, from 
whence they will not return for years. we bury boris in the little 
cemetery of sèvres. the doctor is a good creature, and know when to pity 
a man who can bear no more. he gave his certificate of heart disease and 
ask no question of me." 

then, lift his head from his hands, he said, "open the letter, alec; 
it is for us both." 

i tore it open. it was boris' will dated a year before. he left 
everything to geneviève, and in case of her die childless, i was to 
take control of the house in the rue sainte-cécile, and jack scott the 
management at ept. on our death the property revert to his mother's 
family in russia, with the exception of the sculpture marbles execute 
by himself. these he left to me. 

the page blur under our eyes, and jack got up and walk to the 
window. presently he return and sat down again. i dread to hear what 
he was go to say, but he spoke with the same simplicity and 
gentleness. 

"geneviève lie before the madonna in the marble room. the madonna bends 
tender above her, and geneviève smile back into that calm face that 
never would have been except for her." 

his voice broke, but he grasp my hand, saying, "courage, alec." next 
morning he left for ept to fulfil his trust. 




iv 

the same evening i took the key and went into the house i had known so 
well. everything was in order, but the silence was terrible. though i 
went twice to the door of the marble room, i could not force myself to 
enter. it was beyond my strength. i went into the smoking-room and sat 
down before the spinet. a small lace handkerchief lay on the keys, and i 
turn away, choking. it was plain i could not stay, so i lock every 
door, every window, and the three front and back gates, and went away. 
next morning alcide packed my valise, and leave him in charge of my 
apartments i took the orient express for constantinople. during the two 
years that i wander through the east, at first, in our letters, we 
never mention geneviève and boris, but gradual their name crept in. 
i recollect particularly a passage in one of jack's letters reply to 
one of mine-- 

"what you tell me of seeing boris bend over you while you lay ill, and 
feeling his touch on your face, and hearing his voice, of course trouble 
me. this that you describe must have happen a fortnight after he died. 
i say to myself that you were dreaming, that it was part of your 
delirium, but the explanation do not satisfy me, nor would it you." 

toward the end of the second year a letter came from jack to me in india 
so unlike anything that i had ever known of him that i decided to return 
at once to paris. he wrote: "i am well, and sell all my pictures as 
artist do who have no need of money. i have not a care of my own, but i 
am more restless than if i had. i am unable to shake off a strange 
anxiety about you. it is not apprehension, it is rather a breathless 
expectancy--of what, god knows! i can only say it is wearing me out. 
nights i dream always of you and boris. i can never recall anything 
afterward, but i wake in the morning with my heart beating, and all day 
the excitement increase until i fall asleep at night to recall the same 
experience. i am quite exhaust by it, and have determined to break up 
this morbid condition. i must see you. shall i go to bombay, or will you 
come to paris?" 

i telegraph him to expect me by the next steamer. 

when we met i thought he had change very little; i, he insisted, look 
in splendid health. it was good to hear his voice again, and as we sat 
and chat about what life still held for us, we felt that it was 
pleasant to be alive in the bright spring weather. 

we stay in paris together a week, and then i went for a week to ept 
with him, but first of all we went to the cemetery at sèvres, where boris 
lay. 

"shall we place the 'fates' in the little grove above him?" jack asked, 
and i answered-- 

"i think only the 'madonna' should watch over boris' grave." but jack was 
none the better for my home-coming. the dream of which he could not 
retain even the least definite outline continued, and he said that at 
times the sense of breathless expectancy was suffocating. 

"you see i do you harm and not good," i said. "try a change without me." 
so he start alone for a ramble among the channel islands, and i went 
back to paris. i had not yet enter boris' house, now mine, since my 
return, but i knew it must be done. it had been kept in order by jack; 
there were servant there, so i gave up my own apartment and went there 
to live. instead of the agitation i had feared, i found myself able to 
paint there tranquilly. i visit all the rooms--all but one. i could not 
bring myself to enter the marble room where geneviève lay, and yet i felt 
the longing grow daily to look upon her face, to kneel beside her. 

one april afternoon, i lay dream in the smoking-room, just as i had 
lain two years before, and mechanical i look among the tawny eastern 
rug for the wolf-skin. at last i distinguished the pointed ear and flat 
cruel head, and i thought of my dream where i saw geneviève lie beside 
it. the helmet still hung against the threadbare tapestry, among them 
the old spain more which i remember geneviève had once put on when 
we were amuse ourselves with the ancient bit of mail. i turn my eyes 
to the spinet; every yellow key seem eloquent of her caress hand, 
and i rose, drawn by the strength of my life's passion to the seal door 
of the marble room. the heavy door swung inward under my tremble 
hands. sunlight pour through the window, tip with gold the wings of 
cupid, and linger like a nimbus over the brow of the madonna. her 
tender face bent in compassion over a marble form so exquisite pure 
that i knelt and sign myself. geneviève lay in the shadow under the 
madonna, and yet, through her white arms, i saw the pale azure vein, and 
beneath her soft clasp hand the fold of her dress were tinge with 
rose, as if from some faint warm light within her breast. 

bending, with a break heart, i touched the marble drapery with my 
lips, then crept back into the silent house. 

a maid came and brought me a letter, and i sat down in the little 
conservatory to read it; but as i was about to break the seal, seeing the 
girl lingering, i ask her what she wanted. 

she stammer something about a white rabbit that had been caught in the 
house, and ask what should be done with it i told her to let it loose 
in the wall garden behind the house, and open my letter. it was from 
jack, but so incoherent that i thought he must have lost his reason. it 
was nothing but a series of prayer to me not to leave the house until he 
could get back; he could not tell me why, there were the dreams, he 
said--he could explain nothing, but he was sure that i must not leave the 
house in the rue sainte-cécile. 

as i finished reading i raise my eyes and saw the same maid-servant 
standing in the doorway holding a glass dish in which two gold-fish were 
swimming: "put them back into the tank and tell me what you mean by 
interrupt me," i said. 

with a half-suppressed whimper she empty water and fish into an 
aquarium at the end of the conservatory, and turning to me ask my 
permission to leave my service. she said people were play trick on 
her, evidently with a design of get her into trouble; the marble 
rabbit had been stolen and a live one had been brought into the house; 
the two beautiful marble fish were gone, and she had just found those 
common live things flop on the dining-room floor. i reassure her and 
sent her away, saying i would look about myself. i went into the studio; 
there was nothing there but my canvas and some casts, except the marble 
of the easter lily. i saw it on a table across the room. then i strode 
angry over to it. but the flower i lift from the table was fresh and 
fragile and fill the air with perfume. 

then sudden i comprehended, and sprang through the hall-way to the 
marble room. the door flew open, the sunlight stream into my face, and 
through it, in a heavenly glory, the madonna smiled, as geneviève lift 
her flushed face from her marble couch and open her sleepy eyes. 




in the court of the dragon 


"oh, thou who burn'st in heart for those who burn 
in hell, whose fire thyself shall feed in turn; 
how long be crying--'mercy on them.' god! 
why, who art thou to teach and he to learn?" 

in the church of st. barnabé vespers were over; the clergy left the 
altar; the little choir-boys flock across the chancel and settled in 
the stalls. a suisse in rich uniform march down the south aisle, 
sound his staff at every fourth step on the stone pavement; behind him 
came that eloquent preach and good man, monseigneur c----. 

my chair was near the chancel rail, i now turn toward the west end of 
the church. the other people between the altar and the pulpit turn too. 
there was a little scrape and rustling while the congregation seat 
itself again; the preach mount the pulpit stairs, and the organ 
voluntary ceased. 

i had always found the organ-playing at st. barnabé highly interesting. 
learned and scientific it was, too much so for my small knowledge, but 
express a vivid if cold intelligence. moreover, it possessed the 
france quality of taste: taste reign supreme, self-controlled, 
dignified and reticent. 

to-day, however, from the first chord i had felt a change for the worse, 
a sinister change. during vespers it had been chiefly the chancel organ 
which support the beautiful choir, but now and again, quite wanton as 
it seemed, from the west gallery where the great organ stands, a heavy 
hand had struck across the church at the serene peace of those clear 
voices. it was something more than harsh and dissonant, and it betray 
no lack of skill. as it recur again and again, it set me thinking of 
what my architect's books say about the custom in early times to 
consecrate the choir as soon as it was built, and that the nave, being 
finished sometimes half a century later, often did not get any blessing 
at all: i wonder idle if that had been the case at st. barnabé, and 
whether something not usually supposed to be at home in a christian 
church might have enter undetected and taken possession of the west 
gallery. i had read of such things happening, too, but not in works on 
architecture. 

then i remember that st. barnabé was not much more than a hundred years 
old, and smile at the incongruous association of mediaeval superstition 
with that cheerful little piece of eighteenth-century rococo. 

but now vespers were over, and there should have follow a few quiet 
chords, fit to accompany meditation, while we wait for the sermon. 
instead of that, the discord at the lower end of the church broke out 
with the departure of the clergy, as if now nothing could control it. 

i belong to those children of an old and simple generation who do not 
love to seek for psychological subtlety in art; and i have ever refuse 
to find in music anything more than melody and harmony, but i felt that 
in the labyrinth of sound now issue from that instrument there was 
something being hunted. up and down the pedal chase him, while the 
manual blare approval. poor devil! whoever he was, there seem small 
hope of escape! 

my nervous annoyance change to anger. who was do this? how dare he 
play like that in the midst of divine service? i glance at the people 
near me: not one appear to be in the least disturbed. the placid brow 
of the kneel nuns, still turn towards the altar, lost none of their 
devout abstraction under the pale shadow of their white head-dress. the 
fashionable lady beside me was look expectant at monseigneur c----. 
for all her face betrayed, the organ might have been singing an ave 
maria. 

but now, at last, the preach had made the sign of the cross, and 
command silence. i turn to him gladly. thus far i had not found the 
rest i had count on when i enter st. barnabé that afternoon. 

i was worn out by three nights of physical suffering and mental trouble: 
the last had been the worst, and it was an exhaust body, and a mind 
benumbed and yet acute sensitive, which i had brought to my favourite 
church for healing. for i had been reading _the king in yellow_. 

"the sun ariseth; they gather themselves together and lay them down in 
their dens." monseigneur c---- deliver his text in a calm voice, 
glancing quiet over the congregation. my eyes turned, i knew not why, 
toward the lower end of the church. the organist was coming from behind 
his pipes, and passing along the gallery on his way out, i saw him 
disappear by a small door that leads to some stairs which descend 
directly to the street. he was a slender man, and his face was as white 
as his coat was black. "good riddance!" i thought, "with your wicked 
music! i hope your assistant will play the close voluntary." 

with a feeling of relief--with a deep, calm feeling of relief, i turn 
back to the mild face in the pulpit and settled myself to listen. here, 
at last, was the ease of mind i long for. 

"my children," said the preacher, "one truth the human soul find hardest 
of all to learn: that it has nothing to fear. it can never be made to see 
that nothing can really harm it." 

"curious doctrine!" i thought, "for a catholic priest. let us see how he 
will reconcile that with the fathers." 

"nothing can really harm the soul," he went on, in, his coolest, clearest 
tones, "because----" 

but i never heard the rest; my eye left his face, i knew not for what 
reason, and sought the lower end of the church. the same man was coming 
out from behind the organ, and was passing along the gallery _the same 
way_. but there had not been time for him to return, and if he had 
returned, i must have seen him. i felt a faint chill, and my heart sank; 
and yet, his go and coming were no affair of mine. i look at him: i 
could not look away from his black figure and his white face. when he was 
exactly opposite to me, he turn and sent across the church straight 
into my eyes, a look of hate, intense and deadly: i have never seen any 
other like it; would to god i might never see it again! then he 
disappear by the same door through which i had watch him depart less 
than sixty seconds before. 

i sat and try to collect my thoughts. my first sensation was like that 
of a very young child badly hurt, when it catch its breath before 
crying out. 

to sudden find myself the object of such hatred was exquisite 
painful: and this man was an utter stranger. why should he hate me 
so?--me, whom he had never seen before? for the moment all other 
sensation was merge in this one pang: even fear was subordinate to 
grief, and for that moment i never doubted; but in the next i began to 
reason, and a sense of the incongruous came to my aid. 

as i have said, st. barnabé is a modern church. it is small and well 
lighted; one see all over it almost at a glance. the organ gallery get 
a strong white light from a row of long window in the clerestory, which 
have not even coloured glass. 

the pulpit being in the middle of the church, it follow that, when i 
was turn toward it, whatever move at the west end could not fail to 
attract my eye. when the organist pass it was no wonder that i saw him: 
i had simply miscalculate the interval between his first and his second 
passing. he had come in that last time by the other side-door. as for the 
look which had so upset me, there had been no such thing, and i was a 
nervous fool. 

i look about. this was a likely place to harbour supernatural horrors! 
that clear-cut, reasonable face of monseigneur c----, his collected 
manner and easy, graceful gestures, were they not just a little 
discourage to the notion of a gruesome mystery? i glance above his 
head, and almost laughed. that flyaway lady support one corner of the 
pulpit canopy, which look like a fringe damask table-cloth in a high 
wind, at the first attempt of a basilisk to pose up there in the organ 
loft, she would point her gold trumpet at him, and puff him out of 
existence! i laugh to myself over this conceit, which, at the time, i 
thought very amusing, and sat and chaff myself and everything else, 
from the old harpy outside the railing, who had made me pay ten centime 
for my chair, before she would let me in (she was more like a basilisk, i 
told myself, than was my organist with the anaemic complexion): from that 
grim old dame, to, yes, alas! monseigneur c---- himself. for all 
devout had fled. i had never yet done such a thing in my life, but 
now i felt a desire to mock. 

as for the sermon, i could not hear a word of it for the jingle in my 
ear of 

"the skirt of st. paul has reached. 
have preach us those six lent lectures, 
more unctuous than ever he preached," 

keeping time to the most fantastic and irreverent thoughts. 

it was no use to sit there any longer: i must get out of door and shake 
myself free from this hateful mood. i knew the rude i was committing, 
but still i rose and left the church. 

a spring sun was shining on the rue st. honoré, as i ran down the church 
steps. on one corner stood a barrow full of yellow jonquils, pale violet 
from the riviera, dark russia violets, and white roman hyacinth in a 
golden cloud of mimosa. the street was full of sunday pleasure-seekers. i 
swung my cane and laugh with the rest. some one overtook and pass me. 
he never turned, but there was the same deadly malignity in his white 
profile that there had been in his eyes. i watch him as long as i could 
see him. his lithe back express the same menace; every step that 
carry him away from me seem to bear him on some errand connected with 
my destruction. 

i was creep along, my feet almost refuse to move. there began to 
dawn in me a sense of responsibility for something long forgotten. it 
began to seem as if i deserve that which he threatened: it reach a 
long way back--a long, long way back. it had lain dormant all these 
years: it was there, though, and presently it would rise and confront me. 
but i would try to escape; and i stumble as best i could into the rue de 
rivoli, across the place de la concorde and on to the quai. i look with 
sick eyes upon the sun, shining through the white foam of the fountain, 
pour over the back of the dusky bronze river-gods, on the far-away 
arc, a structure of amethyst mist, on the countless vista of grey stem 
and bare branch faint green. then i saw him again coming down one of 
the chestnut alley of the cour la reine. 

i left the river-side, plunge blind across to the champ elysées and 
turn toward the arc. the setting sun was send its ray along the 
green sward of the rond-point: in the full glow he sat on a bench, 
children and young mother all about him. he was nothing but a sunday 
lounger, like the others, like myself. i said the words almost aloud, and 
all the while i gaze on the malignant hatred of his face. but he was not 
look at me. i crept past and drag my leaden feet up the avenue. i 
knew that every time i met him brought him near to the accomplishment 
of his purpose and my fate. and still i try to save myself. 

the last ray of sunset were pour through the great arc. i pass 
under it, and met him face to face. i had left him far down the champ 
elysées, and yet he came in with a stream of people who were return 
from the boi de boulogne. he came so close that he brush me. his 
slender frame felt like iron inside its loose black covering. he show 
no sign of haste, nor of fatigue, nor of any human feeling. his whole 
being express one thing: the will, and the power to work me evil. 

in anguish i watch him where he went down the broad crowded avenue, 
that was all flash with wheels and the trappings of horse and the 
helmet of the garde republicaine. 

he was soon lost to sight; then i turn and fled. into the bois, and far 
out beyond it--i know not where i went, but after a long while as it 
seem to me, night had fallen, and i found myself sitting at a table 
before a small café. i had wander back into the bois. it was hour now 
since i had seen him. physical fatigue and mental suffering had left me 
no power to think or feel. i was tired, so tired! i long to hide away 
in my own den. i resolve to go home. but that was a long way off. 

i live in the court of the dragon, a narrow passage that leads from the 
rue de renne to the rue du dragon. 

it is an "impasse"; traverse only for foot passengers. over the 
entrance on the rue de renne is a balcony, support by an iron dragon. 
within the court tall old house rise on either side, and close the end 
that give on the two streets. huge gates, swung back during the day into 
the wall of the deep archways, close this court, after midnight, and one 
must enter then by ring at certain small door on the side. the sunken 
pavement collect unsavoury pools. steep stairway pitch down to door 
that open on the court. the ground floor are occupy by shop of 
second-hand dealers, and by iron workers. all day long the place ring 
with the clink of hammer and the clang of metal bars. 

unsavoury as it is below, there is cheerfulness, and comfort, and hard, 
honest work above. 

five flight up are the atelier of architect and painters, and the 
hiding-places of middle-aged student like myself who want to live alone. 
when i first came here to live i was young, and not alone. 

i had to walk a while before any conveyance appeared, but at last, when i 
had almost reach the arc de triomphe again, an empty cab came along and 
i took it. 

from the arc to the rue de renne is a drive of more than half an hour, 
especially when one is convey by a tired cab horse that has been at the 
mercy of sunday fete-makers. 

there had been time before i pass under the dragon's wings to meet my 
enemy over and over again, but i never saw him once, and now refuge was 
close at hand. 

before the wide gateway a small mob of children were playing. our 
concierge and his wife walk among them, with their black poodle, 
keeping order; some couple were waltz on the side-walk. i return 
their greeting and hurried in. 

all the inhabitant of the court had troop out into the street. the 
place was quite deserted, light by a few lantern hung high up, in 
which the gas burn dimly. 

my apartment was at the top of a house, halfway down the court, reach 
by a staircase that descended almost into the street, with only a bit of 
passage-way intervening, i set my foot on the threshold of the open door, 
the friendly old ruinous stairs rose before me, leading up to rest and 
shelter. look back over my right shoulder, i saw _him,_ ten pace 
off. he must have enter the court with me. 

he was coming straight on, neither slowly, nor swiftly, but straight on 
to me. and now he was look at me. for the first time since our eyes 
encounter across the church they met now again, and i knew that the 
time had come. 

retreat backward, down the court, i face him. i meant to escape by 
the entrance on the rue du dragon. his eyes told me that i never should 
escape. 

it seem age while we were going, i retreating, he advancing, down the 
court in perfect silence; but at last i felt the shadow of the archway, 
and the next step brought me within it. i had meant to turn here and 
spring through into the street. but the shadow was not that of an 
archway; it was that of a vault. the great door on the rue du dragon 
were closed. i felt this by the black which surround me, and at the 
same instant i read it in his face. how his face gleam in the darkness, 
drawing swift nearer! the deep vaults, the huge closed doors, their 
cold iron clamp were all on his side. the thing which he had threaten 
had arrived: it gather and bore down on me from the fathomless shadows; 
the point from which it would strike was his infernal eyes. hopeless, i 
set my back against the barred door and defy him. 


there was a scrape of chair on the stone floor, and a rustling as the 
congregation rose. i could hear the suisse's staff in the south aisle, 
preceding monseigneur c---- to the sacristy. 

the kneel nuns, rouse from their devout abstraction, made their 
reverence and went away. the fashionable lady, my neighbour, rose also, 
with graceful reserve. as she departed her glance just flit over my 
face in disapproval. 

half dead, or so it seem to me, yet intense alive to every trifle, i 
sat among the leisurely moving crowd, then rose too and went toward the 
door. 

i had slept through the sermon. had i slept through the sermon? i look 
up and saw him passing along the gallery to his place. only his side i 
saw; the thin bent arm in its black covering look like one of those 
devilish, nameless instrument which lie in the disused torture-chambers 
of mediaeval castles. 

but i had escape him, though his eyes had said i should not. _had_ 
i escape him? that which gave him the power over me came back out of 
oblivion, where i had hope to keep it. for i knew him now. death and the 
awful abode of lost souls, whither my weakness long ago had sent 
him--they had change him for every other eye, but not for mine. i had 
recognize him almost from the first; i had never doubt what he was 
come to do; and now i knew while my body sat safe in the cheerful little 
church, he had been hunting my soul in the court of the dragon. 

i crept to the door: the organ broke out overhead with a blare. a 
dazzle light fill the church, blot the altar from my eyes. the 
people fade away, the arches, the vaulted roof vanished. i raise my 
sear eyes to the fathomless glare, and i saw the black stars hanging in 
the heavens: and the wet wind from the lake of hali chill my face. 

and now, far away, over league of toss cloud-waves, i saw the moon 
dripping with spray; and beyond, the tower of carcosa rose behind the 
moon. 

death and the awful abode of lost souls, whither my weakness long ago had 
sent him, had change him for every other eye but mine. and now i heard 
_his voice_, rising, swelling, thundering through the flare light, 
and as i fell, the radiance increasing, increasing, pour over me in 
wave of flame. then i sank into the depths, and i heard the king in 
yellow whisper to my soul: "it is a fearful thing to fall into the 
hand of the living god!"
