i 

there are so many things which are impossible to explain! why should 
certain chord in music make me think of the brown and golden tint of 
autumn foliage? why should the mass of sainte cécile bend my thought 
wandering among cavern whose wall blaze with ragged masses of virgin 
silver? what was it in the roar and turmoil of broadway at six o'clock 
that flash before my eyes the picture of a still breton forest where 
sunlight filter through spring foliage and sylvia bent, half curiously, 
half tenderly, over a small green lizard, murmuring: "to think that this 
also is a little ward of god!" 

when i first saw the watchman his back was toward me. i look at him 
indifferent until he went into the church. i paid no more attention to 
him than i had to any other man who lounge through washington square 
that morning, and when i shut my window and turn back into my studio i 
had forgotten him. late in the afternoon, the day being warm, i raise 
the window again and lean out to get a sniff of air. a man was standing 
in the courtyard of the church, and i notice him again with as little 
interest as i had that morning. i look across the square to where the 
fountain was play and then, with my mind fill with vague impression 
of trees, asphalt drives, and the moving group of nursemaid and 
holiday-makers, i start to walk back to my easel. as i turned, my 
listless glance included the man below in the churchyard. his face was 
toward me now, and with a perfectly involuntary movement i bent to see 
it. at the same moment he raise his head and look at me. instantly i 
thought of a coffin-worm. whatever it was about the man that repel me 
i did not know, but the impression of a plump white grave-worm was so 
intense and nauseate that i must have shown it in my expression, for he 
turn his puffy face away with a movement which made me think of a 
disturbed grub in a chestnut. 

i went back to my easel and motion the model to resume her pose. after 
working a while i was satisfy that i was spoil what i had done as 
rapid as possible, and i took up a palette knife and scrape the colour 
out again. the flesh tone were sallow and unhealthy, and i did not 
understand how i could have paint such sickly colour into a study which 
before that had glow with healthy tones. 

i look at tessie. she had not changed, and the clear flush of health 
dyed her neck and cheek as i frowned. 

"is it something i've done?" she said. 

"no,--i've made a mess of this arm, and for the life of me i can't see 
how i came to paint such mud as that into the canvas," i replied. 

"don't i pose well?" she insisted. 

"of course, perfectly." 

"then it's not my fault?" 

"no. it's my own." 

"i am very sorry," she said. 

i told her she could rest while i applied rag and turpentine to the 
plague spot on my canvas, and she went off to smoke a cigarette and look 
over the illustration in the _courrier français_. 

i did not know whether it was something in the turpentine or a defect in 
the canvas, but the more i scrub the more that gangrene seem to 
spread. i work like a beaver to get it out, and yet the disease 
appear to creep from limb to limb of the study before me. alarmed, i 
strove to arrest it, but now the colour on the breast change and the 
whole figure seem to absorb the infection as a sponge soak up water. 
vigorous i ply palette-knife, turpentine, and scraper, thinking all 
the time what a _séance_ i should hold with duval who had sold me 
the canvas; but soon i notice that it was not the canvas which was 
defective nor yet the colours of edward. "it must be the turpentine," i 
thought angrily, "or else my eyes have become so blur and confuse by 
the afternoon light that i can't see straight." i call tessie, the 
model. she came and lean over my chair blow ring of smoke into the 
air. 

"what _have_ you been do to it?" she exclaim 

"nothing," i growled, "it must be this turpentine!" 

"what a horrible colour it is now," she continued. "do you think my flesh 
resemble green cheese?" 

"no, i don't," i said angrily; "did you ever know me to paint like that 
before?" 

"no, indeed!" 

"well, then!" 

"it must be the turpentine, or something," she admitted. 

she slip on a japan robe and walk to the window. i scrape and 
rub until i was tired, and finally picked up my brush and hurl 
them through the canvas with a forcible expression, the tone alone of 
which reach tessie's ears. 

nevertheless she prompt began: "that's it! swear and act silly and ruin 
your brushes! you have been three week on that study, and now look! 
what's the good of rip the canvas? what creature artist are!" 

i felt about as much ashamed as i usually did after such an outbreak, and 
i turn the ruin canvas to the wall. tessie help me clean my 
brushes, and then dance away to dress. from the screen she regale me 
with bit of advice concerning whole or partial loss of temper, until, 
thinking, perhaps, i had been torment sufficiently, she came out to 
implore me to button her waist where she could not reach it on the 
shoulder. 

"everything went wrong from the time you came back from the window and 
talk about that horrid-looking man you saw in the churchyard," she 
announced. 

"yes, he probably bewitch the picture," i said, yawning. i look at my 
watch. 

"it's after six, i know," said tessie, adjust her hat before the 
mirror. 

"yes," i replied, "i didn't mean to keep you so long." i lean out of 
the window but recoil with disgust, for the young man with the pasty 
face stood below in the churchyard. tessie saw my gesture of disapproval 
and lean from the window. 

"is that the man you don't like?" she whispered. 

i nodded. 

"i can't see his face, but he do look fat and soft. someway or other," 
she continued, turning to look at me, "he remind me of a dream,--an 
awful dream i once had. or," she mused, look down at her shapely 
shoes, "was it a dream after all?" 

"how should i know?" i smiled. 

tessie smile in reply. 

"you were in it," she said, "so perhaps you might know something about 
it." 

"tessie! tessie!" i protested, "don't you dare flatter by saying that you 
dream about me!" 

"but i did," she insisted; "shall i tell you about it?" 

"go ahead," i replied, lighting a cigarette. 

tessie lean back on the open window-sill and began very seriously. 

"one night last winter i was lie in bed thinking about nothing at all 
in particular. i had been pose for you and i was tired out, yet it 
seem impossible for me to sleep. i heard the bell in the city ring 
ten, eleven, and midnight. i must have fallen asleep about midnight 
because i don't remember hearing the bell after that. it seem to me 
that i had scarcely closed my eyes when i dream that something impel 
me to go to the window. i rose, and raise the sash lean out. 
twenty-fifth street was desert as far as i could see. i began to be 
afraid; everything outside seem so--so black and uncomfortable. then 
the sound of wheels in the distance came to my ears, and it seem to me 
as though that was what i must wait for. very slow the wheels 
approached, and, finally, i could make out a vehicle moving along the 
street. it came near and nearer, and when it pass beneath my window i 
saw it was a hearse. then, as i tremble with fear, the driver turn and 
look straight at me. when i awoke i was standing by the open window 
shiver with cold, but the black-plumed hearse and the driver were 
gone. i dream this dream again in march last, and again awoke beside 
the open window. last night the dream came again. you remember how it was 
raining; when i awoke, standing at the open window, my night-dress was 
soaked." 

"but where did i come into the dream?" i asked. 

"you--you were in the coffin; but you were not dead." 

"in the coffin?" 

"yes." 

"how did you know? could you see me?" 

"no; i only knew you were there." 

"had you been eat welsh rarebits, or lobster salad?" i began, 
laughing, but the girl interrupt me with a frightened cry. 

"hello! what's up?" i said, as she shrank into the embrasure by the 
window. 

"the--the man below in the churchyard;--he drove the hearse." 

"nonsense," i said, but tessie's eyes were wide with terror. i went to 
the window and look out. the man was gone. "come, tessie," i urged, 
"don't be foolish. you have pose too long; you are nervous." 

"do you think i could forget that face?" she murmured. "three times i saw 
the hearse pass below my window, and every time the driver turn and 
look up at me. oh, his face was so white and--and soft? it look 
dead--it look as if it had been dead a long time." 

i induce the girl to sit down and swallow a glass of marsala. then i sat 
down beside her, and try to give her some advice. 

"look here, tessie," i said, "you go to the country for a week or two, 
and you'll have no more dream about hearses. you pose all day, and when 
night come your nerves are upset. you can't keep this up. then again, 
instead of go to bed when your day's work is done, you run off to 
picnic at sulzer's park, or go to the eldorado or coney island, and when 
you come down here next morning you are fagged out. there was no real 
hearse. there was a soft-shell crab dream." 

she smile faintly. 

"what about the man in the churchyard?" 

"oh, he's only an ordinary unhealthy, everyday creature." 

"as true as my name is tessie reardon, i swear to you, mr. scott, that 
the face of the man below in the churchyard is the face of the man who 
drove the hearse!" 

"what of it?" i said. "it's an honest trade." 

"then you think i _did_ see the hearse?" 

"oh," i said diplomatically, "if you really did, it might not be unlikely 
that the man below drove it. there is nothing in that." 

tessie rose, unroll her scent handkerchief, and taking a bit of gum 
from a knot in the hem, placed it in her mouth. then drawing on her 
glove she offer me her hand, with a frank, "good-night, mr. scott," 
and walk out. 




ii 

the next morning, thomas, the bell-boy, brought me the _herald_ and 
a bit of news. the church next door had been sold. i thank heaven for 
it, not that being a catholic i had any repugnance for the congregation 
next door, but because my nerves were shatter by a blatant exhorter, 
whose every word echo through the aisle of the church as if it had been 
my own rooms, and who insist on his r's with a nasal persistence which 
revolt my every instinct. then, too, there was a fiend in human shape, 
an organist, who reel off some of the grand old hymn with an 
interpretation of his own, and i long for the blood of a creature who 
could play the doxology with an amendment of minor chord which one hear 
only in a quartet of very young undergraduates. i believe the minister 
was a good man, but when he bellowed: "and the lorrrrd said unto moses, 
the lorrrd is a man of war; the lorrrd is his name. my wrath shall wax 
hot and i will kill you with the sworrrrd!" i wonder how many century 
of purgatory it would take to atone for such a sin. 

"who bought the property?" i ask thomas. 

"nobody that i knows, sir. they do say the gent wot own this 'ere 
'amilton flat was lookin' at it. 'e might be a bildin' more studios." 

i walk to the window. the young man with the unhealthy face stood by 
the churchyard gate, and at the mere sight of him the same overwhelming 
repugnance took possession of me. 

"by the way, thomas," i said, "who is that fellow down there?" 

thomas sniffed. "that there worm, sir? 'es night-watchman of the church, 
sir. 'e maike me tired a-sittin' out all night on them steps and lookin' 
at you insultin' like. i'd a punch 'is 'ed, sir--beg pardon, sir--" 

"go on, thomas." 

"one night a comin' 'ome with arry, the other english boy, i see 'im a 
sittin' there on them steps. we 'ad mol and jen with us, sir, the two 
girl on the tray service, an' 'e looks so insultin' at us that i up and 
sez: 'wat you look hat, you fat slug?'--beg pardon, sir, but that's 
'ow i sez, sir. then 'e don't say nothin' and i sez: 'come out and i'll 
punch that puddin' 'ed.' then i hopen the gate an' go in, but 'e don't 
say nothin', only looks insultin' like. then i 'its 'im one, but, ugh! 
'is 'ed was that cold and mushy it ud sicken you to touch 'im." 

"what did he do then?" i ask curiously. 

"'im? nawthin'." 

"and you, thomas?" 

the young fellow flushed with embarrassment and smile uneasily. 

"mr. scott, sir, i ain't no coward, an' i can't make it out at all why i 
run. i was in the 5th lawncers, sir, bugle at tel-el-kebir, an' was shot 
by the wells." 

"you don't mean to say you ran away?" 

"yes, sir; i run." 

"why?" 

"that's just what i want to know, sir. i grab mol an' run, an' the 
rest was as frightened as i." 

"but what were they frightened at?" 

thomas refuse to answer for a while, but now my curiosity was arouse 
about the repulsive young man below and i pressed him. three years' 
sojourn in america had not only modify thomas' cockney dialect but had 
given him the american's fear of ridicule. 

"you won't believe me, mr. scott, sir?" 

"yes, i will." 

"you will lawf at me, sir?" 

"nonsense!" 

he hesitated. "well, sir, it's gawd's truth that when i 'it 'im 'e 
grab me wrists, sir, and when i twist 'is soft, mushy fist one of 
'is finger come off in me 'and." 

the utter loathing and horror of thomas' face must have been reflect in 
my own, for he added: 

"it's orful, an' now when i see 'im i just go away. 'e maike me hill." 

when thomas had gone i went to the window. the man stood beside the 
church-railing with both hand on the gate, but i hasty retreat to my 
easel again, sicken and horrified, for i saw that the middle finger of 
his right hand was missing. 

at nine o'clock tessie appear and vanish behind the screen with a 
merry "good morning, mr. scott." when she had reappear and taken her 
pose upon the model-stand i start a new canvas, much to her delight. 
she remain silent as long as i was on the drawing, but as soon as the 
scrape of the charcoal cease and i took up my fixative she began to 
chatter. 

"oh, i had such a lovely time last night. we went to tony pastor's." 

"who are 'we'?" i demanded. 

"oh, maggie, you know, mr. whyte's model, and pinkie mccormick--we call 
her pinkie because she's got that beautiful red hair you artist like so 
much--and lizzie burke." 

i sent a shower of spray from the fixative over the canvas, and said: 
"well, go on." 

"we saw kelly and baby barnes the skirt-dancer and--and all the rest. i 
made a mash." 

"then you have gone back on me, tessie?" 

she laugh and shook her head. 

"he's lizzie burke's brother, ed. he's a perfect gen'l'man." 

i felt constrained to give her some parental advice concerning mashing, 
which she took with a bright smile. 

"oh, i can take care of a strange mash," she said, examine her chew 
gum, "but ed is different. lizzie is my best friend." 

then she related how ed had come back from the stocking mill in lowell, 
massachusetts, to find her and lizzie grown up, and what an accomplished 
young man he was, and how he thought nothing of squander half-a-dollar 
for ice-cream and oyster to celebrate his entry as clerk into the 
woollen department of macy's. before she finished i began to paint, and 
she resume the pose, smile and chatter like a sparrow. by noon i 
had the study fairly well rub in and tessie came to look at it. 

"that's better," she said. 

i thought so too, and ate my lunch with a satisfy feeling that all was 
go well. tessie spread her lunch on a drawing table opposite me and we 
drank our claret from the same bottle and light our cigarette from the 
same match. i was very much attach to tessie. i had watch her shoot 
up into a slender but exquisite form woman from a frail, awkward 
child. she had pose for me during the last three years, and among all my 
model she was my favourite. it would have trouble me very much indeed 
had she become "tough" or "fly," as the phrase goes, but i never notice 
any deteriorate of her manner, and felt at heart that she was all 
right. she and i never discuss morals at all, and i had no intention of 
do so, partly because i had none myself, and partly because i knew she 
would do what she like in spite of me. still i did hope she would steer 
clear of complications, because i wish her well, and then also i had a 
selfish desire to retain the best model i had. i knew that mashing, as 
she term it, had no significance with girl like tessie, and that such 
things in america did not resemble in the least the same things in paris. 
yet, have live with my eyes open, i also knew that somebody would take 
tessie away some day, in one manner or another, and though i professed to 
myself that marriage was nonsense, i sincerely hope that, in this case, 
there would be a priest at the end of the vista. i am a catholic. when i 
listen to high mass, when i sign myself, i feel that everything, 
including myself, is more cheerful, and when i confess, it do me good. 
a man who lives as much alone as i do, must confess to somebody. then, 
again, sylvia was catholic, and it was reason enough for me. but i was 
speak of tessie, which is very different. tessie also was catholic and 
much more devout than i, so, taking it all in all, i had little fear for 
my pretty model until she should fall in love. but _then_ i knew 
that fate alone would decide her future for her, and i pray inward 
that fate would keep her away from men like me and throw into her path 
nothing but ed burke and jimmy mccormicks, bless her sweet face! 

tessie sat blow ring of smoke up to the ceiling and tinkle the ice 
in her tumbler. 

"do you know that i also had a dream last night?" i observed. 

"not about that man," she laughed. 

"exactly. a dream similar to yours, only much worse." 

it was foolish and thoughtless of me to say this, but you know how little 
tact the average painter has. "i must have fallen asleep about ten 
o'clock," i continued, "and after a while i dreamt that i awoke. so 
plainly did i hear the midnight bells, the wind in the tree-branches, and 
the whistle of steamer from the bay, that even now i can scarcely 
believe i was not awake. i seem to be lie in a box which had a glass 
cover. dim i saw the street lamp as i passed, for i must tell you, 
tessie, the box in which i recline appear to lie in a cushion wagon 
which jolt me over a stony pavement. after a while i became impatient 
and try to move, but the box was too narrow. my hand were cross on 
my breast, so i could not raise them to help myself. i listen and then 
try to call. my voice was gone. i could hear the trample of the horse 
attach to the wagon, and even the breathing of the driver. then another 
sound broke upon my ear like the raise of a window sash. i manage to 
turn my head a little, and found i could look, not only through the glass 
cover of my box, but also through the glass pane in the side of the 
cover vehicle. i saw houses, empty and silent, with neither light nor 
life about any of them excepting one. in that house a window was open on 
the first floor, and a figure all in white stood look down into the 
street. it was you." 

tessie had turn her face away from me and lean on the table with her 
elbow. 

"i could see your face," i resumed, "and it seem to me to be very 
sorrowful. then we pass on and turn into a narrow black lane. 
presently the horse stopped. i wait and waited, close my eyes with 
ear and impatience, but all was silent as the grave. after what seem to 
me hours, i began to feel uncomfortable. a sense that somebody was close 
to me made me unclose my eyes. then i saw the white face of the 
hearse-driver look at me through the coffin-lid----" 

a sob from tessie interrupt me. she was tremble like a leaf. i saw i 
had made an ass of myself and attempt to repair the damage. 

"why, tess," i said, "i only told you this to show you what influence 
your story might have on another person's dreams. you don't suppose i 
really lay in a coffin, do you? what are you tremble for? don't you see 
that your dream and my unreasonable dislike for that inoffensive watchman 
of the church simply set my brain working as soon as i fell asleep?" 

she laid her head between her arms, and sob as if her heart would 
break. what a precious triple donkey i had made of myself! but i was 
about to break my record. i went over and put my arm about her. 

"tessie dear, forgive me," i said; "i had no business to frighten you 
with such nonsense. you are too sensible a girl, too good a catholic to 
believe in dreams." 

her hand tighten on mine and her head fell back upon my shoulder, but 
she still tremble and i pet her and comfort her. 

"come, tess, open your eyes and smile." 

her eyes open with a slow languid movement and met mine, but their 
expression was so queer that i hasten to reassure her again. 

"it's all humbug, tessie; you surely are not afraid that any harm will 
come to you because of that." 

"no," she said, but her scarlet lip quivered. 

"then, what's the matter? are you afraid?" 

"yes. not for myself." 

"for me, then?" i demand gaily. 

"for you," she murmur in a voice almost inaudible. "i--i care for you." 

at first i start to laugh, but when i understood her, a shock pass 
through me, and i sat like one turn to stone. this was the crown bit 
of idiocy i had committed. during the moment which elapse between her 
reply and my answer i thought of a thousand response to that innocent 
confession. i could pass it by with a laugh, i could misunderstand her 
and assure her as to my health, i could simply point out that it was 
impossible she could love me. but my reply was quick than my thoughts, 
and i might think and think now when it was too late, for i had kiss 
her on the mouth. 

that evening i took my usual walk in washington park, ponder over the 
occurrence of the day. i was thorough committed. there was no back out 
now, and i stare the future straight in the face. i was not good, not 
even scrupulous, but i had no idea of deceive either myself or tessie. 
the one passion of my life lay bury in the sunlit forest of brittany. 
was it bury for ever? hope cry "no!" for three years i had been 
listen to the voice of hope, and for three years i had wait for a 
footstep on my threshold. had sylvia forgotten? "no!" cry hope. 

i said that i was no good. that is true, but still i was not exactly a 
comic opera villain. i had led an easy-going reckless life, taking what 
invite me of pleasure, deplore and sometimes bitter regret 
consequences. in one thing alone, except my painting, was i serious, and 
that was something which lay hidden if not lost in the breton forests. 

it was too late for me to regret what had occur during the day. 
whatever it had been, pity, a sudden tender for sorrow, or the more 
brutal instinct of gratify vanity, it was all the same now, and unless 
i wish to bruise an innocent heart, my path lay marked before me. the 
fire and strength, the depth of passion of a love which i had never even 
suspected, with all my imagine experience in the world, left me no 
alternative but to respond or send her away. whether because i am so 
cowardly about give pain to others, or whether it was that i have 
little of the gloomy puritan in me, i do not know, but i shrank from 
disclaim responsibility for that thoughtless kiss, and in fact had no 
time to do so before the gate of her heart open and the flood pour 
forth. other who habitual do their duty and find a sullen satisfaction 
in making themselves and everybody else unhappy, might have withstood it. 
i did not. i dare not. after the storm had abate i did tell her that 
she might better have love ed burke and worn a plain gold ring, but she 
would not hear of it, and i thought perhaps as long as she had decided to 
love somebody she could not marry, it had better be me. i, at least, 
could treat her with an intelligent affection, and whenever she became 
tired of her infatuation she could go none the worse for it. for i was 
decided on that point although i knew how hard it would be. i remember 
the usual termination of platonic liaisons, and thought how disgust i 
had been whenever i heard of one. i knew i was undertaking a great deal 
for so unscrupulous a man as i was, and i dream the future, but never 
for one moment did i doubt that she was safe with me. had it been anybody 
but tessie i should not have bother my head about scruples. for it did 
not occur to me to sacrifice tessie as i would have sacrifice a woman of 
the world. i look the future square in the face and saw the several 
probable ending to the affair. she would either tire of the whole thing, 
or become so unhappy that i should have either to marry her or go away. 
if i married her we would be unhappy. i with a wife unsuited to me, and 
she with a husband unsuitable for any woman. for my past life could 
scarcely entitle me to marry. if i went away she might either fall ill, 
recover, and marry some eddie burke, or she might reckless or 
deliberate go and do something foolish. on the other hand, if she tired 
of me, then her whole life would be before her with beautiful vista of 
eddie burke and marriage ring and twin and harlem flat and heaven 
know what. as i stroll along through the tree by the washington arch, 
i decided that she should find a substantial friend in me, anyway, and 
the future could take care of itself. then i went into the house and put 
on my evening dress, for the little faintly-perfumed note on my dresser 
said, "have a cab at the stage door at eleven," and the note was sign 
"edith carmichel, metropolitan theatre." 

i took supper that night, or rather we took supper, miss carmichel and i, 
at solari's, and the dawn was just beginning to gild the cross on the 
memorial church as i enter washington square after leave edith at the 
brunswick. there was not a soul in the park as i pass along the tree 
and took the walk which leads from the garibaldi statue to the hamilton 
apartment house, but as i pass the churchyard i saw a figure sitting on 
the stone steps. in spite of myself a chill crept over me at the sight of 
the white puffy face, and i hasten to pass. then he said something 
which might have been address to me or might merely have been a mutter 
to himself, but a sudden furious anger flame up within me that such a 
creature should address me. for an instant i felt like wheeling about and 
smashing my stick over his head, but i walk on, and enter the 
hamilton went to my apartment. for some time i toss about the bed 
try to get the sound of his voice out of my ears, but could not. it 
fill my head, that mutter sound, like thick oily smoke from a 
fat-rendering vat or an odour of noisome decay. and as i lay and toss 
about, the voice in my ear seem more distinct, and i began to 
understand the words he had muttered. they came to me slow as if i had 
forgotten them, and at last i could make some sense out of the sounds. it 
was this: 

"have you found the yellow sign?" 

"have you found the yellow sign?" 

"have you found the yellow sign?" 

i was furious. what did he mean by that? then with a curse upon him and 
his i roll over and went to sleep, but when i awoke later i look pale 
and haggard, for i had dream the dream of the night before, and it 
trouble me more than i care to think. 

i dress and went down into my studio. tessie sat by the window, but as 
i came in she rose and put both arms around my neck for an innocent kiss. 
she look so sweet and dainty that i kiss her again and then sat down 
before the easel. 

"hello! where's the study i began yesterday?" i asked. 

tessie look conscious, but did not answer. i began to hunt among the 
piles of canvases, saying, "hurry up, tess, and get ready; we must take 
advantage of the morning light." 

when at last i gave up the search among the other canvas and turn to 
look around the room for the missing study i notice tessie standing by 
the screen with her clothes still on. 

"what's the matter," i asked, "don't you feel well?" 

"yes." 

"then hurry." 

"do you want me to pose as--as i have always posed?" 

then i understood. here was a new complication. i had lost, of course, 
the best nude model i had ever seen. i look at tessie. her face was 
scarlet. alas! alas! we had eaten of the tree of knowledge, and eden and 
native innocence were dream of the past--i mean for her. 

i suppose she notice the disappointment on my face, for she said: "i 
will pose if you wish. the study is behind the screen here where i put 
it." 

"no," i said, "we will begin something new;" and i went into my wardrobe 
and picked out a moorish costume which fairly blaze with tinsel. it was 
a genuine costume, and tessie retired to the screen with it enchanted. 
when she came forth again i was astonished. her long black hair was bound 
above her forehead with a circlet of turquoises, and the ends, curl 
about her glittering girdle. her feet were encase in the embroider 
pointed slipper and the skirt of her costume, curious wrought with 
arabesque in silver, fell to her ankles. the deep metallic blue vest 
embroider with silver and the short mauresque jacket spangle and sewn 
with turquoise became her wonderfully. she came up to me and held up her 
face smiling. i slip my hand into my pocket, and drawing out a gold 
chain with a cross attached, drop it over her head. 

"it's yours, tessie." 

"mine?" she faltered. 

"yours. now go and pose," then with a radiant smile she ran behind the 
screen and presently reappear with a little box on which was written my 
name. 

"i had intended to give it to you when i went home to-night," she said, 
"but i can't wait now." 

i open the box. on the pink cotton inside lay a clasp of black onyx, on 
which was inlaid a curious symbol or letter in gold. it was neither 
arabic nor chinese, nor, as i found afterwards, did it belong to any 
human script. 

"it's all i had to give you for a keepsake," she said timidly. 

i was annoyed, but i told her how much i should prize it, and promise to 
wear it always. she fasten it on my coat beneath the lapel. 

"how foolish, tess, to go and buy me such a beautiful thing as this," i 
said. 

"i did not buy it," she laughed. 

"where did you get it?" 

then she told me how she had found it one day while coming from the 
aquarium in the battery, how she had advertise it and watch the 
papers, but at last gave up all hope of finding the owner. 

"that was last winter," she said, "the very day i had the first horrid 
dream about the hearse." 

i remember my dream of the previous night but said nothing, and 
presently my charcoal was flying over a new canvas, and tessie stood 
motionless on the model-stand. 




iii 

the day following was a disastrous one for me. while moving a frame 
canvas from one easel to another my foot slip on the polish floor, 
and i fell heavy on both wrists. they were so badly sprain that it 
was useless to attempt to hold a brush, and i was oblige to wander about 
the studio, glaring at unfinished drawing and sketches, until despair 
seize me and i sat down to smoke and twiddle my thumb with rage. the 
rain blew against the window and rattle on the roof of the church, 
driving me into a nervous fit with its interminable patter. tessie sat 
sewing by the window, and every now and then raise her head and look 
at me with such innocent compassion that i began to feel ashamed of my 
irritation and look about for something to occupy me. i had read all 
the papers and all the books in the library, but for the sake of 
something to do i went to the bookcase and shove them open with my 
elbow. i knew every volume by its colour and examine them all, passing 
slow around the library and whistle to keep up my spirits. i was 
turning to go into the dining-room when my eye fell upon a book bound in 
serpent skin, standing in a corner of the top shelf of the last bookcase. 
i did not remember it, and from the floor could not decipher the pale 
lettering on the back, so i went to the smoking-room and call tessie. 
she came in from the studio and climb up to reach the book. 

"what is it?" i asked. 

"_the king in yellow._" 

i was dumfounded. who had placed it there? how came it in my rooms? i had 
long ago decided that i should never open that book, and nothing on earth 
could have persuade me to buy it. fearful lest curiosity might tempt me 
to open it, i had never even look at it in book-stores. if i ever had 
had any curiosity to read it, the awful tragedy of young castaigne, whom 
i knew, prevent me from explore its wicked pages. i had always 
refuse to listen to any description of it, and indeed, nobody ever 
venture to discuss the second part aloud, so i had absolutely no 
knowledge of what those leaves might reveal. i stare at the poisonous 
mottled binding as i would at a snake. 

"don't touch it, tessie," i said; "come down." 

of course my admonition was enough to arouse her curiosity, and before i 
could prevent it she took the book and, laughing, dance off into the 
studio with it. i call to her, but she slip away with a torment 
smile at my helpless hands, and i follow her with some impatience. 

"tessie!" i cried, enter the library, "listen, i am serious. put that 
book away. i do not wish you to open it!" the library was empty. i went 
into both drawing-rooms, then into the bedrooms, laundry, kitchen, and 
finally return to the library and began a systematic search. she had 
hidden herself so well that it was half-an-hour later when i discover 
her crouch white and silent by the lattice window in the store-room 
above. at the first glance i saw she had been punish for her 
foolishness. _the king in yellow_ lay at her feet, but the book was 
open at the second part. i look at tessie and saw it was too late. she 
had open _the king in yellow_. then i took her by the hand and led 
her into the studio. she seem dazed, and when i told her to lie down on 
the sofa she obey me without a word. after a while she closed her eyes 
and her breathing became regular and deep, but i could not determine 
whether or not she slept. for a long while i sat silent beside her, but 
she neither stir nor spoke, and at last i rose, and, enter the 
unused store-room, took the book in my least injure hand. it seem 
heavy as lead, but i carry it into the studio again, and sitting down 
on the rug beside the sofa, open it and read it through from beginning 
to end. 

when, faint with excess of my emotions, i drop the volume and lean 
weary back against the sofa, tessie open her eyes and look at 
me.... 

we had been speak for some time in a dull monotonous strain before i 
realize that we were discuss _the king in yellow_. oh the sin of 
writing such words,--words which are clear as crystal, limpid and musical 
as bubble springs, words which sparkle and glow like the poison 
diamond of the medicis! oh the wickedness, the hopeless damnation of a 
soul who could fascinate and paralyze human creature with such 
words,--words understood by the ignorant and wise alike, words which are 
more precious than jewels, more soothe than music, more awful than 
death! 

we talk on, unmindful of the gathering shadows, and she was beg me 
to throw away the clasp of black onyx quaint inlaid with what we now 
knew to be the yellow sign. i never shall know why i refused, though even 
at this hour, here in my bedroom as i write this confession, i should be 
glad to know _what_ it was that prevent me from tear the yellow 
sign from my breast and casting it into the fire. i am sure i wish to 
do so, and yet tessie plead with me in vain. night fell and the hour 
drag on, but still we murmur to each other of the king and the 
pallid mask, and midnight sound from the misty spire in the 
fog-wrapped city. we spoke of hastur and of cassilda, while outside the 
fog roll against the blank window-panes as the cloud wave roll and 
break on the shore of hali. 

the house was very silent now, and not a sound came up from the misty 
streets. tessie lay among the cushions, her face a grey blot in the 
gloom, but her hand were clasp in mine, and i knew that she knew and 
read my thought as i read hers, for we had understood the mystery of the 
hyade and the phantom of truth was laid. then as we answer each other, 
swiftly, silently, thought on thought, the shadow stir in the gloom 
about us, and far in the distant street we heard a sound. near and 
near it came, the dull crunch of wheels, near and yet nearer, and 
now, outside before the door it ceased, and i drag myself to the 
window and saw a black-plumed hearse. the gate below open and shut, and 
i crept shake to my door and bolt it, but i knew no bolts, no locks, 
could keep that creature out who was coming for the yellow sign. and now 
i heard him moving very soft along the hall. now he was at the door, 
and the bolt rot at his touch. now he had entered. with eyes start 
from my head i peer into the darkness, but when he came into the room i 
did not see him. it was only when i felt him envelope me in his cold soft 
grasp that i cry out and struggle with deadly fury, but my hand were 
useless and he tore the onyx clasp from my coat and struck me full in the 
face. then, as i fell, i heard tessie's soft cry and her spirit fled: and 
even while fall i long to follow her, for i knew that the king in 
yellow had open his tattered mantle and there was only god to cry to 
now. 

i could tell more, but i cannot see what help it will be to the world. as 
for me, i am past human help or hope. as i lie here, writing, careless 
even whether or not i die before i finish, i can see the doctor gathering 
up his powder and phial with a vague gesture to the good priest beside 
me, which i understand. 

they will be very curious to know the tragedy--they of the outside world 
who write books and print million of newspapers, but i shall write no 
more, and the father confessor will seal my last words with the seal of 
sanctity when his holy office is done. they of the outside world may send 
their creature into wreck home and death-smitten firesides, and their 
newspaper will batten on blood and tears, but with me their spy must 
halt before the confessional. they know that tessie is dead and that i am 
dying. they know how the people in the house, arouse by an infernal 
scream, rush into my room and found one living and two dead, but they 
do not know what i shall tell them now; they do not know that the doctor 
said as he pointed to a horrible decompose heap on the floor--the livid 
corpse of the watchman from the church: "i have no theory, no explanation. 
that man must have been dead for months!" 


i think i am dying. i wish the priest would-- 




the demoiselle d'ys 

"mais je croy que je 
sui descendu on puiz 
ténébreux onquel disoit 
heraclytu estre vereté cachée." 

"there be three things which are too wonderful for me, yea, four which i 
know not: 

"the way of an eagle in the air; the way of a serpent upon a rock; the 
way of a ship in the midst of the sea; and the way of a man with a maid." 


i 

the utter desolate of the scene began to have its effect; i sat down to 
face the situation and, if possible, recall to mind some landmark which 
might aid me in extricate myself from my present position. if i could 
only find the ocean again all would be clear, for i knew one could see 
the island of groix from the cliffs. 

i laid down my gun, and kneel behind a rock light a pipe. then i 
look at my watch. it was nearly four o'clock. i might have wander far 
from kerselec since daybreak. 

standing the day before on the cliff below kerselec with goulven, 
look out over the sombre moor among which i had now lost my way, 
these downs had appear to me level as a meadow, stretch to the 
horizon, and although i knew how deceptive is distance, i could not 
realize that what from kerselec seem to be mere grassy hollow were 
great valley cover with gorse and heather, and what look like 
scattered boulder were in reality enormous cliff of granite. 

"it's a bad place for a stranger," old goulven had said: "you'd better 
take a guide;" and i had replied, "i shall not lose myself." now i knew 
that i had lost myself, as i sat there smoking, with the sea-wind blow 
in my face. on every side stretch the moorland, cover with flowering 
gorse and heath and granite boulders. there was not a tree in sight, much 
less a house. after a while, i picked up the gun, and turning my back on 
the sun tramp on again. 

there was little use in following any of the brawl stream which every 
now and then cross my path, for, instead of flowing into the sea, they 
ran inland to reedy pools in the hollow of the moors. i had follow 
several, but they all led me to swamp or silent little pond from which 
the snipe rose peep and wheel away in an ecstasy of fright i began 
to feel fatigued, and the gun gall my shoulder in spite of the double 
pads. the sun sank lower and lower, shining level across yellow gorse and 
the moorland pools. 

as i walk my own gigantic shadow led me on, seeming to lengthen at 
every step. the gorse scrape against my leggings, crackle beneath my 
feet, shower the brown earth with blossoms, and the brake bowed and 
billow along my path. from tuft of heath rabbit scurry away through 
the bracken, and among the swamp grass i heard the wild duck's drowsy 
quack. once a fox stole across my path, and again, as i stoop to drink 
at a hurry rill, a heron flap heavy from the reeds beside me. i 
turn to look at the sun. it seem to touch the edge of the plain. 
when at last i decided that it was useless to go on, and that i must make 
up my mind to spend at least one night on the moors, i threw myself down 
thorough fagged out. the evening sunlight slant warm across my body, 
but the sea-winds began to rise, and i felt a chill strike through me 
from my wet shooting-boots. high overhead gull were wheeling and toss 
like bit of white paper; from some distant marsh a solitary curlew 
called. little by little the sun sank into the plain, and the zenith 
flushed with the after-glow. i watch the sky change from palest gold to 
pink and then to smoulder fire. cloud of midge dance above me, and 
high in the calm air a bat dip and soared. my eyelid began to droop. 
then as i shook off the drowsy a sudden crash among the bracken 
rouse me. i raise my eyes. a great bird hung quiver in the air above 
my face. for an instant i stared, incapable of motion; then something 
leap past me in the fern and the bird rose, wheeled, and pitch 
headlong into the brake. 

i was on my feet in an instant peer through the gorse. there came the 
sound of a struggle from a bunch of heather close by, and then all was 
quiet. i step forward, my gun poised, but when i came to the heather 
the gun fell under my arm again, and i stood motionless in silent 
astonishment a dead hare lay on the ground, and on the hare stood a 
magnificent falcon, one talon bury in the creature's neck, the other 
plant firm on its limp flank. but what astonish me, was not the 
mere sight of a falcon sitting upon its prey. i had seen that more than 
once. it was that the falcon was fitted with a sort of leash about both 
talons, and from the leash hung a round bit of metal like a sleigh-bell. 
the bird turn its fierce yellow eyes on me, and then stoop and struck 
its curve beak into the quarry. at the same instant hurried steps 
sound among the heather, and a girl sprang into the covert in front. 
without a glance at me she walk up to the falcon, and passing her 
glove hand under its breast, raise it from the quarry. then she deft 
slip a small hood over the bird's head, and holding it out on her 
gauntlet, stoop and picked up the hare. 

she pass a cord about the animal's leg and fasten the end of the 
thong to her girdle. then she start to retrace her steps through the 
covert as she pass me i raise my cap and she acknowledge my presence 
with a scarcely perceptible inclination. i had been so astonished, so 
lost in admiration of the scene before my eyes, that it had not occur 
to me that here was my salvation. but as she move away i recollect 
that unless i want to sleep on a windy moor that night i had better 
recover my speech without delay. at my first word she hesitated, and as i 
step before her i thought a look of fear came into her beautiful eyes. 
but as i humble explain my unpleasant plight, her face flushed and she 
look at me in wonder. 

"surely you did not come from kerselec!" she repeated. 

her sweet voice had no trace of the breton accent nor of any accent which 
i knew, and yet there was something in it i seem to have heard before, 
something quaint and indefinable, like the theme of an old song. 

i explain that i was an american, unacquainted with finistère, shoot 
there for my own amusement. 

"an american," she repeated in the same quaint musical tones. "i have 
never before seen an american." 

for a moment she stood silent, then look at me she said. "if you 
should walk all night you could not reach kerselec now, even if you had a 
guide." 

this was pleasant news. 

"but," i began, "if i could only find a peasant's hut where i might get 
something to eat, and shelter." 

the falcon on her wrist flutter and shook its head. the girl smooth 
its glossy back and glance at me. 

"look around," she said gently. "can you see the end of these moors? 
look, north, south, east, west. can you see anything but moorland and 
bracken?" 

"no," i said. 

"the moor is wild and desolate. it is easy to enter, but sometimes they 
who enter never leave it. there are no peasants' hut here." 

"well," i said, "if you will tell me in which direction kerselec lies, 
to-morrow it will take me no long to go back than it has to come." 

she look at me again with an expression almost like pity. 

"ah," she said, "to come is easy and take hours; to go is different--and 
may take centuries." 

i stare at her in amaze but decided that i had misunderstood her. 
then before i had time to speak she drew a whistle from her belt and 
sound it. 

"sit down and rest," she said to me; "you have come a long distance and 
are tired." 

she gather up her pleat skirt and motion me to follow picked her 
dainty way through the gorse to a flat rock among the ferns. 

"they will be here directly," she said, and taking a seat at one end of 
the rock invite me to sit down on the other edge. the after-glow was 
beginning to fade in the sky and a single star twinkle faint through 
the rosy haze. a long waver triangle of water-fowl drift southward 
over our heads, and from the swamp around plover were calling. 

"they are very beautiful--these moors," she said quietly. 

"beautiful, but cruel to strangers," i answered. 

"beautiful and cruel," she repeated dreamily, "beautiful and cruel." 

"like a woman," i said stupidly. 

"oh," she cry with a little catch in her breath, and look at me. her 
dark eyes met mine, and i thought she seem angry or frightened. 

"like a woman," she repeated under her breath, "how cruel to say so!" 
then after a pause, as though speak aloud to herself, "how cruel for 
him to say that!" 

i don't know what sort of an apology i offer for my inane, though 
harmless speech, but i know that she seem so trouble about it that i 
began to think i had said something very dreadful without knowing it, and 
remember with horror the pitfall and snare which the france language 
set for foreigners. while i was try to imagine what i might have 
said, a sound of voice came across the moor, and the girl rose to her 
feet. 

"no," she said, with a trace of a smile on her pale face, "i will not 
accept your apologies, monsieur, but i must prove you wrong, and that 
shall be my revenge. look. here come hastur and raoul." 

two men loom up in the twilight. one had a sack across his shoulder 
and the other carry a hoop before him as a waiter carry a tray. the 
hoop was fasten with strap to his shoulders, and around the edge of 
the circlet sat three hooded falcon fitted with tinkle bells. the girl 
step up to the falconer, and with a quick turn of her wrist 
transfer her falcon to the hoop, where it quick sidle off and 
nestle among its mates, who shook their hooded head and ruffle their 
feather till the belle jesse tinkle again. the other man step 
forward and bowing respectful took up the hare and drop it into the 
game-sack. 

"these are my piqueurs," said the girl, turning to me with a gentle 
dignity. "raoul is a good fauconnier, and i shall some day make him grand 
veneur. hastur is incomparable." 

the two silent men salute me respectfully. 

"did i not tell you, monsieur, that i should prove you wrong?" she 
continued. "this, then, is my revenge, that you do me the courtesy of 
accept food and shelter at my own house." 

before i could answer she spoke to the falconers, who start instantly 
across the heath, and with a gracious gesture to me she followed. i don't 
know whether i made her understand how profound grateful i felt, but 
she seem pleased to listen, as we walk over the dewy heather. 

"are you not very tired?" she asked. 

i had clean forgotten my fatigue in her presence, and i told her so. 

"don't you think your gallantry is a little old-fashioned?" she said; and 
when i look confuse and humbled, she add quietly, "oh, i like it, i 
like everything old-fashioned, and it is delightful to hear you say such 
pretty things." 

the moorland around us was very still now under its ghostly sheet of 
mist. the plover had cease their calling; the cricket and all the 
little creature of the field were silent as we passed, yet it seem to 
me as if i could hear them beginning again far behind us. well in 
advance, the two tall falconer strode across the heather, and the faint 
jingle of the hawks' bell came to our ear in distant murmur 
chimes. 

sudden a splendid hound dashed out of the mist in front, follow by 
another and another until half-a-dozen or more were bound and leap 
around the girl beside me. she caress and quiet them with her glove 
hand, speak to them in quaint terms which i remember to have seen in 
old france manuscripts. 

then the falcon on the circlet borne by the falconer ahead began to beat 
their wings and scream, and from somewhere out of sight the note of a 
hunting-horn float across the moor. the hound sprang away before us 
and vanish in the twilight, the falcon flap and squeal upon their 
perch, and the girl, taking up the song of the horn, began to hum. clear 
and mellow her voice sound in the night air. 

"chasseur, chasseur, chassez encore, 
quittez rosette et jeanneton, 
tonton, tonton, tontaine, tonton, 
ou, pour, rabattre, dès l'aurore, 
que le amour soient de planton, 
tonton, tontaine, tonton." 

as i listen to her lovely voice a grey mass which rapid grew more 
distinct loom up in front, and the horn rang out joyous through the 
tumult of the hound and falcons. a torch glimmer at a gate, a light 
stream through an opening door, and we step upon a wooden bridge 
which tremble under our feet and rose creak and strain behind us 
as we pass over the moat and into a small stone court, wall on every 
side. from an open doorway a man came and, bend in salutation, 
present a cup to the girl beside me. she took the cup and touched it 
with her lips, then lower it turn to me and said in a low voice, "i 
bid you welcome." 

at that moment one of the falconer came with another cup, but before 
hand it to me, present it to the girl, who taste it. the falconer 
made a gesture to receive it, but she hesitate a moment, and then, 
step forward, offer me the cup with her own hands. i felt this to 
be an act of extraordinary graciousness, but hardly knew what was 
expect of me, and did not raise it to my lip at once. the girl flushed 
crimson. i saw that i must act quickly. 

"mademoiselle," i faltered, "a stranger whom you have save from danger 
he may never realize empty this cup to the gentlest and loveliest 
hostess of france." 

"in his name," she murmured, crossing herself as i drain the cup. then 
step into the doorway she turn to me with a pretty gesture and, 
taking my hand in hers, led me into the house, saying again and again: 
"you are very welcome, indeed you are welcome to the château d'ys." 




ii 

i awoke next morning with the music of the horn in my ears, and leap 
out of the ancient bed, went to a curtain window where the sunlight 
filter through little deep-set panes. the horn cease as i look into 
the court below. 

a man who might have been brother to the two falconer of the night 
before stood in the midst of a pack of hounds. a curve horn was strap 
over his back, and in his hand he held a long-lashed whip. the dogs 
whine and yelped, dance around him in anticipation; there was the 
stamp of horses, too, in the wall yard. 

"mount!" cry a voice in breton, and with a clatter of hoof the two 
falconers, with falcon upon their wrists, rode into the courtyard among 
the hounds. then i heard another voice which sent the blood throb 
through my heart: "piriou louis, hunt the hound well and spare neither 
spur nor whip. thou raoul and thou gaston, see that the _epervier_ 
do not prove himself _niais_, and if it be best in your judgment, 
_faites courtoisie à l'oiseau. jardiner un oiseau_, like the 
_mué_ there on hastur's wrist, is not difficult, but thou, raoul, 
mayest not find it so simple to govern that _hagard_. twice last 
week he foam _au vif_ and lost the _beccade_ although he is 
use to the _leurre_. the bird acts like a stupid _branchier. 
paître un hagard n'est pas si facile."_ 

was i dreaming? the old language of falconry which i had read in yellow 
manuscripts--the old forgotten france of the middle age was sound in 
my ear while the hound bay and the hawks' bell tinkle accompaniment 
to the stamp horses. she spoke again in the sweet forgotten language: 

"if you would rather attach the _longe_ and leave thy _hagard au 
bloc_, raoul, i shall say nothing; for it were a pity to spoil so fair 
a day's sport with an ill-trained _sors_. _essimer abaisser_,--it is 
possibly the best way. _Ça lui donnera des reins._ i was perhaps hasty 
with the bird. it take time to pass _à la filière_ and the exercises 
_d'escap_." 

then the falconer raoul bowed in his stirrup and replied: "if it be the 
pleasure of mademoiselle, i shall keep the hawk." 

"it is my wish," she answered. "falconry i know, but you have yet to give 
me many a lesson in _autourserie_, my poor raoul. sieur piriou louis 
mount!" 

the huntsman sprang into an archway and in an instant returned, mount 
upon a strong black horse, follow by a piqueur also mounted. 

"ah!" she cry joyously, "speed glemarec rené! speed! speed all! sound 
thy horn, sieur piriou!" 

the silvery music of the hunting-horn fill the courtyard, the hound 
sprang through the gateway and galloping hoof-beats plunge out of the 
paved court; loud on the drawbridge, sudden muffled, then lost in the 
heather and bracken of the moors. distant and more distant sound the 
horn, until it became so faint that the sudden carol of a soar lark 
drown it in my ears. i heard the voice below respond to some call 
from within the house. 

"i do not regret the chase, i will go another time courtesy to the 
stranger, pelagie, remember!" 

and a feeble voice came quaver from within the house, 
"_courtoisie_." 

i stripped, and rub myself from head to foot in the huge earthen basin 
of icy water which stood upon the stone floor at the foot of my bed. then 
i look about for my clothes. they were gone, but on a settle near the 
door lay a heap of garment which i inspect with astonishment. as my 
clothes had vanished, i was compel to attire myself in the costume 
which had evidently been placed there for me to wear while my own clothes 
dried. everything was there, cap, shoes, and hunting doublet of silvery 
grey homespun; but the close-fitting costume and seamless shoe belong 
to another century, and i remember the strange costume of the three 
falconer in the court-yard. i was sure that it was not the modern dress 
of any portion of france or brittany; but not until i was dress and 
stood before a mirror between the window did i realize that i was 
clothe much more like a young huntsman of the middle age than like a 
breton of that day. i hesitate and picked up the cap. should i go down 
and present myself in that strange guise? there seem to be no help for 
it, my own clothes were gone and there was no bell in the ancient chamber 
to call a servant; so i contented myself with remove a short hawk's 
feather from the cap, and, opening the door, went downstairs. 

by the fireplace in the large room at the foot of the stairs an old 
breton woman sat spin with a distaff. she look up at me when i 
appeared, and, smile frankly, wish me health in the breton language, 
to which i laughing reply in french. at the same moment my hostess 
appear and return my salutation with a grace and dignity that sent a 
thrill to my heart. her lovely head with its dark curly hair was crown 
with a head-dress which set all doubt as to the epoch of my own costume 
at rest. her slender figure was exquisite set off in the homespun 
hunting-gown edge with silver, and on her gauntlet-covered wrist she 
bore one of her pet hawks. with perfect simplicity she took my hand 
and led me into the garden in the court, and seating herself before a 
table invite me very sweet to sit beside her. then she ask me in her 
soft quaint accent how i had pass the night, and whether i was very 
much inconvenience by wearing the clothes which old pelagie had put 
there for me while i slept. i look at my own clothes and shoes, dry 
in the sun by the garden-wall, and hate them. what horrors they were 
compare with the graceful costume which i now wore! i told her this 
laughing, but she agree with me very seriously. 

"we will throw them away," she said in a quiet voice. in my astonishment 
i attempt to explain that i not only could not think of accept 
clothes from anybody, although for all i knew it might be the custom of 
hospitality in that part of the country, but that i should cut an 
impossible figure if i return to france clothe as i was then. 

she laugh and toss her pretty head, saying something in old france 
which i did not understand, and then pelagie trot out with a tray on 
which stood two bowls of milk, a loaf of white bread, fruit, a platter of 
honey-comb, and a flagon of deep red wine. "you see i have not yet broken 
my fast because i wish you to eat with me. but i am very hungry," she 
smiled. 

"i would rather die than forget one word of what you have said!" i 
blurt out, while my cheek burned. "she will think me mad," i add to 
myself, but she turn to me with sparkle eyes. 

"ah!" she murmured. "then monsieur know all that there is of chivalry--" 

she cross herself and broke bread. i sat and watch her white hands, 
not daring to raise my eyes to hers. 

"will you not eat?" she asked. "why do you look so troubled?" 

ah, why? i knew it now. i knew i would give my life to touch with my lip 
those rosy palms--i understood now that from the moment when i look 
into her dark eyes there on the moor last night i had love her. my great 
and sudden passion held me speechless. 

"are you ill at ease?" she ask again. 

then, like a man who pronounce his own doom, i answer in a low voice: 
"yes, i am ill at ease for love of you." and as she did not stir nor 
answer, the same power move my lip in spite of me and i said, "i, who 
am unworthy of the lightest of your thoughts, i who abuse hospitality and 
repay your gentle courtesy with bold presumption, i love you." 

she lean her head upon her hands, and answer softly, "i love you. 
your words are very dear to me. i love you." 

"then i shall win you." 

"win me," she replied. 

but all the time i had been sitting silent, my face turn toward her. 
she, also silent, her sweet face rest on her upturned palm, sat facing 
me, and as her eyes look into mine i knew that neither she nor i had 
spoken human speech; but i knew that her soul had answer mine, and i 
drew myself up feeling youth and joyous love coursing through every vein. 
she, with a bright colour in her lovely face, seem as one awaken from 
a dream, and her eyes sought mine with a questioning glance which made me 
tremble with delight. we broke our fast, speak of ourselves. i told 
her my name and she told me hers, the demoiselle jeanne d'ys. 

she spoke of her father and mother's death, and how the nineteen of her 
years had been pass in the little fortify farm alone with her nurse 
pelagie, glemarec rené the piqueur, and the four falconers, raoul, 
gaston, hastur, and the sieur piriou louis, who had serve her father. 
she had never been outside the moorland--never even had seen a human soul 
before, except the falconer and pelagie. she did not know how she had 
heard of kerselec; perhaps the falconer had spoken of it. she knew the 
legend of loup garou and jeanne la flamme from her nurse pelagie. she 
embroider and spun flax. her hawk and hound were her only 
distraction. when she had met me there on the moor she had been so 
frightened that she almost drop at the sound of my voice. she had, it 
was true, seen ship at sea from the cliffs, but as far as the eye could 
reach the moor over which she gallop were destitute of any sign of 
human life. there was a legend which old pelagie told, how anybody once 
lost in the unexplored moorland might never return, because the moor 
were enchanted. she did not know whether it was true, she never had 
thought about it until she met me. she did not know whether the falconer 
had even been outside, or whether they could go if they would. the books 
in the house which pelagie, the nurse, had taught her to read were 
hundred of years old. 

all this she told me with a sweet serious seldom seen in any one but 
children. my own name she found easy to pronounce, and insisted, because 
my first name was philip, i must have france blood in me. she did not 
seem curious to learn anything about the outside world, and i thought 
perhaps she considered it had forfeit her interest and respect from the 
story of her nurse. 

we were still sitting at the table, and she was throw grape to the 
small field bird which came fearless to our very feet. 

i began to speak in a vague way of going, but she would not hear of it, 
and before i knew it i had promise to stay a week and hunt with hawk and 
hound in their company. i also obtain permission to come again from 
kerselec and visit her after my return. 

"why," she said innocently, "i do not know what i should do if you never 
came back;" and i, knowing that i had no right to awaken her with the 
sudden shock which the avowal of my own love would bring to her, sat 
silent, hardly daring to breathe. 

"you will come very often?" she asked. 

"very often," i said. 

"every day?" 

"every day." 

"oh," she sighed, "i am very happy. come and see my hawks." 

she rose and took my hand again with a childlike innocence of possession, 
and we walk through the garden and fruit tree to a grassy lawn which 
was border by a brook. over the lawn were scattered fifteen or twenty 
stump of trees--partially imbed in the grass--and upon all of these 
except two sat falcons. they were attach to the stump by thong which 
were in turn fasten with steel rivet to their leg just above the 
talons. a little stream of pure spring water flow in a winding course 
within easy distance of each perch. 

the bird set up a clamour when the girl appeared, but she went from one 
to another, caress some, taking other for an instant upon her wrist, 
or stoop to adjust their jesses. 

"are they not pretty?" she said. "see, here is a falcon-gentil. we call 
it 'ignoble,' because it take the quarry in direct chase. this is a blue 
falcon. in falconry we call it 'noble' because it rise over the quarry, 
and wheeling, drops upon it from above. this white bird is a gerfalcon 
from the north. it is also 'noble!' here is a merlin, and this tiercelet 
is a falcon-heroner." 

i ask her how she had learned the old language of falconry. she did not 
remember, but thought her father must have taught it to her when she was 
very young. 

then she led me away and show me the young falcon still in the nest. 
"they are term _niais_ in falconry," she explained. "a 
_branchier_ is the young bird which is just able to leave the nest 
and hop from branch to branch. a young bird which has not yet moult is 
call a _sors_, and a _mué_ is a hawk which has moult in 
captivity. when we catch a wild falcon which has change its plumage we 
term it a _hagard_. raoul first taught me to dress a falcon. shall i 
teach you how it is done?" 

she seat herself on the bank of the stream among the falcon and i 
threw myself at her feet to listen. 

then the demoiselle d'ys held up one rosy-tipped finger and began very 
gravely. 

"first one must catch the falcon." 

"i am caught," i answered. 

she laugh very prettily and told me my _dressage_ would perhaps be 
difficult, as i was noble. 

"i am already tamed," i replied; "jessed and belled." 

she laughed, delighted. "oh, my brave falcon; then you will return at my 
call?" 

"i am yours," i answer gravely. 

she sat silent for a moment. then the colour heighten in her cheek and 
she held up her finger again, saying, "listen; i wish to speak of 
falconry--" 

"i listen, countess jeanne d'ys." 

but again she fell into the reverie, and her eyes seem fixed on 
something beyond the summer clouds. 

"philip," she said at last. 

"jeanne," i whispered. 

"that is all,--that is what i wished," she sighed,--"philip and jeanne." 

she held her hand toward me and i touched it with my lips. 

"win me," she said, but this time it was the body and soul which spoke in 
unison. 

after a while she began again: "let us speak of falconry." 

"begin," i replied; "we have caught the falcon." 

then jeanne d'ys took my hand in both of hers and told me how with 
infinite patience the young falcon was taught to perch upon the wrist, 
how little by little it became use to the belle jesse and the 
_chaperon à cornette_. 

"they must first have a good appetite," she said; "then little by little 
i reduce their nourishment; which in falconry we call _pât_. when, 
after many nights pass _au bloc_ as these bird are now, i prevail 
upon the _hagard_ to stay quiet on the wrist, then the bird is 
ready to be taught to come for its food. i fix the _pât_ to the end 
of a thong, or _leurre_, and teach the bird to come to me as soon as 
i begin to whirl the cord in circle about my head. at first i drop the 
_pât_ when the falcon comes, and he eats the food on the ground. 
after a little he will learn to seize the _leurre_ in motion as i 
whirl it around my head or drag it over the ground. after this it is easy 
to teach the falcon to strike at game, always remember to _'faire 
courtoisie á l'oiseau'_, that is, to allow the bird to taste the 
quarry." 

a squeal from one of the falcon interrupt her, and she arose to adjust 
the _longe_ which had become whip about the _bloc_, but the 
bird still flap its wings and screamed. 

"what _is_ the matter?" she said. "philip, can you see?" 

i look around and at first saw nothing to cause the commotion, which 
was now heighten by the scream and flap of all the birds. then my 
eye fell upon the flat rock beside the stream from which the girl had 
risen. a grey serpent was moving slow across the surface of the 
boulder, and the eyes in its flat triangular head sparkle like jet. 

"a couleuvre," she said quietly. 

"it is harmless, is it not?" i asked. 

she pointed to the black v-shaped figure on the neck. 

"it is certain death," she said; "it is a viper." 

we watch the reptile moving slow over the smooth rock to where the 
sunlight fell in a broad warm patch. 

i start forward to examine it, but she clung to my arm crying, "don't, 
philip, i am afraid." 

"for me?" 

"for you, philip,--i love you." 

then i took her in my arms and kiss her on the lips, but all i could 
say was: "jeanne, jeanne, jeanne." and as she lay tremble on my breast, 
something struck my foot in the grass below, but i did not heed it. then 
again something struck my ankle, and a sharp pain shot through me. i 
look into the sweet face of jeanne d'ys and kiss her, and with all my 
strength lift her in my arms and flung her from me. then bending, i 
tore the viper from my ankle and set my heel upon its head. i remember 
feeling weak and numb,--i remember fall to the ground. through my 
slow glazing eyes i saw jeanne's white face bend close to mine, and 
when the light in my eyes went out i still felt her arms about my neck, 
and her soft cheek against my drawn lips. 


when i open my eyes, i look around in terror. jeanne was gone. i saw 
the stream and the flat rock; i saw the crush viper in the grass beside 
me, but the hawk and _blocs_ had disappeared. i sprang to my feet. 
the garden, the fruit trees, the drawbridge and the wall court were 
gone. i stare stupid at a heap of crumble ruins, ivy-covered and 
grey, through which great tree had pushed their way. i crept forward, 
drag my numb foot, and as i moved, a falcon sail from the 
tree-tops among the ruins, and soaring, mount in narrow circles, 
fade and vanish in the cloud above. 

"jeanne, jeanne," i cried, but my voice die on my lips, and i fell on my 
knee among the weeds. and as god will it, i, not knowing, had fallen 
kneel before a crumble shrine carve in stone for our mother of 
sorrows. i saw the sad face of the virgin wrought in the cold stone. i 
saw the cross and thorn at her feet, and beneath it i read: 

"pray for the soul of the 
demoiselle jeanne d'ys, 
who die 
in her youth for love of 
philip, a stranger. 
a.d. 1573." 

but upon the icy slab lay a woman's glove still warm and fragrant. 




the prophets' paradise 

"if but the vine and love abjure band 
are in the prophets' paradise to stand, 
alack, i doubt the prophets' paradise, 
were empty as the hollow of one's hand." 




the studio 

he smiled, saying, "seek her throughout the world." 

i said, "why tell me of the world? my world is here, between these wall 
and the sheet of glass above; here among gilded flagon and dull jewelled 
arms, tarnish frames and canvasses, black chest and high-backed 
chairs, quaint carve and stain in blue and gold." 

"for whom do you wait?" he said, and i answered, "when she come i shall 
know her." 

on my hearth a tongue of flame whisper secret to the whitening ashes. 
in the street below i heard footsteps, a voice, and a song. 

"for whom then do you wait?" he said, and i answered, "i shall know her." 

footsteps, a voice, and a song in the street below, and i knew the song 
but neither the steps nor the voice. 

"fool!" he cried, "the song is the same, the voice and steps have but 
change with years!" 

on the hearth a tongue of flame whisper above the whitening ashes: 
"wait no more; they have passed, the steps and the voice in the street 
below." 

then he smiled, saying, "for whom do you wait? seek her throughout the 
world!" 

i answered, "my world is here, between these wall and the sheet of glass 
above; here among gilded flagon and dull jewelled arms, tarnish frames 
and canvasses, black chest and high-backed chairs, quaint carve and 
stain in blue and gold." 




the phantom 

the phantom of the past would go no further. 

"if it is true," she sighed, "that you find in me a friend, let us turn 
back together. you will forget, here, under the summer sky." 

i held her close, pleading, caressing; i seize her, white with anger, 
but she resisted. 

"if it is true," she sighed, "that you find in me a friend, let us turn 
back together." 

the phantom of the past would go no further. 




the sacrifice 

i went into a field of flowers, whose petal are white than snow and 
whose heart are pure gold. 

far afield a woman cried, "i have kill him i loved!" and from a jar she 
pour blood upon the flower whose petal are white than snow and whose 
heart are pure gold. 

far afield i followed, and on the jar i read a thousand names, while from 
within the fresh blood bubble to the brim. 

"i have kill him i loved!" she cried. "the world's athirst; now let it 
drink!" she passed, and far afield i watch her pour blood upon the 
flower whose petal are white than snow and whose heart are pure gold. 




destiny 

i came to the bridge which few may pass. 

"pass!" cry the keeper, but i laughed, saying, "there is time;" and he 
smile and shut the gates. 

to the bridge which few may pass came young and old. all were refused. 
idle i stood and count them, until, weary of their noise and 
lamentations, i came again to the bridge which few may pass. 

those in the throng about the gate shriek out, "he come too late!" 
but i laughed, saying, "there is time." 

"pass!" cry the keeper as i entered; then smile and shut the gates. 




the throng 

there, where the throng was thickest in the street, i stood with pierrot. 
all eyes were turn on me. 

"what are they laugh at?" i asked, but he grinned, dust the chalk 
from my black cloak. "i cannot see; it must be something droll, perhaps 
an honest thief!" 

all eyes were turn on me. 

"he has rob you of your purse!" they laughed. 

"my purse!" i cried; "pierrot--help! it is a thief!" 

they laughed: "he has rob you of your purse!" 

then truth step out, holding a mirror. "if he is an honest thief," 
cry truth, "pierrot shall find him with this mirror!" but he only 
grinned, dust the chalk from my black cloak. 

"you see," he said, "truth is an honest thief, she bring you back your 
mirror." 

all eyes were turn on me. 

"arrest truth!" i cried, forget it was not a mirror but a purse i 
lost, standing with pierrot, there, where the throng was thickest in the 
street.

"stabbed," he tittered. "think of the long journey, the days of peril, 
the dreadful nights! think how he wandered, for her sake, year after 
year, through hostile lands, yearning for kith and kin, yearning for 
her!" 

"stabbed," he tittered, listen to the bell jingle on his cap. 

"was she fair?" i asked, but he only snarled, mutter to the bell 
jingle on his cap. 

"she kiss him at the gate," he tittered, "but in the hall his brother's 
welcome touched his heart." 

"was she fair?" i asked. 

"stabbed," he chuckled. "think of the long journey, the days of peril, 
the dreadful nights! think how he wandered, for her sake, year after year 
through hostile lands, yearning for kith and kin, yearning for her!" 

"she kiss him at the gate, but in the hall his brother's welcome 
touched his heart." 

"was she fair?" i asked; but he only snarled, listen to the bell 
jingle in his cap. 




the green room 

the clown turn his powdered face to the mirror. 

"if to be fair is to be beautiful," he said, "who can compare with me in 
my white mask?" 

"who can compare with him in his white mask?" i ask of death beside me. 

"who can compare with me?" said death, "for i am pale still." 

"you are very beautiful," sigh the clown, turning his powdered face 
from the mirror. 




the love test 

"if it is true that you love," said love, "then wait no longer. give her 
these jewel which would dishonour her and so dishonour you in loving 
one dishonoured. if it is true that you love," said love, "then wait no 
longer." 

i took the jewel and went to her, but she trod upon them, sobbing: 
"teach me to wait--i love you!" 

"then wait, if it is true," said love.

i 

the animal pause on the threshold, interrogative alert, ready for flight 
if necessary. severn laid down his palette, and held out a hand of 
welcome. the cat remain motionless, her yellow eyes fasten upon 
severn. 

"puss," he said, in his low, pleasant voice, "come in." 

the tip of her thin tail twitch uncertainly. 

"come in," he said again. 

apparently she found his voice reassuring, for she slow settled upon all 
fours, her eyes still fasten upon him, her tail tuck under her gaunt 
flanks. 

he rose from his easel smiling. she eye him quietly, and when he walk 
toward her she watch him bend above her without a wince; her eyes 
follow his hand until it touched her head. then she utter a ragged 
mew. 

it had long been severn's custom to converse with animals, probably 
because he live so much alone; and now he said, "what's the matter, 
puss?" 

her timid eyes sought his. 

"i understand," he said gently, "you shall have it at once." 

then moving quiet about he busy himself with the duty of a host, 
rinse a saucer, fill it with the rest of the milk from the bottle on 
the window-sill, and kneel down, crumble a roll into the hollow of his 
hand. 

the creature rose and crept toward the saucer. 

with the handle of a palette-knife he stir the crumb and milk together 
and step back as she thrust her nose into the mess. he watch her in 
silence. from time to time the saucer clink upon the tile floor as she 
reach for a morsel on the rim; and at last the bread was all gone, and 
her purple tongue travelled over every unlicked spot until the saucer 
shone like polish marble. then she sat up, and cool turning her back 
to him, began her ablutions. 

"keep it up," said severn, much interested, "you need it." 

she flatten one ear, but neither turn nor interrupt her toilet. as 
the grime was slow remove severn observe that nature had intended her 
for a white cat. her fur had disappear in patches, from disease or the 
chance of war, her tail was bony and her spine sharp. but what charm she 
had were becoming apparent under vigorous licking, and he wait until she 
had finished before re-opening the conversation. when at last she closed 
her eyes and fold her forepaw under her breast, he began again very 
gently: "puss, tell me your troubles." 

at the sound of his voice she broke into a harsh rumbling which he 
recognize as an attempt to purr. he bent over to rub her cheek and she 
mew again, an amiable inquiring little mew, to which he replied, 
"certainly, you are greatly improved, and when you recover your plumage 
you will be a gorgeous bird." much flattered, she stood up and march 
around and around his legs, push her head between them and making 
pleased remarks, to which he respond with grave politeness. 

"now, what sent you here," he said--"here into the street of the four 
winds, and up five flight to the very door where you would be welcome? 
what was it that prevent your meditate flight when i turn from my 
canvas to encounter your yellow eyes? are you a latin quarter cat as i am 
a latin quarter man? and why do you wear a rose-coloured flowered garter 
buckle about your neck?" the cat had climb into his lap, and now sat 
purr as he pass his hand over her thin coat. 

"excuse me," he continue in lazy soothe tones, harmonize with her 
purring, "if i seem indelicate, but i cannot help muse on this 
rose-coloured garter, flowered so quaint and fasten with a silver 
clasp. for the clasp is silver; i can see the mint mark on the edge, as is 
prescribed by the law of the france republic. now, why is this garter 
woven of rose silk and delicate embroidered,--why is this silken garter 
with its silver clasp about your famished throat? am i indiscreet when i 
inquire if its owner is your owner? is she some age dame living in memory 
of youthful vanities, fond, doting on you, decorate you with her 
intimate personal attire? the circumference of the garter would suggest 
this, for your neck is thin, and the garter fit you. but then again i 
notice--i notice most things--that the garter is capable of being much 
enlarged. these small silver-rimmed eyelets, of which i count five, are 
proof of that. and now i observe that the fifth eyelet is worn out, as 
though the tongue of the clasp were accustomed to lie there. that seem to 
argue a well-rounded form." 

the cat curl her toe in contentment. the street was very still outside.
