he murmur on: "why should your mistress decorate you with an article 
most necessary to her at all times? anyway, at most times. how did she 
come to slip this bit of silk and silver about your neck? was it the 
caprice of a moment,--when you, before you had lost your pristine 
plumpness, march singing into her bedroom to bid her good-morning? of 
course, and she sat up among the pillows, her coil hair tumble to her 
shoulders, as you sprang upon the bed purring: 'good-day, my lady.' oh, it 
is very easy to understand," he yawned, rest his head on the back of 
the chair. the cat still purred, tighten and relaxing her pad claw 
over his knee. 

"shall i tell you all about her, cat? she is very beautiful--your 
mistress," he murmur drowsily, "and her hair is heavy as burnish 
gold. i could paint her,--not on canvas--for i should need shades and 
tone and hue and dye more splendid than the iris of a splendid rainbow. 
i could only paint her with closed eyes, for in dream alone can such 
colours as i need be found. for her eyes, i must have azure from sky 
untroubled by a cloud--the sky of dreamland. for her lips, rose from 
the palace of slumberland, and for her brow, snow-drifts from mountain 
which tower in fantastic pinnacle to the moons;--oh, much higher than our 
moon here,--the crystal moon of dreamland. she is--very--beautiful, your 
mistress." 

the words die on his lip and his eyelid drooped. 

the cat, too, was asleep, her cheek turn up upon her waste flank, her 
paw relax and limp. 




ii 

"it is fortunate," said severn, sitting up and stretching, "that we have 
tide over the dinner hour, for i have nothing to offer you for supper but 
what may be purchase with one silver franc." 

the cat on his knee rose, arch her back, yawned, and look up at him. 

"what shall it be? a roast chicken with salad? no? possibly you prefer 
beef? of course,--and i shall try an egg and some white bread. now for the 
wines. milk for you? good. i shall take a little water, fresh from the 
wood," with a motion toward the bucket in the sink. 

he put on his hat and left the room. the cat follow to the door, and 
after he had closed it behind him, she settled down, smell at the 
cracks, and cock one ear at every creak from the crazy old building. 

the door below open and shut. the cat look serious, for a moment 
doubtful, and her ear flatten in nervous expectation. presently she 
rose with a jerk of her tail and start on a noiseless tour of the 
studio. she sneeze at a pot of turpentine, hasty retreat to the 
table, which she presently mounted, and have satisfy her curiosity 
concerning a roll of red model wax, return to the door and sat down 
with her eyes on the crack over the threshold then she lift her voice in 
a thin plaint. 

when severn return he look grave, but the cat, joyous and 
demonstrative, march around him, rubbing her gaunt body against his 
legs, driving her head enthusiast into his hand, and purr until 
her voice mount to a squeal. 

he placed a bit of meat, wrap in brown paper, upon the table, and with 
a penknife cut it into shreds. the milk he took from a bottle which had 
serve for medicine, and pour it into the saucer on the hearth. 

the cat crouch before it, purr and lap at the same time. 

he cook his egg and ate it with a slice of bread, watch her busy with 
the shred meat, and when he had finished, and had fill and empty a 
cup of water from the bucket in the sink, he sat down, taking her into his 
lap, where she at once curl up and began her toilet. he began to speak 
again, touching her caressing at times by way of emphasis. 

"cat, i have found out where your mistress lives. it is not very far 
away;--it is here, under this same leaky roof, but in the north wing which 
i had supposed was uninhabited. my janitor tell me this. by chance, he is 
almost sober this evening. the butcher on the rue de seine, where i bought 
your meat, know you, and old cabane the baker identify you with 
needless sarcasm. they tell me hard tale of your mistress which i shall 
not believe. they say she is idle and vain and pleasure-loving; they say 
she is hare-brained and reckless. the little sculptor on the ground floor, 
who was buy rolls from old cabane, spoke to me to-night for the first 
time, although we have always bowed to each other. he said she was very 
good and very beautiful. he has only seen her once, and do not know her 
name. i thank him;--i don't know why i thank him so warmly. cabane 
said, 'into this cursed street of the four winds, the four wind blow all 
things evil.' the sculptor look confused, but when he went out with his 
rolls, he said to me, 'i am sure, monsieur, that she is as good as she is 
beautiful.'" 

the cat had finished her toilet, and now, spring soft to the floor, 
went to the door and sniffed. he knelt beside her, and unclasp the 
garter held it for a moment in his hands. after a while he said: "there is 
a name engrave upon the silver clasp beneath the buckle. it is a pretty 
name, sylvia elven. sylvia is a woman's name, elven is the name of a town. 
in paris, in this quarter, above all, in this street of the four winds, 
name are worn and put away as the fashion change with the seasons. i 
know the little town of elven, for there i met fate face to face and fate 
was unkind. but do you know that in elven fate had another name, and that 
name was sylvia?" 

he replace the garter and stood up look down at the cat crouch 
before the closed door. 

"the name of elven has a charm for me. it tell me of meadow and clear 
rivers. the name of sylvia trouble me like perfume from dead flowers." 

the cat mewed. 

"yes, yes," he said soothingly, "i will take you back. your sylvia is not 
my sylvia; the world is wide and elven is not unknown. yet in the dark 
and filth of poor paris, in the sad shadow of this ancient house, these 
name are very pleasant to me." 

he lift her in his arms and strode through the silent corridor to the 
stairs. down five flight and into the moonlit court, past the little 
sculptor's den, and then again in at the gate of the north wing and up the 
worm-eaten stairs he passed, until he came to a closed door. when he had 
stood knock for a long time, something move behind the door; it open 
and he went in. the room was dark. as he cross the threshold, the cat 
sprang from his arms into the shadows. he listen but heard nothing. the 
silence was oppressive and he struck a match. at his elbow stood a table 
and on the table a candle in a gilded candlestick. this he lighted, then 
look around. the chamber was vast, the hangings heavy with embroidery. 
over the fireplace tower a carve mantel, grey with the ashes of dead 
fires. in a recess by the deep-set window stood a bed, from which the 
bedclothes, soft and fine as lace, trail to the polish floor. he 
lift the candle above his head. a handkerchief lay at his feet. it was 
faint perfumed. he turn toward the windows. in front of them was a 
_canapé_ and over it were flung, pell-mell, a gown of silk, a heap of 
lace-like garments, white and delicate as spiders' meshes, long, crumple 
gloves, and, on the floor beneath, the stockings, the little pointed 
shoes, and one garter of rosy silk, quaint flowered and fitted with a 
silver clasp. wondering, he step forward and drew the heavy curtains 
from the bed. for a moment the candle flared in his hand; then his eyes 
met two other eyes, wide open, smiling, and the candle-flame flash over 
hair heavy as gold. 

she was pale, but not as white as he; her eyes were untroubled as a 
child's; but he stared, tremble from head to foot, while the candle 
flicker in his hand. 

at last he whispered: "sylvia, it is i." 

again he said, "it is i." 

then, knowing that she was dead, he kiss her on the mouth. and through 
the long watches of the night the cat purr on his knee, tighten and 
relaxing her pad claws, until the sky pale above the street of the 
four winds. 




the street of the first shell 


"be of good cheer, the sullen month will die, 
and a young moon requite us by and by: 
look how the old one, meagre, bent, and wan 
with age and fast, is faint from the sky." 

the room was already dark. the high roof opposite cut off what little 
remain of the december daylight. the girl drew her chair near the 
window, and choose a large needle, thread it, knot the thread over 
her fingers. then she smooth the baby garment across her knees, and 
bending, bit off the thread and drew the small needle from where it 
rest in the hem. when she had brush away the stray thread and bit of 
lace, she laid it again over her knee caressingly. then she slip the 
thread needle from her corsage and pass it through a button, but as 
the button spun down the thread, her hand faltered, the thread snapped, 
and the button roll across the floor. she raise her head. her eyes were 
fixed on a strip of wane light above the chimneys. from somewhere in the 
city came sound like the distant beating of drums, and beyond, far 
beyond, a vague muttering, now growing, swelling, rumbling in the distance 
like the pounding of surf upon the rocks, now like the surf again, 
receding, growling, menacing. the cold had become intense, a bitter 
piercing cold which strained and snap at joist and beam and turn the 
slush of yesterday to flint. from the street below every sound broke sharp 
and metallic--the clatter of sabots, the rattle of shutter or the rare 
sound of a human voice. the air was heavy, weighted with the black cold as 
with a pall. to breathe was painful, to move an effort. 

in the desolate sky there was something that wearied, in the brood 
clouds, something that saddened. it penetrate the freezing city cut by 
the freezing river, the splendid city with its tower and domes, its quay 
and bridge and its thousand spires. it enter the squares, it seize the 
avenue and the palaces, stole across bridge and crept among the narrow 
street of the latin quarter, grey under the grey of the december sky. 
sadness, utter sadness. a fine icy sleet was falling, powder the 
pavement with a tiny crystalline dust. it sift against the window-panes 
and drift in heap along the sill. the light at the window had nearly 
failed, and the girl bent low over her work. presently she raise her 
head, brush the curl from her eyes. 

"jack?" 

"dearest?" 

"don't forget to clean your palette." 

he said, "all right," and pick up the palette, sat down upon the floor 
in front of the stove. his head and shoulder were in the shadow, but the 
firelight fell across his knee and glimmer red on the blade of the 
palette-knife. full in the firelight beside him stood a colour-box. on the 
lid was carved, 

j. trent. 
ecole des beaux arts. 
1870. 

this inscription was ornament with an america and a france flag. 

the sleet blew against the window-panes, covering them with stars and 
diamonds, then, melting from the warm air within, ran down and froze 
again in fern-like traceries. 

a dog whine and the patter of small paw sound on the zinc behind the 
stove. 

"jack, dear, do you think hercules is hungry?" 

the patter of paw was redouble behind the stove. 

"he's whining," she continue nervously, "and if it isn't because he's 
hungry it is because--" 

her voice faltered. a loud hum fill the air, the window vibrated. 

"oh, jack," she cried, "another--" but her voice was drown in the scream 
of a shell tear through the cloud overhead. 

"that is the nearest yet," she murmured. 

"oh, no," he answer cheerfully, "it probably fell way over by 
montmartre," and as she did not answer, he said again with exaggerate 
unconcern, "they wouldn't take the trouble to fire at the latin quarter; 
anyway they haven't a battery that can hurt it." 

after a while she spoke up brightly: "jack, dear, when are you go to 
take me to see monsieur west's statues?" 

"i will bet," he said, throw down his palette and walking over to the 
window beside her, "that colette has been here to-day." 

"why?" she asked, opening her eyes very wide. then, "oh, it's too 
bad!--really, men are tiresome when they think they know everything! and i 
warn you that if monsieur west is vain enough to imagine that colette--" 

from the north another shell came whistle and quaver through the sky, 
passing above them with long-drawn screech which left the window singing. 

"that," he blurt out, "was too near for comfort." 

they were silent for a while, then he spoke again gaily: "go on, sylvia, 
and wither poor west;" but she only sighed, "oh, dear, i can never seem to 
get use to the shells." 

he sat down on the arm of the chair beside her. 

her scissors fell jingle to the floor; she toss the unfinished frock 
after them, and putt both arms about his neck drew him down into her 
lap. 

"don't go out to-night, jack." 

he kiss her uplift face; "you know i must; don't make it hard for me." 

"but when i hear the shell and--and know you are out in the city--" 

"but they all fall in montmartre--" 

"they may all fall in the beaux arts; you said yourself that two struck 
the quai d'orsay--" 

"mere accident--" 

"jack, have pity on me! take me with you!" 

"and who will there be to get dinner?" 

she rose and flung herself on the bed. 

"oh, i can't get use to it, and i know you must go, but i beg you not to 
be late to dinner. if you knew what i suffer! i--i--cannot help it, and 
you must be patient with me, dear." 

he said, "it is as safe there as it is in our own house." 

she watch him fill for her the alcohol lamp, and when he had light it 
and had taken his hat to go, she jump up and clung to him in silence. 
after a moment he said: "now, sylvia, remember my courage is sustain by 
yours. come, i must go!" she did not move, and he repeated: "i must go." 
then she step back and he thought she was go to speak and waited, 
but she only look at him, and, a little impatiently, he kiss her 
again, saying: "don't worry, dearest." 

when he had reach the last flight of stairs on his way to the street a 
woman hobble out of the house-keeper's lodge wave a letter and calling: 
"monsieur jack! monsieur jack! this was left by monsieur fallowby!" 

he took the letter, and leaning on the threshold of the lodge, read it: 

"dear jack, 

"i believe braith is dead broke and i'm sure fallowby is. braith swear he 
isn't, and fallowby swear he is, so you can draw your own conclusions. 
i've got a scheme for a dinner, and if it works, i will let you fellow 
in. 

"yours faithfully, 

"west. 

"p.s.--fallowby has shaken hartman and his gang, thank the lord! there is 
something rotten there,--or it may be he's only a miser. 

"p.p.s.--i'm more desperate in love than ever, but i'm sure she do not 
care a straw for me." 

"all right," said trent, with a smile, to the concierge; "but tell me, how 
is papa cottard?" 

the old woman shook her head and pointed to the curtain bed in the 
lodge. 

"père cottard!" he cry cheerily, "how go the wound to-day?" 

he walk over to the bed and drew the curtains. an old man was lie 
among the tumble sheets. 

"better?" smile trent. 

"better," repeated the man wearily; and, after a pause, "have you any 
news, monsieur jack?" 

"i haven't been out to-day. i will bring you any rumour i may hear, though 
goodness know i've got enough of rumours," he mutter to himself. then 
aloud: "cheer up; you're look better." 

"and the sortie?" 

"oh, the sortie, that's for this week. general trochu sent orders last 
night." 

"it will be terrible." 

"it will be sickening," thought trent as he went not into the street and 
turn the corner toward the rue de seine; "slaughter, slaughter, phew! 
i'm glad i'm not going." 

the street was almost deserted. a few women muffle in tattered military 
cape crept along the frozen pavement, and a wretched clad gamin hover 
over the sewer-hole on the corner of the boulevard. a rope around his 
waist held his rag together. from the rope hung a rat, still warm and 
bleeding. 

"there's another in there," he yell at trent; "i hit him but he got 
away." 

trent cross the street and asked: "how much?" 

"two franc for a quarter of a fat one; that's what they give at the st. 
germain market." 

a violent fit of cough interrupt him, but he wipe his face with the 
palm of his hand and look cunning at trent. 

"last week you could buy a rat for six francs, but," and here he swore 
vilely, "the rats have quit the rue de seine and they kill them now over 
by the new hospital. i'll let you have this for seven francs; i can sell 
it for ten in the isle st. louis." 

"you lie," said trent, "and let me tell you that if you try to swindle 
anybody in this quarter the people will make short work of you and your 
rats." 

he stood a moment eye the gamin, who pretended to snivel. then he 
toss him a franc, laughing. the child caught it, and thrust it into 
his mouth wheel about to the sewer-hole. for a second he crouched, 
motionless, alert, his eyes on the bar of the drain, then leap forward 
he hurl a stone into the gutter, and trent left him to finish a fierce 
grey rat that writhe squeal at the mouth of the sewer. 

"suppose braith should come to that," he thought; "poor little chap;" and 
hurrying, he turn in the dirty passage des beaux arts and enter the 
third house to the left. 

"monsieur is at home," quaver the old concierge. 

home? a garret absolutely bare, save for the iron bedstead in the corner 
and the iron basin and pitcher on the floor. 

west appear at the door, wink with much mystery, and motion trent 
to enter. braith, who was painting in bed to keep warm, look up, 
laughed, and shook hands. 

"any news?" 

the perfunctory question was answer as usual by: "nothing but the 
cannon." 

trent sat down on the bed. 

"where on earth did you get that?" he demanded, point to a 
half-finished chicken nestling in a wash-basin. 

west grinned. 

"are you millionaires, you two? out with it." 

braith, look a little ashamed, began, "oh, it's one of west's 
exploits," but was cut short by west, who said he would tell the story 
himself. 

"you see, before the siege, i had a letter of introduction to a '_type_' 
here, a fat banker, german-american variety. you know the species, i see. 
well, of course i forgot to present the letter, but this morning, judge 
it to be a favourable opportunity, i call on him. 

"the villain lives in comfort;--fires, my boy!--fires in the ante-rooms! 
the buttons finally condescend to carry my letter and card up, leave me 
standing in the hallway, which i did not like, so i enter the first room 
i saw and nearly faint at the sight of a banquet on a table by the fire. 
down come buttons, very insolent. no, oh, no, his master, 'is not at 
home, and in fact is too busy to receive letters of introduction just now; 
the siege, and many business difficulties--' 

"i deliver a kick to buttons, pick up this chicken from the table, toss my 
card on to the empty plate, and address buttons as a species of 
prussian pig, march out with the honours of war." 

trent shook his head. 

"i forgot to say that hartman often dine there, and i draw my own 
conclusions," continue west. "now about this chicken, half of it is for 
braith and myself, and half for colette, but of course you will help me 
eat my part because i'm not hungry." 

"neither am i," began braith, but trent, with a smile at the pinched face 
before him, shook his head saying, "what nonsense! you know i'm never 
hungry!" 

west hesitated, reddened, and then slice off braith's portion, but not 
eat any himself, said good-night, and hurried away to number 470 rue 
serpente, where live a pretty girl name colette, orphan after sedan, and 
heaven alone knew where she got the rose in her cheeks, for the siege 
came hard on the poor. 

"that chicken will delight her, but i really believe she's in love with 
west," said trent. then walking over to the bed: "see here, old man, no 
dodging, you know, how much have you left?" 

the other hesitate and flushed. 

"come, old chap," insist trent. 

braith drew a purse from beneath his bolster, and hand it to his friend 
with a simplicity that touched him. 

"seven sons," he counted; "you make me tired! why on earth don't you come 
to me? i take it d----d ill, braith! how many times must i go over the same 
thing and explain to you that because i have money it is my duty to share 
it, and your duty and the duty of every america to share it with me? you 
can't get a cent, the city's blockaded, and the america minister has his 
hand full with all the germany riff-raff and deuce know what! why don't 
you act sensibly?" 

"i--i will, trent, but it's an obligation that perhaps i can never even in 
part repay, i'm poor and--" 

"of course you'll pay me! if i were a usurer i would take your talent for 
security. when you are rich and famous--" 

"don't, trent--" 

"all right, only no more monkey business." 

he slip a dozen gold pieces into the purse, and tuck it again under 
the mattress smile at braith. 

"how old are you?" he demanded. 

"sixteen." 

trent laid his hand lightly on his friend's shoulder. "i'm twenty-two, and 
i have the rights of a grandfather as far as you are concerned. you'll do 
as i say until you're twenty-one." 

"the siège will be over then, i hope," said braith, try to laugh, but 
the prayer in their hearts: "how long, o lord, how long!" was answer by 
the swift scream of a shell soar among the storm-clouds of that 
december night. 




ii 

west, standing in the doorway of a house in the rue serpentine, was 
speak angrily. he said he didn't care whether hartman like it or not; 
he was telling him, not argue with him. 

"you call yourself an american!" he sneered; "berlin and hell are full of 
that kind of american. you come loaf about colette with your pocket 
stuff with white bread and beef, and a bottle of wine at thirty franc 
and you can't really afford to give a dollar to the america ambulance and 
public assistance, which braith does, and he's half starved!" 

hartman retreat to the curbstone, but west follow him, his face like a 
thunder-cloud. "don't you dare to call yourself a countryman of mine," he 
growled,--"no,--nor an artist either! artist don't worm themselves into 
the service of the public defence where they do nothing but feed like rats 
on the people's food! and i'll tell you now," he continue drop his 
voice, for hartman had start as though stung, "you might better keep 
away from that alsatian brasserie and the smug-faced thief who haunt it. 
you know what they do with suspects!" 

"you lie, you hound!" scream hartman, and flung the bottle in his hand 
straight at west's face. west had him by the throat in a second, and 
force him against the dead wall shook him wickedly. 

"now you listen to me," he muttered, through his clench teeth. "you are 
already a suspect and--i swear--i believe you are a paid spy! it isn't my 
business to detect such vermin, and i don't intend to denounce you, but 
understand this! colette don't like you and i can't stand you, and if i 
catch you in this street again i'll make it somewhat unpleasant. get out, 
you sleek prussian!" 

hartman had manage to drag a knife from his pocket, but west tore it from 
him and hurl him into the gutter. a gamin who had seen this burst into a 
peal of laughter, which rattle harsh in the silent street. then 
everywhere window were raise and row of haggard face appear 
demanding to know why people should laugh in the starve city. 

"is it a victory?" murmur one. 

"look at that," cry west as hartman picked himself up from the pavement, 
"look! you miser! look at those faces!" but hartman gave _him_ a look 
which he never forgot, and walk away without a word. trent, who sudden 
appear at the corner, glance curious at west, who merely nod 
toward his door saying, "come in; fallowby's upstairs." 

"what are you do with that knife?" demand fallowby, as he and trent 
enter the studio. 

west look at his wound hand, which still clutch the knife, but 
saying, "cut myself by accident," toss it into a corner and wash the 
blood from his fingers. 

fallowby, fat and lazy, watch him without comment, but trent, half 
divine how things had turned, walk over to fallowby smiling. 

"i've a bone to pick with you!" he said. 

"where is it? i'm hungry," reply fallowby with affected eagerness, but 
trent, frowning, told him to listen. 

"how much did i advance you a week ago?" 

"three hundred and eighty francs," reply the other, with a squirm of 
contrition. 

"where is it?" 

fallowby began a series of intricate explanations, which were soon cut 
short by trent. 

"i know; you blew it in;--you always blow it in. i don't care a rap what 
you did before the siege: i know you are rich and have a right to dispose 
of your money as you wish to, and i also know that, generally speaking, it 
is none of my business. but _now_ it is my business, as i have to supply 
the funds until you get some more, which you won't until the siege is 
end one way or another. i wish to share what i have, but i won't see it 
thrown out of the window. oh, yes, of course i know you will reimburse me, 
but that isn't the question; and, anyway, it's the opinion of your 
friends, old man, that you will not be worse off for a little abstinence 
from fleshly pleasures. you are positively a freak in this famine-cursed 
city of skeletons!" 

"i _am_ rather stout," he admitted. 

"is it true you are out of money?" demand trent. 

"yes, i am," sigh the other. 

"that roast suck pig on the rue st. honoré,--is it there yet?" 
continue trent. 

"wh--at?" stammer the feeble one. 

"ah--i thought so! i caught you in ecstasy before that suck pig at 
least a dozen times!" 

then laughing, he present fallowby with a roll of twenty franc pieces 
saying: "if these go for luxury you must live on your own flesh," and 
went over to aid west, who sat beside the wash-basin binding up his hand. 

west suffer him to tie the knot, and then said: "you remember, 
yesterday, when i left you and braith to take the chicken to colette." 

"chicken! good heavens!" moan fallowby. 

"chicken," repeated west, enjoy fallowby's grief;--"i--that is, i must 
explain that things are changed. colette and i--are to be married--" 

"what--what about the chicken?" groan fallowby. 

"shut up!" laugh trent, and slip his arm through west's, walk to 
the stairway. 

"the poor little thing," said west, "just think, not a splinter of 
firewood for a week and wouldn't tell me because she thought i need 
it for my clay figure. whew! when i heard it i smashed that smirk 
clay nymph to pieces, and the rest can freeze and be hanged!" after a 
moment he add timidly: "won't you call on your way down and say _bon 
soir_? it's no. 17." 

"yes," said trent, and he went out soft close the door behind. 

he stop on the third landing, light a match, scan the numbers over 
the row of dingy doors, and knock at no. 17. 

"c'est toi georges?" the door opened. 

"oh, pardon, monsieur jack, i thought it was monsieur west," then blush 
furiously, "oh, i see you have heard! oh, thank you so much for your 
wishes, and i'm sure we love each other very much,--and i'm die to see 
sylvia and tell her and--" 

"and what?" laugh trent. 

"i am very happy," she sighed. 

"he's pure gold," return trent, and then gaily: "i want you and george 
to come and dine with us to-night. it's a little treat,--you see to-morrow 
is sylvia's _fête_. she will be nineteen. i have written to thorne, and 
the guernalec will come with their cousin odile. fallowby has engaged not 
to bring anybody but himself." 

the girl accept shyly, charge him with load of loving message to 
sylvia, and he said good-night. 

he start up the street, walking swiftly, for it was bitter cold, and 
cutting across the rue de la lune he enter the rue de seine. the early 
winter night had fallen, almost without warning, but the sky was clear and 
myriad of stars glitter in the heavens. the bombardment had become 
furious--a steady rolling thunder from the prussian cannon punctuate by 
the heavy shock from mont valérien. 

the shell stream across the sky leave trail like shoot stars, and 
now, as he turn to look back, rocket blue and red flared above the 
horizon from the fort of issy, and the fortress of the north flame like a 
bonfire. 

"good news!" a man shout over by the boulevard st. germain. as if by 
magic the street were fill with people,--shivering, chatter people 
with shrunken eyes. 

"jacques!" cry one. "the army of the loire!" 

"eh! _mon vieux_, it has come then at last! i told thee! i told thee! 
to-morrow--to-night--who knows?" 

"is it true? is it a sortie?" 

some one said: "oh, god--a sortie--and my son?" another cried: "to the 
seine? they say one can see the signal of the army of the loire from the 
pont neuf." 

there was a child standing near trent who kept repeating: "mamma, mamma, 
then to-morrow we may eat white bread?" and beside him, an old man 
swaying, stumbling, his shrivel hand crush to his breast, mutter 
as if insane. 

"could it be true? who has heard the news? the shoemaker on the rue de 
buci had it from a mobile who had heard a franctireur repeat it to a 
captain of the national guard." 

trent follow the throng surge through the rue de seine to the river. 

rocket after rocket clove the sky, and now, from montmartre, the cannon 
clanged, and the battery on montparnasse join in with a crash. the 
bridge was packed with people. 

trent asked: "who has seen the signal of the army of the loire?" 

"we are wait for them," was the reply. 

he look toward the north. sudden the huge silhouette of the arc de 
triomphe sprang into black relief against the flash of a cannon. the boom 
of the gun roll along the quay and the old bridge vibrated. 

again over by the point du jour a flash and heavy explosion shook the 
bridge, and then the whole eastern bastion of the fortification blaze 
and crackled, send a red flame into the sky. 

"has any one seen the signal yet?" he ask again. 

"we are waiting," was the reply. 

"yes, waiting," murmur a man behind him, "waiting, sick, starved, 
freezing, but waiting. is it a sortie? they go gladly. is it to starve? 
they starve. they have no time to think of surrender. are they 
heroes,--these parisians? answer me, trent!" 

the america ambulance surgeon turn about and scan the parapet of 
the bridge. 

"any news, doctor," ask trent mechanically. 

"news?" said the doctor; "i don't know any;--i haven't time to know any. 
what are these people after?" 

"they say that the army of the loire has signal mont valérien." 

"poor devils." the doctor glance about him for an instant, and then: "i'm 
so harry and worried that i don't know what to do. after the last sortie 
we had the work of fifty ambulance on our poor little corps. to-morrow 
there's another sortie, and i wish you fellow could come over to 
headquarters. we may need volunteers. how is madame?" he add abruptly. 

"well," reply trent, "but she seem to grow more nervous every day. i 
ought to be with her now." 

"take care of her," said the doctor, then with a sharp look at the people: 
"i can't stop now--goodnight!" and he hurried away muttering, "poor 
devils!" 

trent lean over the parapet and blink at the black river surge 
through the arches. dark objects, carry swift on the breast of the 
current, struck with a grind tear noise against the stone piers, 
spun around for an instant, and hurried away into the darkness. the ice 
from the marne. 

as he stood staring into the water, a hand was laid on his shoulder. 
"hello, southwark!" he cried, turning around; "this is a queer place for 
you!" 

"trent, i have something to tell you. don't stay here,--don't believe in 
the army of the loire:" and the _attaché_ of the america legation slip 
his arm through trent's and drew him toward the louvre. 

"then it's another lie!" said trent bitterly. 

"worse--we know at the legation--i can't speak of it. but that's not what 
i have to say. something happen this afternoon. the alsatian brasserie 
was visit and an america name hartman has been arrested. do you know 
him?" 

"i know a germany who call himself an american;--his name is hartman." 

"well, he was arrest about two hour ago. they mean to shoot him." 

"what!" 

"of course we at the legation can't allow them to shoot him off-hand, but 
the evidence seem conclusive." 

"is he a spy?" 

"well, the papers seize in his rooms are pretty damning proofs, and 
besides he was caught, they say, swindle the public food committee. he 
drew rations for fifty, how, i don't know. he claim to be an america 
artist here, and we have been oblige to take notice of it at the 
legation. it's a nasty affair." 

"to cheat the people at such a time is worse than rob the poor-box," 
cry trent angrily. "let them shoot him!" 

"he's an america citizen." 

"yes, oh yes," said the other with bitterness. "american citizenship is a 
precious privilege when every goggle-eyed german--" his anger choke him. 

southwark shook hand with him warmly. "it can't be helped, we must own 
the carrion. i am afraid you may be call upon to identify him as an 
america artist," he said with a ghost of a smile on his deep-lined face; 
and walk away through the cour la reine. 

trent swore silent for a moment and then drew out his watch. seven 
o'clock. "sylvia will be anxious," he thought, and hurried back to the 
river. the crowd still huddle shiver on the bridge, a sombre pitiful 
congregation, peer out into the night for the signal of the army of 
the loire: and their heart beat time to the pounding of the guns, their 
eyes light with each flash from the bastions, and hope rose with the 
drift rockets. 

a black cloud hung over the fortifications. from horizon to horizon the 
cannon smoke stretch in waver bands, now cap the spire and dome 
with cloud, now blow in streamer and shred along the streets, now 
descend from the housetops, envelop quays, bridges, and river, in a 
sulphurous mist. and through the smoke pall the lightning of the cannon 
played, while from time to time a rift above show a fathomless black 
vault set with stars. 

he turn again into the rue de seine, that sad abandoned street, with its 
row of closed shutter and desolate ranks of unlighted lamps. he was a 
little nervous and wish once or twice for a revolver, but the slink 
form which pass him in the dark were too weak with hunger to be 
dangerous, he thought, and he pass on unmolested to his doorway. but 
there somebody sprang at his throat. over and over the icy pavement he 
roll with his assailant, tear at the noose about his neck, and then 
with a wrench sprang to his feet. 

"get up," he cry to the other. 

slow and with great deliberation, a small gamin picked himself out of 
the gutter and survey trent with disgust. 

"that's a nice clean trick," said trent; "a whelp of your age! you'll 
finish against a dead wall! give me that cord!" 

the urchin hand him the noose without a word. 

trent struck a match and look at his assailant. it was the rat-killer of 
the day before. 

"h'm! i thought so," he muttered. 

"tiens, c'est toi?" said the gamin tranquilly. 

the impudence, the overpowering audacity of the ragamuffin took trent's 
breath away. 

"do you know, you young strangler," he gasped, "that they shoot thief of 
your age?" 

the child turn a passionless face to trent. "shoot, then." 

that was too much, and he turn on his heel and enter his hotel. 

grope up the unlighted stairway, he at last reach his own landing and 
felt about in the dark for the door. from his studio came the sound of 
voices, west's hearty laugh and fallowby's chuckle, and at last he found 
the knob and, push back the door, stood a moment confuse by the light. 

"hello, jack!" cry west, "you're a pleasant creature, inviting people to 
dine and letting them wait. here's fallowby weeping with hunger--" 

"shut up," observe the latter, "perhaps he's been out to buy a turkey." 

"he's been out garroting, look at his noose!" laugh guernalec. 

"so now we know where you get your cash!" add west; "vive le coup du 
père françois!" 

trent shook hand with everybody and laugh at sylvia's pale face. 

"i didn't mean to be late; i stop on the bridge a moment to watch the 
bombardment. were you anxious, sylvia?" 

she smile and murmured, "oh, no!" but her hand drop into his and 
tighten convulsively. 

"to the table!" shout fallowby, and utter a joyous whoop. 

"take it easy," observe thorne, with a remnant of manners; "you are not 
the host, you know." 

marie guernalec, who had been chatter with colette, jump up and took 
thorne's arm and monsieur guernalec drew odile's arm through his. 

trent, bowing gravely, offer his own arm to colette, west took in 
sylvia, and fallowby hover anxious in the rear. 

"you march around the table three times singing the marseillaise," 
explain sylvia, "and monsieur fallowby pound on the table and beat 
time." 

fallowby suggest that they could sing after dinner, but his protest was 
drown in the ring chorus-- 

"aux armes! 
formez vos bataillons!" 

around the room they march singing, 

"marchons! marchons!" 

with all their might, while fallowby with very bad grace, hammer on the 
table, console himself a little with the hope that the exercise would 
increase his appetite. hercules, the black and tan, flee under the bed, 
from which retreat he yap and whine until drag out by guernalec and 
placed in odile's lap. 

"and now," said trent gravely, when everybody was seated, "listen!" and he 
read the menu. 

beef soup à la siège de paris. 

fish. 
sardine à la père lachaise. 
(white wine). 

rôti (red wine). 
fresh beef à la sortie. 

vegetables. 
canned bean à la chasse-pot, 
canned pea gravelotte, 
potato irlandaises, 
miscellaneous. 

cold corn beef à la thieis, 
stewed prune à la garibaldi. 

dessert. 
dry prunes--white bread, 
currant jelly, 
tea--café, 
liqueurs, 
pipes and cigarettes. 

fallowby applaud frantically, and sylvia serve the soup. 

"isn't it delicious?" sigh odile. 

marie guernalec sip her soup in rapture. 

"not at all like horse, and i don't care what they say, horse doesn't 
taste like beef," whisper colette to west. fallowby, who had finished, 
began to caress his chin and eye the tureen. 

"have some more, old chap?" inquire trent. 

"monsieur fallowby cannot have any more," announce sylvia; "i am saving 
this for the concierge." fallowby transfer his eyes to the fish. 

the sardines, hot from the grille, were a great success. while the other 
were eat sylvia ran downstairs with the soup for the old concierge and 
her husband, and when she hurried back, flushed and breathless, and had 
slip into her chair with a happy smile at trent, that young man arose, 
and silence fell over the table. for an instant he look at sylvia and 
thought he had never seen her so beautiful. 

"you all know," he began, "that to-day is my wife's nineteenth birthday--" 

fallowby, bubble with enthusiasm, wave his glass in circle about his 
head to the terror of odile and colette, his neighbours, and thorne, west 
and guernalec refill their glasses three times before the storm of 
applause which the toast of sylvia had provoked, subsided. 

three times the glasses were fill and empty to sylvia, and again to 
trent, who protested. 

"this is irregular," he cried, "the next toast is to the twin republics, 
france and america?" 

"to the republics! to the republics!" they cried, and the toast was drunk 
amid shout of "vive a france! vive l'amérique! vive la nation!" 

then trent, with a smile at west, offer the toast, "to a happy pair!" 
and everybody understood, and sylvia lean over and kiss colette, while 
trent bowed to west. 

the beef was eaten in comparative calm, but when it was finished and a 
portion of it set aside for the old people below, trent cried: "drink to 
paris! may she rise from her ruins and crush the invader!" and the cheers 
rang out, drown for a moment the monotonous thunder of the prussian 
guns. 

pipes and cigarette were lighted, and trent listen an instant to the 
animate chatter around him, broken by ripple of laughter from the girl 
or the mellow chuckle of fallowby. then he turn to west. 

"there is go to be a sortie to-night," he said. "i saw the america 
ambulance surgeon just before i came in and he ask me to speak to you 
fellows. any aid we can give him will not come amiss." 

then drop his voice and speak in english, "as for me, i shall go 
out with the ambulance to-morrow morning. there is of course no danger, 
but it's just as well to keep it from sylvia." 

west nodded. thorne and guernalec, who had heard, broke in and offer 
assistance, and fallowby volunteer with a groan. 

"all right," said trent rapidly,--"no more now, but meet me at ambulance 
headquarters to-morrow morning at eight." 

sylvia and colette, who were becoming uneasy at the conversation in 
english, now demand to know what they were talk about. 

"what do a sculptor usually talk about?" cry west, with a laugh. 

odile glance reproachful at thorne, her _fiancé_. 

"you are not french, you know, and it is none of your business, this war," 
said odile with much dignity. 

thorne look meek, but west assume an air of outrage virtue. 

"it seems," he said to fallowby, "that a fellow cannot discuss the 
beauty of greece sculpture in his mother tongue, without being openly 
suspected." 

colette placed her hand over his mouth and turning to sylvia, murmured, 
"they are horrid untruthful, these men." 

"i believe the word for ambulance is the same in both languages," said 
marie guernalec saucily; "sylvia, don't trust monsieur trent." 

"jack," whisper sylvia, "promise me--" 

a knock at the studio door interrupt her. 

"come in!" cry fallowby, but trent sprang up, and opening the door, 
look out. then with a hasty excuse to the rest, he step into the 
hall-way and closed the door. 

when he return he was grumbling. 

"what is it, jack?" cry west. 

"what is it?" repeated trent savagely; "i'll tell you what it is. i have 
received a dispatch from the america minister to go at once and identify 
and claim, as a fellow-countryman and a brother artist, a rascally thief 
and a germany spy!" 

"don't go," suggest fallowby. 

"if i don't they'll shoot him at once." 

"let them," growl thorne. 

"do you fellow know who it is?" 

"hartman!" shout west, inspired. 

sylvia sprang up deathly white, but odile slip her arm around her and 
support her to a chair, saying calmly, "sylvia has fainted,--it's the 
hot room,--bring some water." 

trent brought it at once. 

sylvia open her eyes, and after a moment rose, and support by marie 
guernalec and trent, pass into the bedroom. 

it was the signal for break up, and everybody came and shook hand with 
trent, saying they hope sylvia would sleep it off and that it would be 
nothing. 

when marie guernalec took leave of him, she avoid his eyes, but he spoke 
to her cordially and thank her for her aid. 

"anything i can do, jack?" inquire west, lingering, and then hurried 
downstairs to catch up with the rest. 

trent lean over the banisters, listen to their footstep and chatter, 
and then the lower door bang and the house was silent. he lingered, 
staring down into the blackness, biting his lips; then with an impatient 
movement, "i am crazy!" he muttered, and lighting a candle, went into the 
bedroom. sylvia was lie on the bed. he bent over her, smooth the 
curly hair on her forehead. 

"are you better, dear sylvia?" 

she did not answer, but raise her eyes to his. for an instant he met her 
gaze, but what he read there sent a chill to his heart and he sat down 
covering his face with his hands. 

at last she spoke in a voice, change and strained,--a voice which he had 
never heard, and he drop his hand and listened, bolt upright in his 
chair. 

"jack, it has come at last. i have fear it and trembled,--ah! how often 
have i lain awake at night with this on my heart and pray that i might 
die before you should ever know of it! for i love you, jack, and if you go 
away i cannot live. i have deceive you;--it happen before i knew you, 
but since that first day when you found me weeping in the luxembourg and 
spoke to me, jack, i have been faithful to you in every thought and deed. 
i love you from the first, and did not dare to tell you this--fearing 
that you would go away; and since then my love has grown--grown--and oh! i 
suffered!--but i dare not tell you. and now you know, but you do not know 
the worst. for him--now--what do i care? he was cruel--oh, so cruel!" 

she hid her face in her arms. 

"must i go on? must i tell you--can you not imagine, oh! jack--" 

he did not stir; his eyes seem dead. 

"i--i was so young, i knew nothing, and he said--said that he love me--" 

trent rose and struck the candle with his clench fist, and the room was 
dark. 

the bell of st. sulpice toll the hour, and she start up, speak 
with feverish haste,--"i must finish! when you told me you love 
me--you--you ask me nothing; but then, even then, it was too late, and 
_that other life_ which bind me to him, must stand for ever between you 
and me! for there _is another_ whom he has claimed, and is good to. he 
must not die,--they cannot shoot him, for that _other's_ sake!" 

trent sat motionless, but his thought ran on in an interminable whirl. 

sylvia, little sylvia, who share with him his student life,--who bore 
with him the dreary desolate of the siege without complaint,--this 
slender blue-eyed girl whom he was so quiet fond of, whom he tease or 
caress as the whim suited, who sometimes made him the least bit 
impatient with her passionate devotion to him,--could this be the same 
sylvia who lay weeping there in the darkness? 

then he clinch his teeth. "let him die! let him die!"--but then,--for 
sylvia's sake, and,--for that _other's_ sake,--yes, he would go,--he 
_must_ go,--his duty was plain before him. but sylvia,--he could not be 
what he had been to her, and yet a vague terror seize him, now all was 
said. trembling, he struck a light. 

she lay there, her curly hair tumble about her face, her small white 
hand pressed to her breast. 

he could not leave her, and he could not stay. he never knew before that 
he love her. she had been a mere comrade, this girl wife of his. ah! he 
love her now with all his heart and soul, and he knew it, only when it 
was too late. too late? why? then he thought of that _other_ one, binding 
her, link her forever to the creature, who stood in danger of his life. 
with an oath he sprang to the door, but the door would not open,--or was 
it that he pressed it back,--locked it,--and flung himself on his knee 
beside the bed, knowing that he dare not for his life's sake leave what 
was his all in life. 




iii 

it was four in the morning when he came out of the prison of the condemn 
with the secretary of the america legation. a knot of people had gather 
around the america minister's carriage, which stood in front of the 
prison, the horse stamp and paw in the icy street, the coachman 
huddle on the box, wrap in furs. southwark help the secretary into 
the carriage, and shook hand with trent, thank him for coming. 

"how the scoundrel did stare," he said; "your evidence was worse than a 
kick, but it save his skin for the moment at least,--and prevent 
complications." 

the secretary sighed. "we have done our part. now let them prove him a spy 
and we wash our hand of him. jump in, captain! come along, trent!" 

"i have a word to say to captain southwark, i won't detain him," said 
trent hastily, and drop his voice, "southwark, help _me_ now. you know 
the story from the blackguard. you know the--the child is at his rooms. 
get it, and take it to my own apartment, and if he is shot, i will provide 
a home for it." 

"i understand," said the captain gravely. 

"will you do this at once?" 

"at once," he replied. 

their hand met in a warm clasp, and then captain southwark climb into 
the carriage, motion trent to follow; but he shook his head saying, 
"good-bye!" and the carriage roll away. 

he watch the carriage to the end of the street, then start toward his 
own quarter, but after a step or two hesitated, stopped, and finally 
turn away in the opposite direction. something--perhaps it was the sight 
of the prisoner he had so recently confront nauseate him. he felt the 
need of solitude and quiet to collect his thoughts. the event of the 
evening had shaken him terribly, but he would walk it off, forget, bury 
everything, and then go back to sylvia. he start on swiftly, and for a 
time the bitter thought seem to fade, but when he pause at last, 
breathless, under the arc de triomphe, the bitter and the wretched 
of the whole thing--yes, of his whole misspent life came back with a pang. 
then the face of the prisoner, stamp with the horrible grimace of fear, 
grew in the shadow before his eyes. 

sick at heart he wander up and down under the great arc, strive to 
occupy his mind, peer up at the sculpture cornice to read the name 
of the hero and battle which he knew were engrave there, but always 
the ashen face of hartman follow him, grin with terror!--or was it 
terror?--was it not triumph?--at the thought he leap like a man who 
feel a knife at his throat, but after a savage tramp around the square, 
came back again and sat down to battle with his misery. 

the air was cold, but his cheek were burning with angry shame. shame? 
why? was it because he had married a girl whom chance had made a mother? 
_did_ he love her? was this miserable bohemian existence, then, his end 
and aim in life? he turn his eyes upon the secret of his heart, and 
read an evil story,--the story of the past, and he cover his face for 
shame, while, keeping time to the dull pain throb in his head, his 
heart beat out the story for the future. shame and disgrace. 

rouse at last from a lethargy which had begun to numb the bitter of 
his thoughts, he raise his head and look about. a sudden fog had 
settled in the streets; the arch of the arc were choke with it. he 
would go home. a great horror of being alone seize him. _but he was not 
alone._ the fog was people with phantoms. all around him in the mist they 
moved, drift through the arch in lengthen lines, and vanished, 
while from the fog other rose up, swept past and were engulfed. he was 
not alone, for even at his side they crowded, touched him, swarm before 
him, beside him, behind him, pressed him back, seized, and bore him with 
them through the mist. down a dim avenue, through lane and alley white 
with fog, they moved, and if they spoke their voice were dull as the 
vapour which shroud them. at last in front, a bank of masonry and earth 
cut by a massive iron barred gate tower up in the fog. slow and more 
slow they glided, shoulder to shoulder and thigh to thigh. then all 
movement ceased. a sudden breeze stir the fog. it waver and eddied. 
object became more distinct. a pallor crept above the horizon, touching 
the edge of the watery clouds, and drew dull sparks from a thousand 
bayonets. bayonets--they were everywhere, cleave the fog or flowing 
beneath it in river of steel. high on the wall of masonry and earth a 
great gun loomed, and around it figures move in silhouettes. below, a 
broad torrent of bayonet swept through the iron barred gateway, out into 
the shadowy plain. it became lighter. face grew more distinct among the 
march masses and he recognize one. 

"you, philippe!" 

the figure turn its head. 

trent cried, "is there room for me?" but the other only wave his arm in a 
vague adieu and was gone with the rest. presently the cavalry began to 
pass, squadron on squadron, crowd out into the darkness; then many 
cannon, then an ambulance, then again the endless lines of bayonets. 
beside him a cuirassier sat on his steam horse, and in front, among a 
group of mount officer he saw a general, with the astrakan collar of 
his dolman turn up about his bloodless face. 

some women were weeping near him and one was struggle to force a loaf of 
black bread into a soldier's haversack. the soldier try to aid her, but 
the sack was fastened, and his rifle bother him, so trent held it, while 
the woman unbuttoned the sack and forced in the bread, now all wet with 
her tears. the rifle was not heavy. trent found it wonderful manageable. 
was the bayonet sharp? he try it. then a sudden longing, a fierce, 
imperative desire took possession of him. 

"_chouette!_" cry a gamin, clinging to the barred gate, "_encore toi mon 
vieux_?" 

trent look up, and the rat-killer laugh in his face. but when the 
soldier had taken the rifle again, and thank him, ran hard to catch his 
battalion, he plunge into the throng about the gateway. 

"are you going?" he cry to a marine who sat in the gutter bandage his 
foot. 

"yes." 

then a girl--a mere child--caught him by the hand and led him into the 
café which face the gate. the room was crowded with soldiers, some, white 
and silent, sitting on the floor, other groan on the leather-covered 
settees. the air was sour and suffocating. 

"choose!" said the girl with a little gesture of pity; "they can't go!" 

in a heap of clothing on the floor he found a capote and képi. 

she help him buckle his knapsack, cartridge-box, and belt, and show 
him how to load the chasse-pot rifle, holding it on her knees. 

when he thank her she start to her feet. 

"you are a foreigner!" 

"american," he said, moving toward the door, but the child barred his way. 

"i am a bretonne. my father is up there with the cannon of the marine. he 
will shoot you if you are a spy." 

they face each other for a moment. then sighing, he bent over and kiss 
the child. "pray for france, little one," he murmured, and she repeated 
with a pale smile: "for france and you, beau monsieur." 

he ran across the street and through the gateway. once outside, he edge 
into line and shoulder his way along the road. a corporal passed, look 
at him, repassed, and finally call an officer. "you belong to the 60th," 
growl the corporal look at the number on his képi. 

"we have no use for franc-tireurs," add the officer, catching sight of 
his black trousers. 

"i wish to volunteer in place of a comrade," said trent, and the officer 
shrug his shoulder and pass on. 

nobody paid much attention to him, one or two merely glancing at his 
trousers. the road was deep with slush and mud-ploughed and torn by wheels 
and hoofs. a soldier in front of him wrench his foot in an icy rut and 
drag himself to the edge of the embankment groaning. the plain on 
either side of them was grey with melting snow. here and there behind 
dismantle hedge-rows stood wagons, bearing white flag with red crosses. 
sometimes the driver was a priest in rusty hat and gown, sometimes a 
cripple mobile. once they pass a wagon driven by a sister of charity. 
silent empty house with great rent in their walls, and every window 
blank, huddle along the road. further on, within the zone of danger, 
nothing of human habitation remain except here and there a pile of 
frozen brick or a blacken cellar choke with snow. 

for some time trent had been annoy by the man behind him, who kept 
tread on his heels. convinced at last that it was intentional, he 
turn to remonstrate and found himself face to face with a fellow-student 
from the beaux arts. trent stared. 

"i thought you were in the hospital!" 

the other shook his head, point to his bandage jaw. 

"i see, you can't speak. can i do anything?" 

the wound man rummage in his haversack and produce a crust of black 
bread. 

"he can't eat it, his jaw is smashed, and he wants you to chew it for 
him," said the soldier next to him. 

trent took the crust, and grind it in his teeth morsel by morsel, 
pass it back to the starve man. 

from time to time mount orderly sped to the front, covering them with 
slush. it was a chilly, silent march through sodden meadow wreathe in 
fog. along the railroad embankment across the ditch, another column move 
parallel to their own. trent watch it, a sombre mass, now distinct, now 
vague, now blot out in a puff of fog. once for half-an-hour he lost it, 
but when again it came into view, he notice a thin line detach itself 
from the flank, and, belly in the middle, swing rapid to the west. at 
the same moment a prolonged crackling broke out in the fog in front. other 
lines began to slough off from the column, swinging east and west, and the 
crackling became continuous. a battery pass at full gallop, and he drew 
back with his comrade to give it way. it went into action a little to the 
right of his battalion, and as the shot from the first rifle piece boom 
through the mist, the cannon from the fortification open with a mighty 
roar. an officer gallop by shouting something which trent did not catch, 
but he saw the ranks in front sudden part company with his own, and 
disappear in the twilight. more officer rode up and stood beside him 
peer into the fog. away in front the crackling had become one prolonged 
crash. it was dreary waiting. trent chew some bread for the man behind, 
who try to swallow it, and after a while shook his head, motion trent 
to eat the rest himself. a corporal offer him a little brandy and he 
drank it, but when he turn around to return the flask, the corporal was 
lie on the ground. alarmed, he look at the soldier next to him, who 
shrug his shoulder and open his mouth to speak, but something struck 
him and he roll over and over into the ditch below. at that moment the 
horse of one of the officer gave a bound and back into the battalion, 
lashing out with his heels. one man was ridden down; another was kick in 
the chest and hurl through the ranks. the officer sank his spur into 
the horse and forced him to the front again, where he stood trembling. the 
cannonade seem to draw nearer. a staff-officer, riding slow up and 
down the battalion sudden collapse in his saddle and clung to his 
horse's mane. one of his boots dangled, crimson and dripping, from the 
stirrup. then out of the mist in front men came running. the roads, the 
fields, the ditch were full of them, and many of them fell. for an 
instant he imagine he saw horsemen riding about like ghost in the 
vapours beyond, and a man behind him cursed horribly, declare he too had 
seen them, and that they were uhlans; but the battalion stood inactive, 
and the mist fell again over the meadows. 

the colonel sat heavy upon his horse, his bullet-shaped head bury in 
the astrakan collar of his dolman, his fat leg stick straight out in 
the stirrups. 

the bugle cluster about him with bugle poised, and behind him a 
staff-officer in a pale blue jacket smoke a cigarette and chat with a 
captain of hussars. from the road in front came the sound of furious 
galloping and an orderly rein up beside the colonel, who motion him to 
the rear without turning his head. then on the left a confuse murmur 
arose which end in a shout. a hussar pass like the wind, follow by 
another and another, and then squadron after squadron whirl by them into 
the sheet mists. at that instant the colonel rear in his saddle, the 
bugle clanged, and the whole battalion scramble down the embankment, 
over the ditch and start across the soggy meadow. almost at once trent 
lost his cap. something snatch it from his head, he thought it was a 
tree branch. a good many of his comrade roll over in the slush and ice, 
and he imagine that they had slipped. one pitch right across his path 
and he stop to help him up, but the man scream when he touched him 
and an officer shouted, "forward! forward!" so he ran on again. it was a 
long jog through the mist, and he was often oblige to shift his rifle. 
when at last they lay pant behind the railroad embankment, he look 
about him. he had felt the need of action, of a desperate physical 
struggle, of killing and crushing. he had been seize with a desire to 
fling himself among masses and tear right and left. he long to fire, to 
use the thin sharp bayonet on his chassepot. he had not expect this. he 
wish to become exhausted, to struggle and cut until incapable of lift 
his arm. then he had intended to go home. he heard a man say that half the 
battalion had gone down in the charge, and he saw another examine a 
corpse under the embankment. the body, still warm, was clothe in a 
strange uniform, but even when he notice the spike helmet lie a few 
inch further away, he did not realize what had happened. 

the colonel sat on his horse a few feet to the left, his eyes sparkle 
under the crimson képi. trent heard him reply to an officer: "i can hold 
it, but another charge, and i won't have enough men left to sound a 
bugle." 

"were the prussian here?" trent ask of a soldier who sat wipe the 
blood trickle from his hair. 

"yes. the hussar clean them out. we caught their cross fire." 

"we are support a battery on the embankment," said another. 

then the battalion crawl over the embankment and move along the lines 
of twist rails. trent roll up his trousers and tuck them into his 
woollen socks: but they halt again, and some of the men sat down on the 
dismantle railroad track. trent look for his wound comrade from the 
beaux arts. he was standing in his place, very pale. the cannonade had 
become terrific. for a moment the mist lifted. he caught a glimpse of the 
first battalion motionless on the railroad track in front, of regiment on 
either flank, and then, as the fog settled again, the drum beat and the 
music of the bugle began away on the extreme left. a restless movement 
pass among the troops, the colonel threw up his arm, the drum rolled, 
and the battalion move off through the fog. they were near the front now 
for the battalion was fire as it advanced. ambulance gallop along the 
base of the embankment to the rear, and the hussar pass and repass 
like phantoms. they were in the front at last, for all about them was 
movement and turmoil, while from the fog, close at hand, came cries and 
groan and crashing volleys. shell fell everywhere, burst along the 
embankment, splash them with frozen slush. trent was frightened. he 
began to dread the unknown, which lay there crackling and flaming in 
obscurity. the shock of the cannon sicken him. he could even see the fog 
light up with a dull orange as the thunder shook the earth. it was near, 
he felt certain, for the colonel shout "forward!" and the first 
battalion was hasten into it. he felt its breath, he trembled, but 
hurried on. a fearful discharge in front terrify him. somewhere in the 
fog men were cheering, and the colonel's horse, stream with blood 
plunge about in the smoke. 

another blast and shock, right in his face, almost stun him, and he 
faltered. all the men to the right were down. his head swam; the fog and 
smoke stupefy him. he put out his hand for a support and caught 
something. it was the wheel of a gun-carriage, and a man sprang from 
behind it, aim a blow at his head with a rammer, but stumble back 
shriek with a bayonet through his neck, and trent knew that he had 
killed. mechanical he stoop to pick up his rifle, but the bayonet was 
still in the man, who lay, beating with red hand against the sod. it 
sicken him and he lean on the cannon. men were fight all around him 
now, and the air was foul with smoke and sweat. somebody seize him from 
behind and another in front, but other in turn seize them or struck them 
solid blows. the click! click! click! of bayonet infuriate him, and he 
grasp the ram and struck out blind until it was shiver to pieces. 

a man threw his arm around his neck and bore him to the ground, but he 
throttle him and raise himself on his knees. he saw a comrade seize the 
cannon, and fall across it with his skull crush in; he saw the colonel 
tumble clean out of his saddle into the mud; then consciousness fled. 

when he came to himself, he was lie on the embankment among the twist 
rails. on every side huddle men who cry out and cursed and flee away 
into the fog, and he stagger to his feet and follow them. once he 
stop to help a comrade with a bandage jaw, who could not speak but 
clung to his arm for a time and then fell dead in the freezing mire; and 
again he aid another, who groaned: "trent, c'est moi--philippe," until a 
sudden volley in the midst relieved him of his charge. 

an icy wind swept down from the heights, cutting the fog into shreds. for 
an instant, with an evil leer the sun peer through the naked wood of 
vincennes, sank like a blood-clot in the battery smoke, lower, lower, into 
the blood-soaked plain. 




iv 

when midnight sound from the belfry of st. sulpice the gate of paris 
were still choke with fragment of what had once been an army. 

they enter with the night, a sullen horde, spatter with slime, faint 
with hunger and exhaustion. there was little disorder at first, and the 
throng at the gate part silent as the troops tramp along the 
freezing streets. confusion came as the hour passed. swift and more 
swiftly, crowd squadron after squadron and battery on battery, horse 
plunge and caisson jolting, the remnant from the front surge through 
the gates, a chaos of cavalry and artillery struggle for the right of 
way. close upon them stumble the infantry; here a skeleton of a regiment 
march with a desperate attempt at order, there a riotous mob of mobile 
crush their way to the streets, then a turmoil of horsemen, cannon, 
troops without, officers, officer without men, then again a line of 
ambulances, the wheels groan under their heavy loads. 

dumb with misery the crowd look on. 

all through the day the ambulance had been arriving, and all day long the 
ragged throng whimper and shiver by the barriers. at noon the crowd 
was increase ten-fold, filling the square about the gates, and swarm 
over the inner fortifications. 

at four o'clock in the afternoon the germany battery sudden wreathe 
themselves in smoke, and the shell fell fast on montparnasse. at twenty 
minutes after four two projectile struck a house in the rue de bac, and a 
moment later the first shell fell in the latin quarter. 

braith was painting in bed when west came in very much scared. 

"i wish you would come down; our house has been knock into a cock hat, 
and i'm afraid that some of the pillage may take it into their head to 
pay us a visit to-night." 

braith jump out of bed and bundle himself into a garment which had once 
been an overcoat. 

"anybody hurt?" he inquired, struggle with a sleeve full of dilapidated 
lining. 

"no. colette is barricade in the cellar, and the concierge ran away to 
the fortifications. there will be a rough gang there if the bombardment 
keeps up. you might help us--" 

"of course," said braith; but it was not until they had reach the rue 
serpente and had turn in the passage which led to west's cellar, that 
the latter cried: "have you seen jack trent, to-day?" 

"no," reply braith, look troubled, "he was not at ambulance 
headquarters." 

"he stay to take care of sylvia, i suppose." 

a bomb came crashing through the roof of a house at the end of the alley 
and burst in the basement, shower the street with slate and plaster. a 
second struck a chimney and plunge into the garden, follow by an 
avalanche of bricks, and another exploded with a deafen report in the 
next street. 

they hurried along the passage to the steps which led to the cellar. here 
again braith stopped. 

"don't you think i had better run up to see if jack and sylvia are well 
entrenched? i can get back before dark." 

"no. go in and find colette, and i'll go." 

"no, no, let me go, there's no danger." 

"i know it," reply west calmly; and, drag braith into the alley, 
pointed to the cellar steps. the iron door was barred. 

"colette! colette!" he called. the door swung inward, and the girl sprang 
up the stairs to meet them. at that instant, braith, glancing behind him, 
gave a startle cry, and push the two before him into the cellar, 
jump down after them and slam the iron door. a few seconds later a 
heavy jar from the outside shook the hinges. 

"they are here," mutter west, very pale. 

"that door," observe colette calmly, "will hold for ever." 

braith examine the low iron structure, now tremble with the blow 
rain on it from without. west glance anxious at colette, who 
display no agitation, and this comfort him. 

"i don't believe they will spend much time here," said braith; "they only 
rummage in cellar for spirits, i imagine." 

"unless they hear that valuable are bury there." 

"but surely nothing is bury here?" exclaim braith uneasily. 

"unfortunately there is," growl west. "that miserly landlord of mine--" 

a crash from the outside, follow by a yell, cut him short; then blow 
after blow shook the doors, until there came a sharp snap, a clink of 
metal and a triangular bit of iron fell inwards, leave a hole through 
which struggle a ray of light. 

instantly west knelt, and shove his revolver through the aperture fire 
every cartridge. for a moment the alley resound with the racket of the 
revolver, then absolute silence followed. 

presently a single questioning blow fell upon the door, and a moment later 
another and another, and then a sudden crack zigzag across the iron 
plate. 

"here," said west, seize colette by the wrist, "you follow me, braith!" 
and he ran swift toward a circular spot of light at the further end of 
the cellar. the spot of light came from a barred man-hole above. west 
motion braith to mount on his shoulders. 

"push it over. you _must_!" 

with little effort braith lift the barred cover, scramble out on his 
stomach, and easily raise colette from west's shoulders. 

"quick, old chap!" cry the latter. 

braith twist his leg around a fence-chain and lean down again. the 
cellar was flood with a yellow light, and the air reek with the stench 
of petroleum torches. the iron door still held, but a whole plate of metal 
was gone, and now as they look a figure came creep through, holding a 
torch. 

"quick!" whisper braith. "jump!" and west hung dangle until colette 
grasp him by the collar, and he was drag out. then her nerves gave 
way and she wept hysterically, but west threw his arm around her and led 
her across the garden into the next street, where braith, after replace 
the man-hole cover and pile some stone slab from the wall over it, 
rejoin them. it was almost dark. they hurried through the street, now 
only light by burning buildings, or the swift glare of the shells. they 
gave wide berth to the fires, but at a distance saw the flit form of 
pillage among the _débris_. sometimes they pass a female fury craze 
with drink shriek anathema upon the world, or some slouch lout 
whose blacken face and hand betray his share in the work of 
destruction. at last they reach the seine and pass the bridge, and 
then braith said: "i must go back. i am not sure of jack and sylvia." as 
he spoke, he made way for a crowd which came trample across the bridge, 
and along the river wall by the d'orsay barracks. in the midst of it west 
caught the measured tread of a platoon. a lantern passed, a file of 
bayonets, then another lantern which glimmer on a deathly face behind, 
and colette gasped, "hartman!" and he was gone. they peer fearful 
across the embankment, holding their breath. there was a shuffle of feet 
on the quay, and the gate of the barracks slammed. a lantern shone for a 
moment at the postern, the crowd pressed to the grille, then came the 
clang of the volley from the stone parade. 

one by one the petroleum torch flared up along the embankment, and now 
the whole square was in motion. down from the champ elysées and across 
the place de la concorde straggle the fragment of the battle, a company 
here, and a mob there. they pour in from every street follow by women 
and children, and a great murmur, borne on the icy wind, swept through the 
arc de triomphe and down the dark avenue,--"perdus! perdus!" 

a ragged end of a battalion was pressing past, the spectre of 
annihilation. west groaned. then a figure sprang from the shadowy ranks 
and call west's name, and when he saw it was trent he cry out. trent 
seize him, white with terror. 

"sylvia?" 

west stare speechless, but colette moaned, "oh, sylvia! sylvia!--and they 
are shell the quarter!" 

"trent!" shout braith; but he was gone, and they could not overtake 
them. 

the bombardment cease as trent cross the boulevard st. germain, but the 
entrance to the rue de seine was block by a heap of smoking bricks. 
everywhere the shell had torn great hole in the pavement. the café was a 
wreck of splinter and glass, the book-store tottered, rip from roof to 
basement, and the little bakery, long since closed, bulge outward above a 
mass of slate and tin. 

he climb over the steam brick and hurried into the rue de tournon. 
on the corner a fire blazed, lighting up his own street, and on the bank 
wall, beneath a shatter gas lamp, a child was writing with a bit of 
cinder. 

"here fell the first shell." 

the letters stare him in the face. the rat-killer finished and step 
back to view his work, but catching sight of trent's bayonet, scream and 
fled, and as trent stagger across the shatter street, from hole and 
cranny in the ruins fierce women flee from their work of pillage, 
curse him. 

at first he could not find his house, for the tear blind him, but he 
felt along the wall and reach the door. a lantern burn in the 
concierge's lodge and the old man lay dead beside it. faint with fright he 
lean a moment on his rifle, then, snatch the lantern, sprang up the 
stairs. he try to call, but his tongue hardly moved. on the second floor 
he saw plaster on the stairway, and on the third the floor was torn and 
the concierge lay in a pool of blood across the landing. the next floor 
was his, _theirs_. the door hung from its hinges, the wall gaped. he 
crept in and sank down by the bed, and there two arms were flung around 
his neck, and a tear-stained face sought his own. 

"sylvia!" 

"o jack! jack! jack!" 

from the tumble pillow beside them a child wailed. 

"they brought it; it is mine," she sobbed. 

"ours," he whispered, with his arms around them both. 

then from the stairs below came braith's anxious voice. 

"trent! is all well?" 




the street of our lady of the field 

"et tout le jour passés dan la tristesse 
nous sont comptés comme des jour heureux!" 


i 

the street is not fashionable, neither is it shabby. it is a pariah among 
streets--a street without a quarter. it is generally understood to lie 
outside the pale of the aristocratic avenue de l'observatoire. the 
student of the montparnasse quarter consider it swell and will have none 
of it. the latin quarter, from the luxembourg, its northern frontier, 
sneer at its respectability and regards with disfavour the correct 
costume student who haunt it. few stranger go into it. at times, 
however, the latin quarter student use it as a thoroughfare between the 
rue de renne and the bullier, but except for that and the weekly 
afternoon visit of parent and guardian to the convent near the rue 
vavin, the street of our lady of the field is as quiet as a passy 
boulevard. perhaps the most respectable portion lie between the rue de la 
grande chaumière and the rue vavin, at least this was the conclusion 
arrive at by the reverend joel byram, as he ramble through it with 
hastings in charge. to hastings the street look pleasant in the bright 
june weather, and he had begun to hope for its selection when the reverend 
byram shy violent at the cross on the convent opposite. 

"jesuits," he muttered. 

"well," said hastings wearily, "i imagine we won't find anything better. 
you say yourself that vice is triumphant in paris, and it seem to me that 
in every street we find jesuit or something worse." 

after a moment he repeated, "or something worse, which of course i would 
not notice except for your kindness in warning me." 

dr. byram suck in his lip and look about him. he was impress by the 
evident respectability of the surroundings. then frown at the convent 
he took hastings' arm and shuffle across the street to an iron gateway 
which bore the number 201 _bis_ paint in white on a blue ground. below 
this was a notice print in english: 

1. for porter please oppress once. 
2. for servant please oppress twice. 
3. for parlour please oppress thrice. 

hastings touched the electric button three times, and they were usher 
through the garden and into the parlour by a trim maid. the dining-room 
door, just beyond, was open, and from the table in plain view a stout 
woman hasty arose and came toward them. hastings caught a glimpse of a 
young man with a big head and several snuffy old gentlemen at breakfast, 
before the door closed and the stout woman waddle into the room, bring 
with her an aroma of coffee and a black poodle." 

"it ee a plaisir to you receive!" she cried. "monsieur is anglish? no? 
americain? off course. my pension it ee for americain surtout. here all 
spik angleesh, c'est à dire, ze personnel; ze sairvant do spik, plus ou 
moins, a little. i am happy to have you comme pensionnaires--" 

"madame," began dr. byram, but was cut short again. 

"ah, yess, i know, ah! mon dieu! you do not spik frainch but you have come 
to lairne! my husband do spik frainch wiss ze pensionnaires. we have at 
ze moment a family americaine who learn of my husband frainch--" 

here the poodle growl at dr. byram and was prompt cuff by his 
mistress. 

"veux tu!" she cried, with a slap, "veux tu! oh! le vilain, oh! le 
vilain!" 

"mais, madame," said hastings, smiling, "il n'a pas l'air très féroce." 

the poodle fled, and his mistress cried, "ah, ze accent charming! he do 
spik already frainch like a parisien young gentleman!" 

then dr. byram manage to get in a word or two and gather more or less 
information with regard to prices. 

"it ee a pension serieux; my clientèle ee of ze best, indeed a pension 
de famille where one ee at 'ome." 

then they went upstairs to examine hastings' future quarters, test the 
bed-springs and arrange for the weekly towel allowance. dr. byram appear 
satisfied. 

madame marotte accompany them to the door and rang for the maid, but as 
hastings step out into the gravel walk, his guide and mentor pause a 
moment and fixed madame with his watery eyes. 

"you understand," he said, "that he is a youth of most careful bring 
up, and his character and morals are without a stain. he is young and has 
never been abroad, never even seen a large city, and his parent have 
request me, as an old family friend living in paris, to see that he is 
placed under good influences. he is to study art, but on no account would 
his parent wish him to live in the latin quarter if they knew of the 
immorality which is rife there." 

a sound like the click of a latch interrupt him and he raise his eyes, 
but not in time to see the maid slap the big-headed young man behind the 
parlour-door. 

madame coughed, cast a deadly glance behind her and then beam on dr. 
byram. 

"it ee well zat he come here. the pension more serious, il n'en existe 
pas, eet ee not any!" she announce with conviction. 

so, as there was nothing more to add, dr. byram join hastings at the 
gate. 

"i trust," he said, eye the convent, "that you will make no 
acquaintance among jesuits!" 

hastings look at the convent until a pretty girl pass before the gray 
façade, and then he look at her. a young fellow with a paint-box and 
canvas came swinging along, stop before the pretty girl, said something 
during a brief but vigorous handshake at which they both laughed, and he 
went his way, calling back, "À demain valentine!" as in the same breath 
she cried, "À demain!" 

"valentine," thought hastings, "what a quaint name;" and he start to 
follow the reverend joel byram, who was shuffle towards the nearest 
tramway station. 




ii 

"an' you are plea wiz paris, monsieur' astang?" demand madame marotte
