the next morning as hastings came into the breakfast-room of the pension, 
rosy from his plunge in the limited bath above. 

"i am sure i shall like it," he replied, wonder at his own depression 
of spirits. 

the maid brought him coffee and rolls. he return the vacant glance of 
the big-headed young man and acknowledge diffident the salute of the 
snuffy old gentlemen. he did not try to finish his coffee, and sat 
crumble a roll, unconscious of the sympathetic glance of madame 
marotte, who had tact enough not to bother him. 

presently a maid enter with a tray on which were balanced two bowls of 
chocolate, and the snuffy old gentlemen leer at her ankles. the maid 
deposit the chocolate at a table near the window and smile at hastings. 
then a thin young lady, follow by her counterpart in all except years, 
march into the room and took the table near the window. they were 
evidently american, but hastings, if he expect any sign of recognition, 
was disappointed. to be ignore by compatriot intensify his depression. 
he fumble with his knife and look at his plate. 

the thin young lady was talkative enough. she was quite aware of hastings' 
presence, ready to be flatter if he look at her, but on the other hand 
she felt her superiority, for she had been three week in paris and he, it 
was easy to see, had not yet unpack his steamer-trunk. 

her conversation was complacent. she argue with her mother upon the 
relative merit of the louvre and the bon marché, but her mother's part of 
the discussion was mostly confine to the observation, "why, susie!" 

the snuffy old gentlemen had left the room in a body, outwardly polite and 
inward raging. they could not endure the americans, who fill the room 
with their chatter. 

the big-headed young man look after them with a knowing cough, 
murmuring, "gay old birds!" 

"they look like bad old men, mr. bladen," said the girl. 

to this mr. bladen smile and said, "they've had their day," in a tone 
which imply that he was now have his. 

"and that's why they all have baggy eyes," cry the girl. "i think it's a 
shame for young gentlemen--" 

"why, susie!" said the mother, and the conversation lagged. 

after a while mr. bladen threw down the _petit journal_, which he daily 
studied at the expense of the house, and turning to hastings, start to 
make himself agreeable. he began by saying, "i see you are american." 

to this brilliant and original opening, hastings, deadly homesick, reply 
gratefully, and the conversation was judicious nourish by observations 
from miss susie byng distinct address to mr. bladen. in the course of 
event miss susie, forget to address herself exclusively to mr. 
bladen, and hastings reply to her general question, the _entente 
cordiale_ was established, and susie and her mother extend a 
protectorate over what was clearly neutral territory. 

"mr. hastings, you must not desert the pension every evening as mr. bladen 
does. paris is an awful place for young gentlemen, and mr. bladen is a 
horrid cynic." 

mr. bladen look gratified. 

hastings answered, "i shall be at the studio all day, and i imagine i 
shall be glad enough to come back at night." 

mr. bladen, who, at a salary of fifteen dollar a week, act as agent for 
the pew manufacture company of troy, n.y., smile a sceptical smile 
and withdrew to keep an appointment with a customer on the boulevard 
magenta. 

hastings walk into the garden with mrs. byng and susie, and, at their 
invitation, sat down in the shade before the iron gate. 

the chestnut tree still bore their fragrant spike of pink and white, and 
the bee hum among the roses, trellis on the white-walled house. 

a faint fresh was in the air. the water cart move up and down the 
street, and a clear stream bubble over the spotless gutter of the rue de 
la grande chaumière. the sparrow were merry along the curb-stones, taking 
bath after bath in the water and ruffle their feather with delight. in 
a wall garden across the street a pair of blackbird whistle among the 
almond trees. 

hastings swallow the lump in his throat, for the song of the bird and 
the ripple of water in a paris gutter brought back to him the sunny 
meadow of millbrook. 

"that's a blackbird," observe miss byng; "see him there on the bush with 
pink blossoms. he's all black except his bill, and that looks as if it had 
been dip in an omelet, as some frenchman says--" 

"why, susie!" said mrs. byng. 

"that garden belong to a studio inhabit by two americans," continue 
the girl serenely, "and i often see them pass. they seem to need a great 
many models, mostly young and feminine--" 

"why, susie!" 

"perhaps they prefer painting that kind, but i don't see why they should 
invite five, with three more young gentlemen, and all get into two cab 
and drive away singing. this street," she continued, "is dull. there is 
nothing to see except the garden and a glimpse of the boulevard 
montparnasse through the rue de la grande chaumière. no one ever pass 
except a policeman. there is a convent on the corner." 

"i thought it was a jesuit college," began hastings, but was at once 
overwhelm with a baedecker description of the place, ending with, "on 
one side stand the palatial hotel of jean paul lauren and guillaume 
bouguereau, and opposite, in the little passage stanislas, carolu duran 
paints the masterpiece which charm the world." 

the blackbird burst into a ripple of golden throaty notes, and from some 
distant green spot in the city an unknown wild-bird answer with a frenzy 
of liquid trill until the sparrow pause in their ablutions to look up 
with restless chirps. 

then a butterfly came and sat on a cluster of heliotrope and wave his 
crimson-banded wings in the hot sunshine. hastings knew him for a friend, 
and before his eyes there came a vision of tall mullein and scent 
milkweed alive with paint wings, a vision of a white house and 
woodbine-covered piazza,--a glimpse of a man reading and a woman leaning 
over the pansy bed,--and his heart was full. he was startle a moment 
later by miss byng. 

"i believe you are homesick!" hastings blushed. miss byng look at him 
with a sympathetic sigh and continued: "whenever i felt homesick at first 
i use to go with mamma and walk in the luxembourg gardens. i don't know 
why it is, but those old-fashioned garden seem to bring me near home 
than anything in this artificial city." 

"but they are full of marble statues," said mrs. byng mildly; "i don't see 
the resemblance myself." 

"where is the luxembourg?" inquire hastings after a silence. 

"come with me to the gate," said miss byng. he rose and follow her, and 
she pointed out the rue vavin at the foot of the street. 

"you pass by the convent to the right," she smiled; and hastings went. 




iii 

the luxembourg was a blaze of flowers. he walk slow through the long 
avenue of trees, past mossy marbles and old-time columns, and thread 
the grove by the bronze lion, came upon the tree-crowned terrace above the 
fountain. below lay the basin shining in the sunlight. flowering almond 
encircle the terrace, and, in a greater spiral, grove of chestnut wound 
in and out and down among the moist thicket by the western palace wing. 
at one end of the avenue of tree the observatory rose, its white dome 
pile up like an eastern mosque; at the other end stood the heavy palace, 
with every window-pane ablaze in the fierce sun of june. 

around the fountain, children and white-capped nurse armed with bamboo 
pole were push toy boats, whose sail hung limp in the sunshine. a 
dark policeman, wearing red epaulette and a dress sword, watch them for 
a while and then went away to remonstrate with a young man who had 
unchained his dog. the dog was pleasant occupy in rubbing grass and 
dirt into his back while his leg wave into the air. 

the policeman pointed at the dog. he was speechless with indignation. 

"well, captain," smile the young fellow. 

"well, monsieur student," growl the policeman. 

"what do you come and complain to me for?" 

"if you don't chain him i'll take him," shout the policeman. 

"what's that to me, mon capitaine?" 

"wha--t! isn't that bull-dog yours?" 

"if it was, don't you suppose i'd chain him?" 

the officer glare for a moment in silence, then decide that as he was a 
student he was wicked, grab at the dog, who prompt dodged. around and 
around the flower-beds they raced, and when the officer came too near for 
comfort, the bull-dog cut across a flower-bed, which perhaps was not 
play fair. 

the young man was amused, and the dog also seem to enjoy the exercise. 

the policeman notice this and decided to strike at the fountain-head of 
the evil. he storm up to the student and said, "as the owner of this 
public nuisance i arrest you!" 

"but," object the other, "i disclaim the dog." 

that was a poser. it was useless to attempt to catch the dog until three 
garden lent a hand, but then the dog simply ran away and disappear in 
the rue de medici. 

the policeman shamble off to find consolation among the white-capped 
nurses, and the student, look at his watch, stood up yawning. then 
catching sight of hastings, he smile and bowed. hastings walk over to 
the marble, laughing. 

"why, clifford," he said, "i didn't recognize you." 

"it's my moustache," sigh the other. "i sacrifice it to humour a whim 
of--of--a friend. what do you think of my dog?" 

"then he is yours?" cry hastings. 

"of course. it's a pleasant change for him, this play tag with 
policemen, but he is known now and i'll have to stop it. he's gone home. 
he always do when the garden take a hand. it's a pity; he's fond of 
rolling on lawns." then they chat for a moment of hastings' prospects, 
and clifford polite offer to stand his sponsor at the studio. 

"you see, old tabby, i mean dr. byram, told me about you before i met 
you," explain clifford, "and elliott and i will be glad to do anything 
we can." then look at his watch again, he muttered, "i have just ten 
minutes to catch the versaille train; au revoir," and start to go, but 
catching sight of a girl advance by the fountain, took off his hat with 
a confuse smile. 

"why are you not at versailles?" she said, with an almost imperceptible 
acknowledgment of hastings' presence. 

"i--i'm going," murmur clifford. 

for a moment they face each other, and then clifford, very red, 
stammered, "with your permission i have the honour of present to you my 
friend, monsieur hastings." 

hastings bowed low. she smile very sweetly, but there was something of 
malice in the quiet inclination of her small parisienne head. 

"i could have wished," she said, "that monsieur clifford might spare me 
more time when he bring with him so charming an american." 

"must--must i go, valentine?" began clifford. 

"certainly," she replied. 

clifford took his leave with very bad grace, wincing, when she added, "and 
give my dearest love to cécile!" as he disappear in the rue d'assas, the 
girl turn as if to go, but then sudden remember hastings, look at 
him and shook her head. 

"monsieur clifford is so perfectly harebrained," she smiled, "it is 
embarrass sometimes. you have heard, of course, all about his success 
at the salon?" 

he look puzzle and she notice it. 

"you have been to the salon, of course?" 

"why, no," he answered, "i only arrive in paris three days ago." 

she seem to pay little heed to his explanation, but continued: "nobody 
imagine he had the energy to do anything good, but on varnish day the 
salon was astonish by the entrance of monsieur clifford, who stroll 
about as bland as you please with an orchid in his buttonhole, and a 
beautiful picture on the line." 

she smile to herself at the reminiscence, and look at the fountain. 

"monsieur bouguereau told me that monsieur julian was so astonish that 
he only shook hand with monsieur clifford in a daze manner, and actually 
forgot to pat him on the back! fancy," she continue with much merriment, 
"fancy papa julian forget to pat one on the back." 

hastings, wonder at her acquaintance with the great bouguereau, look 
at her with respect. "may i ask," he said diffidently, "whether you are a 
pupil of bouguereau?" 

"i?" she said in some surprise. then she look at him curiously. was he 
permit himself the liberty of joke on such short acquaintance? 

his pleasant serious face question hers. 

"tiens," she thought, "what a droll man!" 

"you surely study art?" he said. 

she lean back on the crooked stick of her parasol, and look at him. 
"why do you think so?" 

"because you speak as if you did." 

"you are making fun of me," she said, "and it is not good taste." 

she stopped, confused, as he coloured to the roots of his hair. 

"how long have you been in paris?" she said at length. 

"three days," he reply gravely. 

"but--but--surely you are not a nouveau! you speak france too well!" 

then after a pause, "really are you a nouveau?" 

"i am," he said. 

she sat down on the marble bench lately occupy by clifford, and tilt 
her parasol over her small head look at him. 

"i don't believe it." 

he felt the compliment, and for a moment hesitate to declare himself one 
of the despised. then muster up his courage, he told her how new and 
green he was, and all with a frank which made her blue eyes open very 
wide and her lip part in the sweetest of smiles. 

"you have never seen a studio?" 

"never." 

"nor a model?" 

"no." 

"how funny," she said solemnly. then they both laughed. 

"and you," he said, "have seen studios?" 

"hundreds." 

"and models?" 

"millions." 

"and you know bouguereau?" 

"yes, and henner, and constant and laurens, and puvi de chavanne and 
dagnan and courtois, and--and all the rest of them!" 

"and yet you say you are not an artist." 

"pardon," she said gravely, "did i say i was not?" 

"won't you tell me?" he hesitated. 

at first she look at him, shake her head and smiling, then of a sudden 
her eyes fell and she began tracing figures with her parasol in the gravel 
at her feet. hastings had taken a place on the seat, and now, with his 
elbow on his knees, sat watch the spray drift above the fountain 
jet. a small boy, dress as a sailor, stood poke his yacht and crying, 
"i won't go home! i won't go home!" his nurse raise her hand to heaven. 

"just like a little america boy," thought hastings, and a pang of 
homesick shot through him. 

presently the nurse capture the boat, and the small boy stood at bay. 

"monsieur rené, when you decide to come here you may have your boat." 

the boy back away scowling. 

"give me my boat, i say," he cried, "and don't call me rené, for my 
name's randall and you know it!" 

"hello!" said hastings,--"randall?--that's english." 

"i am american," announce the boy in perfectly good english, turning to 
look at hastings, "and she's such a fool she call me rené because mamma 
call me ranny--" 

here he dodge the exasperate nurse and took up his station behind 
hastings, who laughed, and catching him around the waist lift him into 
his lap. 

"one of my countrymen," he said to the girl beside him. he smile while he 
spoke, but there was a queer feeling in his throat. 

"don't you see the stars and stripe on my yacht?" demand randall. sure 
enough, the america colours hung limp under the nurse's arm. 

"oh," cry the girl, "he is charming," and impulsive stoop to kiss 
him, but the infant randall wriggle out of hastings' arms, and his nurse 
pounce upon him with an angry glance at the girl. 

she redden and then bit her lip as the nurse, with eyes still fixed on 
her, drag the child away and ostentatious wipe his lip with her 
handkerchief. 

then she stole a look at hastings and bit her lip again. 

"what an ill-tempered woman!" he said. "in america, most nurse are 
flatter when people kiss their children." 

for an instant she tip the parasol to hide her face, then closed it 
with a snap and look at him defiantly. 

"do you think it strange that she objected?" 

"why not?" he said in surprise. 

again she look at him with quick searching eyes. 

his eyes were clear and bright, and he smile back, repeating, "why not?" 

"you _are_ droll," she murmured, bend her head. 

"why?" 

but she made no answer, and sat silent, tracing curve and circle in the 
dust with her parasol. after a while he said--"i am glad to see that young 
people have so much liberty here. i understood that the france were not at 
all like us. you know in america--or at least where i live in milbrook, 
girl have every liberty,--go out alone and receive their friends alone, 
and i was afraid i should miss it here. but i see how it is now, and i am 
glad i was mistaken." 

she raise her eyes to his and kept them there. 

he continue pleasantly--"since i have sat here i have seen a lot of 
pretty girl walking alone on the terrace there,--and then _you_ are alone 
too. tell me, for i do not know france customs,--do you have the liberty 
of go to the theatre without a chaperone?" 

for a long time she studied his face, and then with a tremble smile 
said, "why do you ask me?" 

"because you must know, of course," he said gaily. 

"yes," she reply indifferently, "i know." 

he wait for an answer, but get none, decided that perhaps she had 
misunderstood him. 

"i hope you don't think i mean to presume on our short acquaintance," he 
began,--"in fact it is very odd but i don't know your name. when mr. 
clifford present me he only mention mine. is that the custom in 
france?" 

"it is the custom in the latin quarter," she said with a queer light in 
her eyes. then sudden she began talk almost feverishly. 

"you must know, monsieur hastings, that we are all _un peu sans gêne_ here 
in the latin quarter. we are very bohemian, and etiquette and ceremony are 
out of place. it was for that monsieur clifford present you to me with 
small ceremony, and left us together with less,--only for that, and i am 
his friend, and i have many friends in the latin quarter, and we all know 
each other very well--and i am not study art, but--but--" 

"but what?" he said, bewildered. 

"i shall not tell you,--it is a secret," she said with an uncertain smile. 
on both cheek a pink spot was burning, and her eyes were very bright. 

then in a moment her face fell. "do you know monsieur clifford very 
intimately?" 

"not very." 

after a while she turn to him, grave and a little pale. 

"my name is valentine--valentine tissot. might--might i ask a service of 
you on such very short acquaintance?" 

"oh," he cried, "i should be honoured." 

"it is only this," she said gently, "it is not much. promise me not to 
speak to monsieur clifford about me. promise me that you will speak to no 
one about me." 

"i promise," he said, greatly puzzled. 

she laugh nervously. "i wish to remain a mystery. it is a caprice." 

"but," he began, "i had wished, i had hope that you might give monsieur 
clifford permission to bring me, to present me at your house." 

"my--my house!" she repeated. 

"i mean, where you live, in fact, to present me to your family." 

the change in the girl's face shock him. 

"i beg your pardon," he cried, "i have hurt you." 

and as quick as a flash she understood him because she was a woman. 

"my parent are dead," she said. 

presently he began again, very gently. 

"would it displease you if i beg you to receive me? it is the custom?" 

"i cannot," she answered. then glancing up at him, "i am sorry; i should 
like to; but believe me. i cannot." 

he bowed seriously and look vague uneasy. 

"it isn't because i don't wish to. i--i like you; you are very kind to 
me." 

"kind?" he cried, surprise and puzzled. 

"i like you," she said slowly, "and we will see each other sometimes if 
you will." 

"at friends' houses." 

"no, not at friends' houses." 

"where?" 

"here," she said with defiant eyes. 

"why," he cried, "in paris you are much more liberal in your view than we 
are." 

she look at him curiously. 

"yes, we are very bohemian." 

"i think it is charming," he declared. 

"you see, we shall be in the best of society," she venture timidly, with 
a pretty gesture toward the statue of the dead queens, range in stately 
ranks above the terrace. 

he look at her, delighted, and she brighten at the success of her 
innocent little pleasantry. 

"indeed," she smiled, "i shall be well chaperoned, because you see we are 
under the protection of the gods themselves; look, there are apollo, and 
juno, and venus, on their pedestals," count them on her small glove 
fingers, "and ceres, hercules, and--but i can't make out--" 

hastings turn to look up at the wing god under whose shadow they were 
seated. 

"why, it's love," he said. 




iv 

"there is a nouveau here," drawl laffat, leaning around his easel and 
address his friend bowles, "there is a nouveau here who is so tender 
and green and appetizing that heaven help him if he should fall into a 
salad bowl." 

"hayseed?" inquire bowles, plastering in a background with a broken 
palette-knife and squint at the effect with approval. 

"yes, squeedunk or oshkosh, and how he ever grew up among the daisy and 
escape the cows, heaven alone knows!" 

bowl rub his thumb across the outline of his study to "throw in a 
little atmosphere," as he said, glare at the model, pull at his pipe 
and finding it out struck a match on his neighbour's back to relight it. 

"his name," continue laffat, hurling a bit of bread at the hat-rack, "his 
name is hastings. he _is_ a berry. he know no more about the world,"--and 
here mr. laffat's face spoke volumes for his own knowledge of that 
planet,--"than a maiden cat on its first moonlight stroll." 

bowl now have succeed in lighting his pipe, repeated the thumb touch 
on the other edge of the study and said, "ah!" 

"yes," continue his friend, "and would you imagine it, he seem to think 
that everything here go on as it do in his d----d little backwoods 
ranch at home; talks about the pretty girl who walk alone in the street; 
say how sensible it is; and how france parent are misrepresent in 
america; say that for his part he find france girls,--and he confessed 
to only knowing one,--as jolly as america girls. i try to set him 
right, try to give him a pointer as to what sort of ladies walk about 
alone or with students, and he was either too stupid or too innocent to 
catch on. then i gave it to him straight, and he said i was a vile-minded 
fool and march off." 

"did you assist him with your shoe?" inquire bowles, languid 
interested. 

"well, no." 

"he call you a vile-minded fool." 

"he was correct," said clifford from his easel in front. 

"what--what do you mean?" demand laffat, turning red. 

"_that_," reply clifford. 

"who spoke to you? is this your business?" sneer bowles, but nearly lost 
his balance as clifford swung about and eye him. 

"yes," he said slowly, "it's my business." 

no one spoke for some time. 

then clifford sang out, "i say, hastings!" 

and when hastings left his easel and came around, he nod toward the 
astonish laffat. 

"this man has been disagreeable to you, and i want to tell you that any 
time you feel inclined to kick him, why, i will hold the other creature." 

hastings, embarrassed, said, "why no, i don't agree with his ideas, 
nothing more." 

clifford said "naturally," and slip his arm through hastings', 
stroll about with him, and introduce him to several of his own friends, 
at which all the nouveaux open their eyes with envy, and the studio were 
given to understand that hastings, although prepared to do menial work as 
the latest nouveau, was already within the charm circle of the old, 
respect and feared, the truly great. 

the rest finished, the model resume his place, and work went on in a 
chorus of song and yell and every ear-splitting noise which the art 
student utter when study the beautiful. 

five o'clock struck,--the model yawned, stretch and climb into his 
trousers, and the noisy contents of six studio crowded through the hall 
and down into the street. ten minutes later, hastings found himself on top 
of a montrouge tram, and shortly afterward was join by clifford. 

they climb down at the rue gay lussac. 

"i always stop here," observe clifford, "i like the walk through the 
luxembourg." 

"by the way," said hastings, "how can i call on you when i don't know 
where you live?" 

"why, i live opposite you." 

"what--the studio in the garden where the almond tree are and the 
blackbirds--" 

"exactly," said clifford. "i'm with my friend elliott." 

hastings thought of the description of the two america artist which he 
had heard from miss susie byng, and look blank. 

clifford continued, "perhaps you had better let me know when you think of 
coming so,--so that i will be sure to--to be there," he end rather 
lamely. 

"i shouldn't care to meet any of your model friends there," said hastings, 
smiling. "you know--my idea are rather straitlaced,--i suppose you would 
say, puritanical. i shouldn't enjoy it and wouldn't know how to behave." 

"oh, i understand," said clifford, but add with great cordiality,--"i'm 
sure we'll be friends although you may not approve of me and my set, but 
you will like severn and selby because--because, well, they are like 
yourself, old chap." 

after a moment he continued, "there is something i want to speak about. 
you see, when i introduce you, last week, in the luxembourg, to 
valentine--" 

"not a word!" cry hastings, smiling; "you must not tell me a word of 
her!" 

"why--" 

"no--not a word!" he said gaily. "i insist,--promise me upon your honour 
you will not speak of her until i give you permission; promise!" 

"i promise," said clifford, amazed. 

"she is a charming girl,--we had such a delightful chat after you left, 
and i thank you for present me, but not another word about her until i 
give you permission." 

"oh," murmur clifford. 

"remember your promise," he smiled, as he turn into his gateway. 

clifford stroll across the street and, traverse the ivy-covered alley, 
enter his garden. 

he felt for his studio key, muttering, "i wonder--i wonder,--but of course 
he doesn't!" 

he enter the hallway, and fitting the key into the door, stood staring 
at the two cards tack over the panels. 

foxhall clifford 

richard osborne elliott 

"why the devil doesn't he want me to speak of her?" 

he open the door, and, discourage the caress of two brindle 
bull-dogs, sank down on the sofa. 

elliott sat smoking and sketch with a piece of charcoal by the window. 

"hello," he said without look around. 

clifford gaze absently at the back of his head, murmuring, "i'm afraid, 
i'm afraid that man is too innocent. i say, elliott," he said, at last, 
"hastings,--you know the chap that old tabby byram came around here to 
tell us about--the day you had to hide colette in the armoire--" 

"yes, what's up?" 

"oh, nothing. he's a brick." 

"yes," said elliott, without enthusiasm. 

"don't you think so?" demand clifford. 

"why yes, but he is go to have a tough time when some of his illusion 
are dispelled." 

"more shame to those who dispel 'em!" 

"yes,--wait until he come to pay his call on us, unexpectedly, of 
course--" 

clifford look virtuous and light a cigar. 

"i was just go to say," he observed, "that i have ask him not to come 
without letting us know, so i can postpone any orgie you may have 
intended--" 

"ah!" cry elliott indignantly, "i suppose you put it to him in that 
way." 

"not exactly," grin clifford. then more seriously, "i don't want 
anything to occur here to bother him. he's a brick, and it's a pity we 
can't be more like him." 

"i am," observe elliott complacently, "only living with you--" 

"listen!" cry the other. "i have manage to put my foot in it in great 
style. do you know what i've done? well--the first time i met him in the 
street,--or rather, it was in the luxembourg, i introduce him to 
valentine!" 

"did he object?" 

"believe me," said clifford, solemnly, "this rustic hastings has no more 
idea that valentine is--is--in fact is valentine, than he has that he 
himself is a beautiful example of moral decency in a quarter where morals 
are as rare as elephants. i heard enough in a conversation between that 
blackguard loffat and the little immoral eruption, bowles, to open my 
eyes. i tell you hastings is a trump! he's a healthy, clean-minded young 
fellow, bred in a small country village, brought up with the idea that 
saloon are way-stations to hell--and as for women--" 

"well?" demand elliott 

"well," said clifford, "his idea of the dangerous woman is probably a 
paint jezabel." 

"probably," reply the other. 

"he's a trump!" said clifford, "and if he swear the world is as good and 
pure as his own heart, i'll swear he's right." 

elliott rub his charcoal on his file to get a point and turn to his 
sketch saying, "he will never hear any pessimism from richard osborne e." 

"he's a lesson to me," said clifford. then he unfold a small perfume 
note, written on rose-coloured paper, which had been lie on the table 
before him. 

he read it, smiled, whistle a bar or two from "miss helyett," and sat 
down to answer it on his best cream-laid note-paper. when it was written 
and sealed, he picked up his stick and march up and down the studio two 
or three times, whistling. 

"going out?" inquire the other, without turning. 

"yes," he said, but linger a moment over elliott's shoulder, watch 
him pick out the lights in his sketch with a bit of bread. 

"to-morrow is sunday," he observe after a moment's silence. 

"well?" inquire elliott. 

"have you seen colette?" 

"no, i will to-night. she and rowden and jacqueline are coming to 
boulant's. i suppose you and, cécile will be there?" 

"well, no," reply clifford. "cécile dine at home to-night, and i--i had 
an idea of go to mignon's." 

elliott look at him with disapproval. 

"you can make all the arrangement for la roche without me," he continued, 
avoid elliott's eyes. 

"what are you up to now?" 

"nothing," protest clifford. 

"don't tell me," reply his chum, with scorn; "fellows don't rush off to 
mignon's when the set dine at boulant's. who is it now?--but no, i won't 
ask that,--what's the use!" then he lift up his voice in complaint and 
beat upon the table with his pipe. "what's the use of ever try to keep 
track of you? what will cécile say,--oh, yes, what will she say? it's a 
pity you can't be constant two months, yes, by jove! and the quarter is 
indulgent, but you abuse its good nature and mine too!" 

presently he arose, and jam his hat on his head, march to the door. 

"heaven alone know why any one put up with your antics, but they all do 
and so do i. if i were cécile or any of the other pretty fool after whom 
you have toddle and will, in all human probabilities, continue to toddle, 
i say, if i were cécile i'd spank you! now i'm go to boulant's, and as 
usual i shall make excuse for you and arrange the affair, and i don't 
care a continental where you are going, but, by the skull of the studio 
skeleton! if you don't turn up to-morrow with your sketching-kit under one 
arm and cécile under the other,--if you don't turn up in good shape, i'm 
done with you, and the rest can think what they please. good-night." 

clifford said good-night with as pleasant a smile as he could muster, and 
then sat down with his eyes on the door. he took out his watch and gave 
elliott ten minutes to vanish, then rang the concierge's call, murmuring, 
"oh dear, oh dear, why the devil do i do it?" 

"alfred," he said, as that gimlet-eyed person answer the call, "make 
yourself clean and proper, alfred, and replace your sabot with a pair of 
shoes. then put on your best hat and take this letter to the big white 
house in the rue de dragon. there is no answer, _mon petit_ alfred." 

the concierge departed with a snort in which unwilling for the errand 
and affection for m. clifford were blended. then with great care the young 
fellow array himself in all the beauty of his and elliott's wardrobe. 
he took his time about it, and occasional interrupt his toilet to play 
his banjo or make pleasing diversion for the bull-dogs by gamble about 
on all fours. "i've got two hour before me," he thought, and borrow a 
pair of elliott's silken foot-gear, with which he and the dogs play ball 
until he decided to put them on. then he light a cigarette and inspect 
his dress-coat. when he had empty it of four handkerchiefs, a fan, and a 
pair of crumple glove as long as his arm, he decided it was not suit 
to add _éclat_ to his charm and cast about in his mind for a substitute. 
elliott was too thin, and, anyway, his coat were now under lock and key. 
rowden probably was as badly off as himself. hastings! hastings was the 
man! but when he threw on a smoking-jacket and saunter over to hastings' 
house, he was informed that he had been gone over an hour. 

"now, where in the name of all that's reasonable could he have gone!" 
mutter clifford, look down the street. 

the maid didn't know, so he bestow upon her a fascinating smile and 
lounge back to the studio. 

hastings was not far away. the luxembourg is within five minutes' walk of 
the rue notre dame des champs, and there he sat under the shadow of a 
wing god, and there he had sat for an hour, poke hole in the dust and 
watch the steps which lead from the northern terrace to the fountain. 
the sun hung, a purple globe, above the misty hill of meudon. long 
streamer of cloud touched with rose swept low on the western sky, and 
the dome of the distant invalid burn like an opal through the haze. 
behind the palace the smoke from a high chimney mount straight into the 
air, purple until it cross the sun, where it change to a bar of 
smoulder fire. high above the darken foliage of the chestnut the 
twin tower of st. sulpice rose, an ever-deepening silhouette. 

a sleepy blackbird was carol in some near thicket, and pigeon pass 
and repass with the whisper of soft wind in their wings. the light on 
the palace window had die away, and the dome of the pantheon swam aglow 
above the northern terrace, a fiery valhalla in the sky; while below in 
grim array, along the terrace ranged, the marble ranks of queens look 
out into the west. 

from the end of the long walk by the northern façade of the palace came 
the noise of omnibus and the cries of the street. hastings look at the 
palace clock. six, and as his own watch agree with it, he fell to poke 
hole in the gravel again. a constant stream of people pass between the 
odéon and the fountain. priest in black, with silver-buckled shoes; line 
soldiers, slouchy and rakish; neat girl without hat bearing milliners' 
boxes, student with black portfolio and high hats, student with bérets 
and big canes, nervous, quick-stepping officers, symphony in turquoise 
and silver; ponderous jangle cavalrymen all over dust, pastry cooks' 
boys skip along with utter disregard for the safety of the basket 
balanced on the impish head, and then the lean outcast, the shamble 
paris tramp, slouch with shoulder bent and little eye furtive 
scan the ground for smokers' refuse;--all these move in a steady 
stream across the fountain circle and out into the city by the odeon, 
whose long arcade were now beginning to flicker with gas-jets. the 
melancholy bell of st sulpice struck the hour and the clock-tower of the 
palace light up. then hurried steps sound across the gravel and 
hastings raise his head. 

"how late you are," he said, but his voice was hoarse and only his flushed 
face told how long had seem the waiting. 

she said, "i was kept--indeed, i was so much annoyed--and--and i may only 
stay a moment." 

she sat down beside him, casting a furtive glance over her shoulder at the 
god upon his pedestal. 

"what a nuisance, that intrude cupid still there?" 

"wings and arrow too," said hastings, unheede her motion to be seated. 

"wings," she murmured, "oh, yes--to fly away with when he's tired of his 
play. of course it was a man who conceive the idea of wings, otherwise 
cupid would have been insupportable." 

"do you think so?" 

"_ma foi_, it's what men think." 

"and women?" 

"oh," she said, with a toss of her small head, "i really forget what we 
were speak of." 

"we were speak of love," said hastings. 

"_i_ was not," said the girl. then look up at the marble god, "i don't 
care for this one at all. i don't believe he know how to shoot his 
arrows--no, indeed, he is a coward;--he creeps up like an assassin in the 
twilight. i don't approve of cowardice," she announced, and turn her 
back on the statue. 

"i think," said hastings quietly, "that he do shoot fairly--yes, and 
even give one warning." 

"is it your experience, monsieur hastings?" 

he look straight into her eyes and said, "he is warning me." 

"heed the warning then," she cried, with a nervous laugh. as she spoke she 
strip off her gloves, and then careful proceed to draw them on 
again. when this was accomplished she glance at the palace clock, saying, 
"oh dear, how late it is!" furl her umbrella, then unfurl it, and 
finally look at him. 

"no," he said, "i shall not heed his warning." 

"oh dear," she sigh again, "still talk about that tiresome statue!" 
then steal a glance at his face, "i suppose--i suppose you are in 
love." 

"i don't know," he muttered, "i suppose i am." 

she raise her head with a quick gesture. "you seem delight at the 
idea," she said, but bit her lip and tremble as his eyes met hers. then 
sudden fear came over her and she sprang up, staring into the gathering 
shadows. 

"are you cold?" he said. 

but she only answered, "oh dear, oh dear, it is late--so late! i must 
go--good-night." 

she gave him her glove hand a moment and then withdrew it with a start. 

"what is it?" he insisted. "are you frightened?" 

she look at him strangely. 

"no--no--not frightened,--you are very good to me--" 

"by jove!" he burst out, "what do you mean by saying i'm good to you? 
that's at least the third time, and i don't understand!" 

the sound of a drum from the guard-house at the palace cut him short. 
"listen," she whispered, "they are go to close. it's late, oh, so 
late!" 

the rolling of the drum came near and nearer, and then the silhouette of 
the drummer cut the sky above the eastern terrace. the fade light 
linger a moment on his belt and bayonet, then he pass into the 
shadows, drum the echo awake. the roll became faint along the 
eastern terrace, then grew and grew and rattle with increase sharp 
when he pass the avenue by the bronze lion and turn down the western 
terrace walk. loud and loud the drum sounded, and the echo struck 
back the note from the grey palace wall; and now the drummer loom up 
before them--his red trousers a dull spot in the gathering gloom, the 
brass of his drum and bayonet touched with a pale spark, his epaulette 
toss on his shoulders. he pass leave the crash of the drum in their 
ears, and far into the alley of tree they saw his little tin cup shining 
on his haversack. then the sentinel began the monotonous cry: "on ferme! 
on ferme!" and the bugle blew from the barracks in the rue de tournon. 

"on ferme! on ferme!" 

"good-night," she whispered, "i must return alone to-night." 

he watch her until she reach the northern terrace, and then sat down 
on the marble seat until a hand on his shoulder and a glimmer of bayonet 
warn him away. 

she pass on through the grove, and turning into the rue de medici, 
traverse it to the boulevard. at the corner she bought a bunch of violet 
and walk on along the boulevard to the rue des Écoles. a cab was drawn 
up before boulant's, and a pretty girl aid by elliott jump out. 

"valentine!" cry the girl, "come with us!" 

"i can't," she said, stopping a moment--"i have a rendezvous at mignon's." 

"not victor?" cry the girl, laughing, but she pass with a little 
shiver, nod good-night, then turning into the boulevard st. germain, 
she walk a tittle fast to escape a gay party sitting before the café 
cluny who call to her to join them. at the door of the restaurant mignon 
stood a coal-black negro in buttons. he took off his peaked cap as she 
mount the carpet stairs. 

"send eugene to me," she said at the office, and passing through the 
hallway to the right of the dining-room stop before a row of panel 
doors. a waiter pass and she repeated her demand for eugene, who 
presently appeared, noiseless skipping, and bowed murmuring, "madame." 

"who is here?" 

"no one in the cabinets, madame; in the half madame madelon and monsieur 
gay, monsieur de clamart, monsieur clisson, madame marie and their set." 
then he look around and bowing again murmured, "monsieur await madame 
since half an hour," and he knock at one of the panel door bearing 
the number six. 

clifford open the door and the girl entered. 

the garçon bowed her in, and whispering, "will monsieur have the goodness 
to ring?" vanished. 

he help her off with her jacket and took her hat and umbrella. when she 
was seat at the little table with clifford opposite she smile and 
lean forward on both elbow look him in the face. 

"what are you do here?" she demanded. 

"waiting," he replied, in accent of adoration. 

for an instant she turn and examine herself in the glass. the wide blue 
eyes, the curling hair, the straight nose and short curl lip flash in 
the mirror an instant only, and then its depths reflect her pretty neck 
and back. "thus do i turn my back on vanity," she said, and then leaning 
forward again, "what are you do here?" 

"waiting for you," repeated clifford, slightly troubled. 

"and cécile." 

"now don't, valentine--" 

"do you know," she said calmly, "i dislike your conduct?" 

he was a little disconcerted, and rang for eugene to cover his confusion. 

the soup was bisque, and the wine pommery, and the course follow each 
other with the usual regularity until eugene brought coffee, and there was 
nothing left on the table but a small silver lamp. 

"valentine," said clifford, after have obtain permission to smoke, "is 
it the vaudeville or the eldorado--or both, or the nouveau cirque, or--" 

"it is here," said valentine. 

"well," he said, greatly flattered, "i'm afraid i couldn't amuse you--" 

"oh, yes, you are funny than the eldorado." 

"now see here, don't guy me, valentine. you always do, and, and,--you know 
what they say,--a good laugh kills--" 

"what?" 

"er--er--love and all that." 

she laugh until her eyes were moist with tears. "tiens," she cried, "he 
is dead, then!" 

clifford eye her with grow alarm. 

"do you know why i came?" she said. 

"no," he reply uneasily, "i don't." 

"how long have you made love to me?" 

"well," he admitted, somewhat startled,--"i should say,--for about a 
year." 

"it is a year, i think. are you not tired?" 

he did not answer. 

"don't you know that i like you too well to--to ever fall in love with 
you?" she said. "don't you know that we are too good comrades,--too old 
friends for that? and were we not,--do you think that i do not know your 
history, monsieur clifford?" 

"don't be--don't be so sarcastic," he urged; "don't be unkind, valentine." 

"i'm not. i'm kind. i'm very kind,--to you and to cécile." 

"cécile is tired of me." 

"i hope she is," said the girl, "for she deserve a better fate. tiens, do 
you know your reputation in the quarter? of the inconstant, the most 
inconstant,--utterly incorrigible and no more serious than a gnat on a 
summer night. poor cécile!" 

clifford look so uncomfortable that she spoke more kindly. 

"i like you. you know that. everybody does. you are a spoil child here. 
everything is permit you and every one make allowance, but every one 
cannot be a victim to caprice." 

"caprice!" he cried. "by jove, if the girl of the latin quarter are not 
capricious--" 

"never mind,--never mind about that! you must not sit in judgment--you of 
all men. why are you here to-night? oh," she cried, "i will tell you why! 
monsieur receive a little note; he send a little answer; he dress in 
his conquer raiment--" 

"i don't," said clifford, very red. 

"you do, and it become you," she retort with a faint smile. then again, 
very quietly, "i am in your power, but i know i am in the power of a 
friend. i have come to acknowledge it to you here,--and it is because of 
that that i am here to beg of you--a--a favour." 

clifford open his eyes, but said nothing. 

"i am in--great distress of mind. it is monsieur hastings." 

"well?" said clifford, in some astonishment. 

"i want to ask you," she continue in a low voice, "i want to ask you 
to--to--in case you should speak of me before him,--not to say,--not to 
say,--" 

"i shall not speak of you to him," he said quietly. 

"can--can you prevent others?" 

"i might if i was present. may i ask why?" 

"that is not fair," she murmured; "you know how--how he consider me,--as 
he consider every woman. you know how different he is from you and the 
rest. i have never seen a man,--such a man as monsieur hastings." 

he let his cigarette go out unnoticed. 

"i am almost afraid of him--afraid he should know--what we all are in the 
quarter. oh, i do not wish him to know! i do not wish him to--to turn from 
me--to cease from speak to me as he does! you--you and the rest cannot 
know what it has been to me. i could not believe him,--i could not believe 
he was so good and--and noble. i do not wish him to know--so soon. he will 
find out--sooner or later, he will find out for himself, and then he will 
turn away from me. why!" she cry passionately, "why should he turn from 
me and not from _you_?" 

clifford, much embarrassed, eye his cigarette. 

the girl rose, very white. "he is your friend--you have a right to warn 
him." 

"he is my friend," he said at length. 

they look at each other in silence. 

then she cried, "by all that i hold to me most sacred, you need not warn 
him!" 

"i shall trust your word," he said pleasantly.

the month pass quick for hastings, and left few definite impression 
after it. it did leave some, however. one was a painful impression of 
meeting mr. bladen on the boulevard des capucine in company with a very 
pronounced young person whose laugh dismay him, and when at last he 
escape from the café where mr. bladen had haul him to join them in a 
_bock_ he felt as if the whole boulevard was look at him, and judge 
him by his company. later, an instinctive conviction regarding the young 
person with mr. bladen sent the hot blood into his cheek, and he return 
to the pension in such a miserable state of mind that miss byng was 
alarm and advise him to conquer his homesick at once. 

another impression was equally vivid. one saturday morning, feeling 
lonely, his wanderings about the city brought him to the gare st. lazare. 
it was early for breakfast, but he enter the hôtel terminus and took a 
table near the window. as he wheel about to give his order, a man 
passing rapid along the aisle collide with his head, and look up to 
receive the expect apology, he was met instead by a slap on the shoulder 
and a hearty, "what the deuce are you do here, old chap?" it was 
rowden, who seize him and told him to come along. so, mildly protesting, 
he was usher into a private dining-room where clifford, rather red, 
jump up from the table and welcome him with a startle air which was 
soften by the unaffected glee of rowden and the extreme courtesy of 
elliott. the latter present him to three bewitch girl who welcome 
him so charming and second rowden in his demand that hastings should 
make one of the party, that he consent at once. while elliott brief 
outline the project excursion to la roche, hastings delighted ate his 
omelet, and return the smile of encouragement from cécile and colette 
and jacqueline. meantime clifford in a bland whisper was telling rowden 
what an ass he was. poor rowden look miserable until elliott, divine 
how affair were turning, frown on clifford and found a moment to let 
rowden know that they were all go to make the best of it. 

"you shut up," he observe to clifford, "it's fate, and that settle it." 

"it's rowden, and that settle it," murmur clifford, conceal a grin. 
for after all he was not hastings' wet nurse. so it came about that the 
train which left the gare st. lazare at 9.15 a.m. stop a moment in its 
career towards havre and deposit at the red-roofed station of la roche a 
merry party, armed with sunshades, trout-rods, and one cane, carry by 
the non-combatant, hastings. then, when they had establish their camp in 
a grove of sycamore which border the little river ept, clifford, the 
acknowledge master of all that pertain to sportsmanship, took command. 

"you, rowden," he said, "divide your flies with elliott and keep an eye on 
him or else he'll be try to put on a float and sinker. prevent him by 
force from grub about for worms." 

elliott protested, but was forced to smile in the general laugh. 

"you make me ill," he asserted; "do you think this is my first trout?" 

"i shall be delight to see your first trout," said clifford, and dodge 
a fly hook, hurl with intent to hit, proceed to sort and equip three 
slender rod destined to bring joy and fish to cécil, colette, and 
jacqueline. with perfect gravity he ornament each line with four split 
shot, a small hook, and a brilliant quill float. 

"_i_ shall never touch the worms," announce cécile with a shudder. 

jacqueline and colette hasten to sustain her, and hastings pleasant 
offer to act in the capacity of general bait and taker-off of fish. 
but cécile, doubtless fascinate by the gaudy flies in clifford's book, 
decided to accept lesson from him in the true art, and presently 
disappear up the ept with clifford in tow. 

elliott look doubtful at colette. 

"i prefer gudgeons," said that damsel with decision, "and you and monsieur 
rowden may go away when you please; may they not, jacqueline?" 

"certainly," respond jacqueline. 

elliott, undecided, examine his rod and reel. 

"you've got your reel on wrong side up," observe rowden. 

elliott wavered, and stole a glance at colette. 

"i--i--have almost decided to--er--not to flip the flies about just now," 
he began. "there's the pole that cécile left--" 

"don't call it a pole," correct rowden. 

"_rod_, then," continue elliott, and start off in the wake of the two 
girls, but was prompt collar by rowden. 

"no, you don't! fancy a man fishing with a float and sinker when he has a 
fly rod in his hand! you come along!" 

where the placid little ept flow down between its thicket to the seine, 
a grassy bank shadow the haunt of the gudgeon, and on this bank sat 
colette and jacqueline and chatter and laugh and watch the swerve 
of the scarlet quills, while hastings, his hat over his eyes, his head on 
a bank of moss, listen to their soft voice and gallant unhook the 
small and indignant gudgeon when a flash of a rod and a half-suppressed 
scream announce a catch. the sunlight filter through the leafy thicket 
awake to song the forest birds. magpie in spotless black and white 
flirt past, alight near by with a hop and bound and twitch of the 
tail. blue and white jay with rosy breast shriek through the trees, 
and a low-sailing hawk wheel among the field of ripen wheat, putt 
to flight flock of twitter hedge birds. 

across the seine a gull drop on the water like a plume. the air was 
pure and still. scarcely a leaf moved. sound from a distant farm came 
faintly, the shrill cock-crow and dull baying. now and then a steam-tug 
with big rake smoke-pipe, bearing the name "guêpe 27," plough up the 
river drag its interminable train of barges, or a sailboat drop 
down with the current toward sleepy rouen. 

a faint fresh odour of earth and water hung in the air, and through the 
sunlight, orange-tipped butterfly dance above the marsh grass, soft 
velvety butterfly flap through the mossy woods. 

hastings was thinking of valentine. it was two o'clock when elliott 
stroll back, and frankly admit that he had elude rowden, sat down 
beside colette and prepared to doze with satisfaction. 

"where are your trout?" said colette severely. 

"they still live," murmur elliott, and went fast asleep. 

rowden return shortly after, and casting a scornful glance at the 
slumber one, display three crimson-flecked trout. 

"and that," smile hastings lazily, "that is the holy end to which the 
faithful plod,--the slaughter of these small fish with a bit of silk and 
feather." 

rowden disdain to answer him. colette caught another gudgeon and awoke 
elliott, who protest and gaze about for the lunch baskets, as clifford 
and cécile came up demanding instant refreshment. cécile's skirt were 
soaked, and her glove torn, but she was happy, and clifford, drag out 
a two-pound trout, stood still to receive the applause of the company. 

"where the deuce did you get that?" demand elliott. 

cécile, wet and enthusiastic, recount the battle, and then clifford 
eulogize her powers with the fly, and, in proof, produce from his creel 
a defunct chub, which, he observed, just miss being a trout. 

they were all very happy at luncheon, and hastings was vote "charming." 
he enjoy it immensely,--only it seem to him at moments that flirtation 
went further in france than in millbrook, connecticut, and he thought that 
cécile might be a little less enthusiast about clifford, that perhaps it 
would be quite as well if jacqueline sat further away from rowden, and 
that possibly colette could have, for a moment at least, taken her eyes 
from elliott's face. still he enjoy it--except when his thought drift 
to valentine, and then he felt that he was very far away from her. la 
roche is at least an hour and a half from paris. it is also true that he 
felt a happiness, a quick heart-beat when, at eight o'clock that night the 
train which bore them from la roche roll into the gare st. lazare and he 
was once more in the city of valentine. 

"good-night," they said, pressing around him. "you must come with us next 
time!" 

he promised, and watch them, two by two, drift into the darken city, 
and stood so long that, when again he raise his eyes, the vast boulevard 
was twinkling with gas-jets through which the electric lights stare like 
moons. 




vi 

it was with another quick heart-beat that he awoke next morning, for his 
first thought was of valentine. 

the sun already gilded the tower of notre dame, the clatter of workmen's 
sabot awoke sharp echo in the street below, and across the way a 
blackbird in a pink almond tree was go into an ecstasy of trills. 

he determined to awake clifford for a brisk walk in the country, hope 
later to beguile that gentleman into the america church for his soul's 
sake. he found alfred the gimlet-eyed washing the asphalt walk which led 
to the studio. 

"monsieur elliott?" he reply to the perfunctory inquiry, "_je ne sai 
pas_." 

"and monsieur clifford," began hastings, somewhat astonished. 

"monsieur clifford," said the concierge with fine irony, "will be pleased 
to see you, as he retired early; in fact he has just come in." 

hastings hesitate while the concierge pronounced a fine eulogy on people 
who never stay out all night and then came batter at the lodge gate 
during hour which even a gendarme held sacred to sleep. he also 
discourse eloquent upon the beauty of temperance, and took an 
ostentatious draught from the fountain in the court. 

"i do not think i will come in," said hastings. 

"pardon, monsieur," growl the concierge, "perhaps it would be well to 
see monsieur clifford. he possibly needs aid. me he drive forth with 
hair-brushes and boots. it is a mercy if he has not set fire to something 
with his candle." 

hastings hesitate for an instant, but swallow his dislike of such a 
mission, walk slow through the ivy-covered alley and across the inner 
garden to the studio. he knocked. perfect silence. then he knock again, 
and this time something struck the door from within with a crash. 

"that," said the concierge, "was a boot." he fitted his duplicate key into 
the lock and usher hastings in. clifford, in disorder evening dress, 
sat on the rug in the middle of the room. he held in his hand a shoe, and 
did not appear astonish to see hastings. 

"good-morning, do you use pears' soap?" he inquire with a vague wave of 
his hand and a vague smile. 

hastings' heart sank. "for heaven's sake," he said, "clifford, go to bed." 

"not while that--that alfred poke his shaggy head in here an' i have a 
shoe left." 

hastings blew out the candle, picked up clifford's hat and cane, and said, 
with an emotion he could not conceal, "this is terrible, 
clifford,--i--never knew you did this sort of thing." 

"well, i do," said clifford. 

"where is elliott?" 

"ole chap," return clifford, becoming maudlin, "providence which 
feeds--feeds--er--sparrows an' that sort of thing watcheth over the 
intemperate wanderer--" 

"where is elliott?" 

but clifford only wag his head and wave his arm about. "he's out 
there,--somewhere about." then sudden feeling a desire to see his 
missing chum, lift up his voice and howl for him. 

hastings, thorough shocked, sat down on the lounge without a word. 
presently, after shed several scalding tears, clifford brighten up 
and rose with great precaution. 

"ole chap," he observed, "do you want to see er--er miracle? well, here 
goes. i'm goin' to begin." 

he paused, beam at vacancy. 

"er miracle," he repeated. 

hastings supposed he was allude to the miracle of his keeping his 
balance, and said nothing. 

"i'm goin' to bed," he announced, "poor ole clifford's goin' to bed, an' 
that's er miracle!" 

and he did with a nice calculation of distance and equilibrium which would 
have rung enthusiast yell of applause from elliott had he been there to 
assist _en connaisseur_. but he was not. he had not yet reach the 
studio. he was on his way, however, and smile with magnificent 
condescension on hastings, who, half an hour later, found him recline 
upon a bench in the luxembourg. he permit himself to be aroused, dust 
and escort to the gate. here, however, he refuse all further 
assistance, and bestow a patronize bow upon hastings, steer a 
tolerably true course for the rue vavin. 

hastings watch him out of sight, and then slow retrace his steps 
toward the fountain. at first he felt gloomy and depressed, but gradual 
the clear air of the morning lift the pressure from his heart, and he 
sat down on the marble seat under the shadow of the wing god. 

the air was fresh and sweet with perfume from the orange flowers. 
everywhere pigeon were bathing, dashing the water over their iris-hued 
breasts, flash in and out of the spray or nestling almost to the neck 
along the polish basin. the sparrows, too, were abroad in force, soaking 
their dust-coloured feather in the limpid pool and chirp with might 
and main. under the sycamore which surround the duck-pond opposite the 
fountain of marie de medici, the water-fowl crop the herbage, or 
waddle in row down the bank to embark on some solemn aimless cruise. 

butterflies, somewhat lame from a chilly night's repose under the lilac 
leaves, crawl over and over the white phlox, or took a rheumatic flight 
toward some sun-warmed shrub. the bee were already busy among the 
heliotrope, and one or two grey flies with brick-coloured eyes sat in a 
spot of sunlight beside the marble seat, or chase each other about, only 
to return again to the spot of sunshine and rub their fore-legs, exulting. 

the sentry pace brisk before the paint boxes, pause at times to 
look toward the guard-house for their relief. 

they came at last, with a shuffle of feet and click of bayonets, the word 
was passed, the relief fell out, and away they went, crunch, crunch, 
across the gravel. 

a mellow chime float from the clock-tower of the palace, the deep bell 
of st. sulpice echo the stroke. hastings sat dream in the shadow of 
the god, and while he muse somebody came and sat down beside him. at 
first he did not raise his head. it was only when she spoke that he sprang 
up. 

"you! at this hour?" 

"i was restless, i could not sleep." then in a low, happy voice--"and 
_you!_ at this hour?" 

"i--i slept, but the sun awoke me." 

"_i_ could not sleep," she said, and her eyes seemed, for a moment, 
touched with an indefinable shadow. then, smiling, "i am so glad--i seem 
to know you were coming. don't laugh, i believe in dreams." 

"did you really dream of,--of my being here?" 

"i think i was awake when i dream it," she admitted. then for a time 
they were mute, acknowledge by silence the happiness of being together. 
and after all their silence was eloquent, for faint smiles, and glance 
born of their thoughts, cross and recrossed, until lip move and words 
were formed, which seem almost superfluous. what they said was not very 
profound. perhaps the most valuable jewel that fell from hastings' lip 
bore direct reference to breakfast. 

"i have not yet had my chocolate," she confessed, "but what a material man 
you are." 

"valentine," he said impulsively, "i wish,--i do wish that you 
would,--just for this once,--give me the whole day,--just for this once." 

"oh dear," she smiled, "not only material, but selfish!" 

"not selfish, hungry," he said, look at her. 

"a cannibal too; oh dear!" 

"will you, valentine?" 

"but my chocolate--" 

"take it with me." 

"but _déjeuner_--" 

"together, at st. cloud." 

"but i can't--" 

"together,--all day,--all day long; will you, valentine?" 

she was silent. 

"only for this once." 

again that indefinable shadow fell across her eyes, and when it was gone 
she sighed. "yes,--together, only for this once." 

"all day?" he said, doubt his happiness. 

"all day," she smiled; "and oh, i am so hungry!" 

he laughed, enchanted. 

"what a material young lady it is." 

on the boulevard st. michel there is a crémerie paint white and blue 
outside, and neat and clean as a whistle inside. the auburn-haired young 
woman who speak france like a native, and rejoice in the name of murphy, 
smile at them as they entered, and toss a fresh napkin over the zinc 
_tête-à-tête_ table, whisk before them two cup of chocolate and a 
basket full of crisp, fresh croissons. 

the primrose-coloured pat of butter, each stamp with a shamrock in 
relief, seem saturate with the fragrance of normandy pastures. 

"how delicious!" they said in the same breath, and then laugh at the 
coincidence. 

"with but a single thought," he began. 

"how absurd!" she cry with cheek all rosy. "i'm thinking i'd like a 
croisson." 

"so am i," he reply triumphant, "that prove it." 

then they had a quarrel; she accuse him of behaviour unworthy of a child 
in arms, and he deny it, and bring counter charges, until 
mademoiselle murphy laugh in sympathy, and the last croisson was eaten 
under a flag of truce. then they rose, and she took his arm with a bright 
nod to mile. murphy, who cry them a merry: "_bonjour, madame! bonjour, 
monsieur_!" and watch them hail a passing cab and drive away. "_dieu! 
qu'il est beau_," she sighed, add after a moment, "do they be married, 
i dunno,--_ma foi ils ont bien l'air_." 

the cab swung around the rue de medici, turn into the rue de vaugirard, 
follow it to where it cross the rue de rennes, and taking that noisy 
thoroughfare, drew up before the gare montparnasse. they were just in time 
for a train and scamper up the stairway and out to the car as the last 
note from the starting-gong rang through the arch station. the guard 
slam the door of their compartment, a whistle sounded, answer by a 
screech from the locomotive, and the long train glide from the station, 
faster, faster, and sped out into the morning sunshine. the summer wind 
blew in their face from the open window, and sent the soft hair dance 
on the girl's forehead. 

"we have the compartment to ourselves," said hastings. 

she lean against the cushion window-seat, her eyes bright and wide 
open, her lip parted. the wind lift her hat, and flutter the ribbon 
under her chin. with a quick movement she untie them, and, drawing a long 
hat-pin from her hat, laid it down on the seat beside her. the train was 
flying. 

the colour surge in her cheeks, and, with each quick-drawn breath, her 
breath rose and fell under the cluster of lily at her throat. trees, 
houses, ponds, dance past, cut by a mist of telegraph poles. 

"faster! faster!" she cried. 

his eyes never left her, but hers, wide open, and blue as the summer sky, 
seem fixed on something far ahead,--something which came no nearer, but 
flee before them as they fled. 

was it the horizon, cut now by the grim fortress on the hill, now by the 
cross of a country chapel? was it the summer moon, ghost-like, slip 
through the vague blue above? 

"faster! faster!" she cried. 

her part lip burn scarlet. 

the car shook and shivered, and the field stream by like an emerald 
torrent. he caught the excitement, and his face glowed. 

"oh," she cried, and with an unconscious movement caught his hand, drawing 
him to the window beside her. "look! lean out with me!" 

he only saw her lip move; her voice was drown in the roar of a trestle, 
but his hand closed in hers and he clung to the sill. the wind whistle in 
their ears. "not so far out, valentine, take care!" he gasped. 

below, through the tie of the trestle, a broad river flash into view 
and out again, as the train thunder along a tunnel, and away once more 
through the freshest of green fields. the wind roar about them. the girl 
was leaning far out from the window, and he caught her by the waist, 
crying, "not too far!" but she only murmured, "faster! faster! away out of 
the city, out of the land, faster, faster! away out of the world!" 

"what are you saying all to yourself?" he said, but his voice was broken, 
and the wind whirl it back into his throat. 

she heard him, and, turning from the window look down at his arm about 
her. then she raise her eyes to his. the car shook and the window 
rattled. they were dashing through a forest now, and the sun swept the 
dewy branch with running flash of fire. he look into her trouble 
eyes; he drew her to him and kiss the half-parted lips, and she cry 
out, a bitter, hopeless cry, "not that--not that!" 

but he held her close and strong, whisper words of honest love and 
passion, and when she sobbed--"not that--not that--i have promised! you 
must--you must know--i am--not--worthy--" in the purity of his own heart 
her words were, to him, meaningless then, meaningless for ever after. 
presently her voice ceased, and her head rest on his breast. he lean 
against the window, his ear swept by the furious wind, his heart in a 
joyous tumult. the forest was passed, and the sun slip from behind the 
trees, flood the earth again with brightness. she raise her eyes and 
look out into the world from the window. then she began to speak, but 
her voice was faint, and he bent his head close to hers and listened. "i 
cannot turn from you; i am too weak. you were long ago my master--master 
of my heart and soul. i have broken my word to one who trust me, but i 
have told you all;--what matter the rest?" he smile at her innocence and 
she worship his. she spoke again: "take me or cast me away;--what 
matter it? now with a word you can kill me, and it might be easy to die 
than to look upon happiness as great as mine." 

he took her in his arms, "hush, what are you saying? look,--look out at 
the sunlight, the meadow and the streams. we shall be very happy in so 
bright a world." 

she turn to the sunlight. from the window, the world below seem very 
fair to her. 

tremble with happiness, she sighed: "is this the world? then i have 
never known it." 

"nor have i, god forgive me," he murmured. 

perhaps it was our gentle lady of the field who forgave them both.
