one morning at julian's, a student said to selby, "that is foxhall 
clifford," point with his brush at a young man who sat before an 
easel, do nothing. 

selby, shy and nervous, walk over and began: "my name is selby,--i have 
just arrive in paris, and bring a letter of introduction--" his voice was 
lost in the crash of a fall easel, the owner of which prompt 
assault his neighbour, and for a time the noise of battle roll through 
the studio of mm. boulanger and lefebvre, presently subside into a 
scuffle on the stairs outside. selby, apprehensive as to his own reception 
in the studio, look at clifford, who sat serene watch the fight. 

"it's a little noisy here," said clifford, "but you will like the fellow 
when you know them." his unaffected manner delight selby. then with a 
simplicity that won his heart, he present him to half a dozen student 
of as many nationalities. some were cordial, all were polite. even the 
majestic creature who held the position of massier, unbent enough to say: 
"my friend, when a man speak france as well as you do, and is also a 
friend of monsieur clifford, he will have no trouble in this studio. you 
expect, of course, to fill the stove until the next new man comes?" 

"of course." 

"and you don't mind chaff?" 

"no," reply selby, who hate it. 

clifford, much amused, put on his hat, saying, "you must expect lot of it 
at first." 

selby placed his own hat on his head and follow him to the door. 

as they pass the model stand there was a furious cry of "chapeau! 
chapeau!" and a student sprang from his easel menace selby, who redden 
but look at clifford. 

"take off your hat for them," said the latter, laughing. 

a little embarrassed, he turn and salute the studio. 

"et moi?" cry the model. 

"you are charming," reply selby, astonish at his own audacity, but the 
studio rose as one man, shouting: "he has done well! he's all right!" 
while the model, laughing, kiss her hand to him and cried: "À demain 
beau jeune homme!" 

all that week selby work at the studio unmolested. the france student 
christen him "l'enfant prodigue," which was freely translated, "the 
prodigious infant," "the kid," "kid selby," and "kidby." but the disease 
soon ran its course from "kidby" to "kidney," and then naturally to 
"tidbits," where it was arrest by clifford's authority and ultimately 
relapse to "kid." 

wednesday came, and with it m. boulanger. for three hour the student 
writhe under his biting sarcasms,--among the other clifford, who was 
informed that he knew even less about a work of art than he did about the 
art of work. selby was more fortunate. the professor examine his drawing 
in silence, look at him sharply, and pass on with a non-committal 
gesture. he presently departed arm in arm with bouguereau, to the relief 
of clifford, who was then at liberty to jam his hat on his head and 
depart. 

the next day he did not appear, and selby, who had count on seeing him 
at the studio, a thing which he learned later it was vanity to count on, 
wander back to the latin quarter alone. 

paris was still strange and new to him. he was vague trouble by its 
splendour. no tender memory stir his america bosom at the place du 
châtelet, nor even by notre dame. the palais de justice with its clock and 
turret and stalk sentinel in blue and vermilion, the place st. michel 
with its jumble of omnibus and ugly water-spitting griffins, the hill of 
the boulevard st. michel, the toot trams, the policemen dawdle two by 
two, and the table-lined terrace of the café vacehett were nothing to 
him, as yet, nor did he even know, when he step from the stone of the 
place st. michel to the asphalt of the boulevard, that he had cross the 
frontier and enter the student zone,--the famous latin quarter. 

a cabman hail him as "bourgeois," and urge the superior of driving 
over walking. a gamin, with an appearance of great concern, request the 
latest telegraphic news from london, and then, standing on his head, 
invite selby to feat of strength. a pretty girl gave him a glance from a 
pair of violet eyes. he did not see her, but she, catching her own 
reflection in a window, wonder at the colour burning in her cheeks. 
turning to resume her course, she met foxhall clifford, and hurried on. 
clifford, open-mouthed, follow her with his eyes; then he look after 
selby, who had turn into the boulevard st. germain toward the rue de 
seine. then he examine himself in the shop window. the result seem to 
be unsatisfactory. 

"i'm not a beauty," he mused, "but neither am i a hobgoblin. what do she 
mean by blush at selby? i never before saw her look at a fellow in my 
life,--neither has any one in the quarter. anyway, i can swear she never 
looks at me, and goodness know i have done all that respectful adoration 
can do." 

he sighed, and murmur a prophecy concerning the salvation of his 
immortal soul swung into that graceful lounge which at all times 
characterize clifford. with no apparent exertion, he overtook selby at 
the corner, and together they cross the sunlit boulevard and sat down 
under the awning of the café du cercle. clifford bowed to everybody on the 
terrace, saying, "you shall meet them all later, but now let me present 
you to two of the sight of paris, mr. richard elliott and mr. stanley 
rowden." 

the "sights" look amiable, and took vermouth. 

"you cut the studio to-day," said elliott, sudden turning on clifford, 
who avoid his eyes. 

"to commune with nature?" observe rowden. 

"what's her name this time?" ask elliott, and rowden answer promptly, 
"name, yvette; nationality, breton--" 

"wrong," reply clifford blandly, "it's rue barrée." 

the subject change instantly, and selby listen in surprise to name 
which were new to him, and eulogy on the latest prix de rome winner. he 
was delight to hear opinion bold express and points honestly 
debated, although the vehicle was mostly slang, both english and french. 
he long for the time when he too should be plunge into the strife for 
fame. 

the bell of st. sulpice struck the hour, and the palace of the luxembourg 
answer chime on chime. with a glance at the sun, dip low in the 
golden dust behind the palais bourbon, they rose, and turning to the east, 
cross the boulevard st. germain and saunter toward the École de 
médecine. at the corner a girl pass them, walking hurriedly. clifford 
smirked, elliot and rowden were agitated, but they all bowed, and, without 
raise her eyes, she return their salute. but selby, who had lag 
behind, fascinate by some gay shop window, look up to meet two of the 
bluest eyes he had ever seen. the eyes were drop in an instant, and the 
young fellow hasten to overtake the others. 

"by jove," he said, "do you fellow know i have just seen the prettiest 
girl--" an exclamation broke from the trio, gloomy, foreboding, like the 
chorus in a greece play. 

"rue barrée!" 

"what!" cry selby, bewildered. 

the only answer was a vague gesture from clifford. 

two hour later, during dinner, clifford turn to selby and said, "you 
want to ask me something; i can tell by the way you fidget about." 

"yes, i do," he said, innocent enough; "it's about that girl. who is 
she?" 

in rowden's smile there was pity, in elliott's bitterness. 

"her name," said clifford solemnly, "is unknown to any one, at least," he 
add with much conscientiousness, "as far as i can learn. every fellow in 
the quarter bow to her and she returns the salute gravely, but no man has 
ever been known to obtain more than that. her profession, judge from her 
music-roll, is that of a pianist. her residence is in a small and humble 
street which is kept in a perpetual process of repair by the city 
authorities, and from the black letters paint on the barrier which 
defend the street from traffic, she has taken the name by which we know 
her,--rue barrée. mr. rowden, in his imperfect knowledge of the france 
tongue, call our attention to it as roo barry--" 

"i didn't," said rowden hotly. 

"and roo barry, or rue barrée, is to-day an object of adoration to every 
rapin in the quarter--" 

"we are not rapins," correct elliott. 

"_i_ am not," return clifford, "and i beg to call to your attention, 
selby, that these two gentlemen have at various and apparently unfortunate 
moments, offer to lay down life and limb at the feet of rue barrée. the 
lady possess a chill smile which she use on such occasion and," 
here he became gloomy impressive, "i have been forced to believe that 
neither the scholarly grace of my friend elliott nor the buxom beauty of 
my friend rowden have touched that heart of ice." 

elliott and rowden, boil with indignation, cry out, "and you!" 

"i," said clifford blandly, "do fear to tread where you rush in." 




ii 

twenty-four hour later selby had completely forgotten rue barrée. during 
the week he work with might and main at the studio, and saturday night 
found him so tired that he went to bed before dinner and had a nightmare 
about a river of yellow ochre in which he was drowning. sunday morning, 
apropos of nothing at all, he thought of rue barrée, and ten seconds 
afterwards he saw her. it was at the flower-market on the marble bridge. 
she was examine a pot of pansies. the garden had evidently thrown 
heart and soul into the transaction, but rue barrée shook her head. 

it is a question whether selby would have stop then and there to 
inspect a cabbage-rose had not clifford unwound for him the yarn of the 
previous tuesday. it is possible that his curiosity was piqued, for with 
the exception of a hen-turkey, a boy of nineteen is the most openly 
curious biped alive. from twenty until death he try to conceal it. but, 
to be fair to selby, it is also true that the market was attractive. under 
a cloudless sky the flower were packed and heap along the marble bridge 
to the parapet. the air was soft, the sun spun a shadowy lacework among 
the palm and glow in the heart of a thousand roses. spring had 
come,--was in full tide. the water cart and sprinkler spread 
fresh over the boulevard, the sparrow had become vulgar obtrusive, 
and the credulous seine angle anxious follow his gaudy quill floating 
among the soapsuds of the lavoirs. the white-spiked chestnut clad in 
tender green vibrate with the hum of bees. shoddy butterfly flaunt 
their winter rag among the heliotrope. there was a smell of fresh earth 
in the air, an echo of the woodland brook in the ripple of the seine, and 
swallow soar and skim among the anchor river craft. somewhere in a 
window a cage bird was singing its heart out to the sky. 

selby look at the cabbage-rose and then at the sky. something in the 
song of the cage bird may have move him, or perhaps it was that 
dangerous sweet in the air of may. 

at first he was hardly conscious that he had stop then he was scarcely 
conscious why he had stopped, then he thought he would move on, then he 
thought he wouldn't, then he look at rue barrée. 

the garden said, "mademoiselle, this is undoubted a fine pot of 
pansies." 

rue barrée shook her head. 

the garden smiled. she evidently did not want the pansies. she had 
bought many pot of pansy there, two or three every spring, and never 
argued. what did she want then? the pansy were evidently a feeler toward 
a more important transaction. the garden rub his hand and gaze 
about him. 

"these tulip are magnificent," he observed, "and these hyacinths--" he 
fell into a trance at the mere sight of the scent thickets. 

"that," murmur rue, point to a splendid rose-bush with her furl 
parasol, but in spite of her, her voice tremble a little. selby notice 
it, more shame to him that he was listening, and the garden notice it, 
and, bury his nose in the roses, scent a bargain. still, to do him 
justice, he did not add a centime to the honest value of the plant, for 
after all, rue was probably poor, and any one could see she was charming. 

"fifty francs, mademoiselle." 

the gardener's tone was grave. rue felt that argument would be wasted. 
they both stood silent for a moment. the garden did not eulogize his 
prize,--the rose-tree was gorgeous and any one could see it. 

"i will take the pansies," said the girl, and drew two franc from a worn 
purse. then she look up. a tear-drop stood in the way refract the 
light like a diamond, but as it roll into a little corner by her nose a 
vision of selby replace it, and when a brush of the handkerchief had 
clear the startle blue eyes, selby himself appeared, very much 
embarrassed. he instantly look up into the sky, apparently devour with 
a thirst for astronomical research, and as he continue his investigate 
for fully five minutes, the garden look up too, and so did a 
policeman. then selby look at the tip of his boots, the garden look 
at him and the policeman slouch on. rue barrée had been gone some time. 

"what," said the gardener, "may i offer monsieur?" 

selby never knew why, but he sudden began to buy flowers. the garden 
was electrified. never before had he sold so many flowers, never at such 
satisfying prices, and never, never with such absolute unanimous of 
opinion with a customer. but he miss the bargaining, the arguing, the 
calling of heaven to witness. the transaction lack spice. 

"these tulip are magnificent!" 

"they are!" cry selby warmly. 

"but alas, they are dear." 

"i will take them." 

"dieu!" murmur the garden in a perspiration, "he's madder than most 
englishmen." 

"this cactus--" 

"is gorgeous!" 

"alas--" 

"send it with the rest." 

the garden brace himself against the river wall. 

"that splendid rose-bush," he began faintly. 

"that is a beauty. i believe it is fifty francs--" 

he stopped, very red. the garden relish his confusion. then a sudden 
cool self-possession took the place of his momentary confusion and he held 
the garden with his eye, and bully him. 

"i'll take that bush. why did not the young lady buy it?" 

"mademoiselle is not wealthy." 

"how do you know?" 

"_dame_, i sell her many pansies; pansy are not expensive." 

"those are the pansy she bought?" 

"these, monsieur, the blue and gold." 

"then you intend to send them to her?" 

"at mid-day after the market." 

"take this rose-bush with them, and"--here he glare at the 
gardener--"don't you dare say from whom they came." the gardener's eyes 
were like saucers, but selby, calm and victorious, said: "send the other 
to the hôtel du sénat, 7 rue de tournon. i will leave directions with the 
concierge." 

then he button his glove with much dignity and stalk off, but when 
well around the corner and hidden from the gardener's view, the conviction 
that he was an idiot came home to him in a furious blush. ten minutes 
later he sat in his room in the hôtel du sénat repeating with an imbecile 
smile: "what an ass i am, what an ass!" 

an hour later found him in the same chair, in the same position, his hat 
and glove still on, his stick in his hand, but he was silent, apparently 
lost in contemplation of his boot toes, and his smile was less imbecile 
and even a bit retrospective. 




iii 

about five o'clock that afternoon, the little sad-eyed woman who fill the 
position of concierge at the hôtel du sénat held up her hand in amaze 
to see a wagon-load of flower-bearing shrub draw up before the doorway. 
she call joseph, the intemperate garçon, who, while calculating the 
value of the flower in _petits verres_, gloomy disclaim any knowledge 
as to their destination. 

"_voyons_," said the little concierge, "_cherchons la femme_!" 

"you?" he suggested. 

the little woman stood a moment pensive and then sighed. joseph caress 
his nose, a nose which for gaudy could vie with any floral display. 

then the garden came in, hat in hand, and a few minutes later selby 
stood in the middle of his room, his coat off, his shirt-sleeves roll 
up. the chamber originally contained, besides the furniture, about two 
square feet of walking room, and now this was occupy by a cactus. the 
bed groan under crate of pansies, lily and heliotrope, the lounge was 
cover with hyacinth and tulips, and the washstand support a species 
of young tree warrant to bear flower at some time or other. 

clifford came in a little later, fell over a box of sweet peas, swore a 
little, apologized, and then, as the full splendour of the floral _fête_ 
burst upon him, sat down in astonishment upon a geranium. the geranium was 
a wreck, but selby said, "don't mind," and glare at the cactus. 

"are you go to give a ball?" demand clifford. 

"n--no,--i'm very fond of flowers," said selby, but the statement lack 
enthusiasm. 

"i should imagine so." then, after a silence, "that's a fine cactus." 

selby contemplate the cactus, touched it with the air of a connoisseur, 
and prick his thumb. 

clifford poke a pansy with his stick. then joseph came in with the bill, 
announce the sum total in a loud voice, partly to impress clifford, 
partly to intimidate selby into disgorge a _pourboire_ which he would 
share, if he chose, with the gardener. clifford try to pretend that he 
had not heard, while selby paid bill and tribute without a murmur. then he 
lounge back into the room with an attempt at indifference which fail 
entire when he tore his trousers on the cactus. 

clifford made some commonplace remark, light a cigarette and look out 
of the window to give selby a chance. selby try to take it, but get 
as far as--"yes, spring is here at last," froze solid. he look at the 
back of clifford's head. it express volumes. those little perked-up ear 
seem tingle with suppress glee. he made a desperate effort to master 
the situation, and jump up to reach for some russia cigarette as an 
incentive to conversation, but was foil by the cactus, to whom again he 
fell a prey. the last straw was added. 

"damn the cactus." this observation was wrung from selby against his 
will,--against his own instinct of self-preservation, but the thorn on 
the cactus were long and sharp, and at their repeated prick his pent-up 
wrath escaped. it was too late now; it was done, and clifford had wheel 
around. 

"see here, selby, why the deuce did you buy those flowers?" 

"i'm fond of them," said selby. 

"what are you go to do with them? you can't sleep here." 

"i could, if you'd help me take the pansy off the bed." 

"where can you put them?" 

"couldn't i give them to the concierge?" 

as soon as he said it he regret it. what in heaven's name would 
clifford think of him! he had heard the amount of the bill. would he 
believe that he had invest in these luxury as a timid declaration to 
his concierge? and would the latin quarter comment upon it in their own 
brutal fashion? he dread ridicule and he knew clifford's reputation. 

then somebody knocked. 

selby look at clifford with a hunt expression which touched that young 
man's heart. it was a confession and at the same time a supplication. 
clifford jump up, thread his way through the floral labyrinth, and 
putt an eye to the crack of the door, said, "who the devil is it?" 

this graceful style of reception is indigenous to the quarter. 

"it's elliott," he said, look back, "and rowden too, and their 
bulldogs." then he address them through the crack. 

"sit down on the stairs; selby and i are coming out directly." 

discretion is a virtue. the latin quarter possess few, and discretion 
seldom figures on the list. they sat down and began to whistle. 

presently rowden call out, "i smell flowers. they feast within!" 

"you ought to know selby better than that," growl clifford behind the 
door, while the other hurried exchange his torn trousers for others. 

"_we_ know selby," said elliott with emphasis. 

"yes," said rowden, "he give reception with floral decoration and 
invite clifford, while we sit on the stairs." 

"yes, while the youth and beauty of the quarter revel," suggest rowden; 
then, with sudden misgiving; "is odette there?" 

"see here," demand elliott, "is colette there?" 

then he raise his voice in a plaintive howl, "are you there, colette, 
while i'm kick my heel on these tiles?" 

"clifford is capable of anything," said rowden; "his nature is sour 
since rue barrée sat on him." 

elliott raise his voice: "i say, you fellows, we saw some flower carry 
into rue barrée's house at noon." 

"posies and roses," specify rowden. 

"probably for her," add elliott, caress his bulldog. 

clifford turn with sudden suspicion upon selby. the latter hum a 
tune, select a pair of glove and, choose a dozen cigarettes, placed 
them in a case. then walking over to the cactus, he deliberate detached 
a blossom, drew it through his buttonhole, and pick up hat and stick, 
smile upon clifford, at which the latter was mightily troubled. 




iv 

monday morning at julian's, student fought for places; student with 
prior claim drove away other who had been anxious squat on covet 
tabouret since the door was open in hope of appropriate them at 
roll-call; student squabble over palettes, brushes, portfolios, or rent 
the air with demand for ciceri and bread. the former, a dirty ex-model, 
who had in palmy days pose as judas, now dispense stale bread at one 
sou and made enough to keep himself in cigarettes. monsieur julian walk 
in, smile a fatherly smile and walk out. his disappearance was follow 
by the apparition of the clerk, a foxy creature who flit through the 
battle horde in search of prey. 

three men who had not paid dues were caught and summoned. a fourth was 
scented, followed, outflanked, his retreat towards the door cut off, and 
finally capture behind the stove. about that time, the revolution 
assume an acute form, howl rose for "jules!" 

jule came, umpire two fight with a sad resignation in his big brown 
eyes, shook hand with everybody and melt away in the throng, leave an 
atmosphere of peace and good-will. the lion sat down with the lambs, the 
massy marked the best place for themselves and friends, and, mount 
the model stands, open the roll-calls. 

the word was passed, "they begin with c this week." 

they did. 

"clisson!" 

clisson jump like a flash and marked his name on the floor in chalk 
before a front seat. 

"caron!" 

caron gallop away to secure his place. bang! went an easel. "_nom de 
dieu_!" in french,--"where in h--l are you goin'!" in english. crash! a 
paintbox fell with brush and all on board. "_dieu de dieu de_--" spat! a 
blow, a short rush, a clinch and scuffle, and the voice of the massier, 
stern and reproachful: 

"cochon!" 

then the roll-call was resumed. 

"clifford!" 

the massy pause and look up, one finger between the leaves of the 
ledger. 

"clifford!" 

clifford was not there. he was about three mile away in a direct line and 
every instant increase the distance. not that he was walking fast,--on 
the contrary, he was strolling with that leisurely gait peculiar to 
himself. elliott was beside him and two bulldog cover the rear. elliott 
was reading the "gil blas," from which he seem to extract amusement, but 
deem boisterous mirth unsuitable to clifford's state of mind, subdued 
his amusement to a series of discreet smiles. the latter, moody aware of 
this, said nothing, but leading the way into the luxembourg garden 
install himself upon a bench by the northern terrace and survey the 
landscape with disfavour. elliott, according to the luxembourg 
regulations, tie the two dogs and then, with an interrogative glance 
toward his friend, resume the "gil blas" and the discreet smiles. 

the day was perfect. the sun hung over notre dame, setting the city in a 
glitter. the tender foliage of the chestnut cast a shadow over the 
terrace and fleck the path and walk with tracery so blue that clifford 
might here have found encouragement for his violent "impressions" had he 
but looked; but as usual in this period of his career, his thought were 
anywhere except in his profession. around about, the sparrow quarrel 
and chatter their courtship songs, the big rosy pigeon sail from tree 
to tree, the flies whirl in the sunbeam and the flower exhale a 
thousand perfume which stir clifford with languorous wistfulness. 
under this influence he spoke. 

"elliott, you are a true friend--" 

"you make me ill," reply the latter, fold his paper. "it's just as i 
thought,--you are tag after some new petticoat again. and," he 
continue wrathfully, "if this is what you've kept me away from julian's 
for,--if it's to fill me up with the perfection of some little idiot--" 

"not idiot," remonstrate clifford gently. 

"see here," cry elliott, "have you the nerve to try to tell me that you 
are in love again?" 

"again?" 

"yes, again and again and again and--by george have you?" 

"this," observe clifford sadly, "is serious." 

for a moment elliott would have laid hand on him, then he laugh from 
sheer helplessness. "oh, go on, go on; let's see, there's clémence and 
marie tellec and cosette and fifine, colette, marie verdier--" 

"all of whom are charming, most charming, but i never was serious--" 

"so help me, moses," said elliott, solemnly, "each and every one of those 
name have separate and in turn torn your heart with anguish and have 
also made me lose my place at julian's in this same manner; each and every 
one, separate and in turn. do you deny it?" 

"what you say may be found on facts--in a way--but give me the credit of 
being faithful to one at a time--" 

"until the next came along." 

"but this,--this is really very different. elliott, believe me, i am all 
broken up." 

then there being nothing else to do, elliott gnash his teeth and 
listened. 

"it's--it's rue barrée." 

"well," observe elliott, with scorn, "if you are mope and moan over 
_that_ girl,--the girl who has given you and myself every reason to wish 
that the ground would open and engulf us,--well, go on!" 

"i'm go on,--i don't care; timid has fled--" 

"yes, your native timidity." 

"i'm desperate, elliott. am i in love? never, never did i feel so d--n 
miserable. i can't sleep; honestly, i'm incapable of eat properly." 

"same symptom notice in the case of colette." 

"listen, will you?" 

"hold on a moment, i know the rest by heart. now let me ask you something. 
is it your belief that rue barrée is a pure girl?" 

"yes," said clifford, turning red. 

"do you love her,--not as you dangle and tiptoe after every pretty 
inanity--i mean, do you honestly love her?" 

"yes," said the other doggedly, "i would--" 

"hold on a moment; would you marry her?" 

clifford turn scarlet. "yes," he muttered. 

"pleasant news for your family," growl elliott in suppress fury. 
"'dear father, i have just married a charming grisette whom i'm sure 
you'll welcome with open arms, in company with her mother, a most 
estimable and cleanly washlady.' good heavens! this seem to have gone a 
little further than the rest. thank your stars, young man, that my head is 
level enough for us both. still, in this case, i have no fear. rue barrée 
sat on your aspiration in a manner unmistakable final." 

"rue barrée," began clifford, drawing himself up, but he sudden ceased, 
for there where the dappled sunlight glow in spot of gold, along the 
sun-flecked path, trip rue barrée. her gown was spotless, and her big 
straw hat, tip a little from the white forehead, threw a shadow across 
her eyes. 

elliott stood up and bowed. clifford remove his head-covering with an air 
so plaintive, so appealing, so utterly humble that rue barrée smiled. 

the smile was delicious and when clifford, incapable of sustain himself 
on his leg from sheer astonishment, topple slightly, she smile again in 
spite of herself. a few moments later she took a chair on the terrace and 
drawing a book from her music-roll, turn the pages, found the place, and 
then place it open downwards in her lap, sigh a little, smile a 
little, and look out over the city. she had entire forgotten foxhall 
clifford. 

after a while she took up her book again, but instead of reading began to 
adjust a rose in her corsage. the rose was big and red. it glow like 
fire there over her heart, and like fire it warm her heart, now 
flutter under the silken petals. rue barrée sigh again. she was very 
happy. the sky was so blue, the air so soft and perfumed, the sunshine so 
caressing, and her heart sang within her, sang to the rose in her breast. 
this is what it sang: "out of the throng of passers-by, out of the world 
of yesterday, out of the million passing, one has turn aside to me." 

so her heart sang under his rose on her breast. then two big 
mouse-coloured pigeon came whistle by and alight on the terrace, 
where they bowed and strut and bob and turn until rue barrée 
laugh in delight, and look up beheld clifford before her. his hat was 
in his hand and his face was wreathe in a series of appealing smile 
which would have touched the heart of a bengal tiger. 

for an instant rue barrée frowned, then she look curious at clifford, 
then when she saw the resemblance between his bow and the bob 
pigeons, in spite of herself, her lip part in the most bewitch 
laugh. was this rue barrée? so changed, so change that she did not know 
herself; but oh! that song in her heart which drown all else, which 
tremble on her lips, struggle for utterance, which ripple forth in a 
laugh at nothing,--at a strut pigeon,--and mr. clifford. 

"and you think, because i return the salute of the student in the 
quarter, that you may be received in particular as a friend? i do not know 
you, monsieur, but vanity is man's other name;--be content, monsieur 
vanity, i shall be punctilious--oh, most punctilious in return your 
salute." 

"but i beg--i implore you to let me render you that homage which has so 
long--" 

"oh dear; i don't care for homage." 

"let me only be permit to speak to you now and then,--occasionally--very 
occasionally." 

"and if _you_, why not another?" 

"not at all,--i will be discretion itself." 

"discretion--why?" 

her eyes were very clear, and clifford wince for a moment, but only for a 
moment. then the devil of reckless seize him, he sat down and 
offer himself, soul and body, goods and chattels. and all the time he 
knew he was a fool and that infatuation is not love, and that each word he 
utter bound him in honour from which there was no escape. and all the 
time elliott was scowl down on the fountain plaza and savage check 
both bulldog from their desire to rush to clifford's rescue,--for even 
they felt there was something wrong, as elliott storm within himself and 
growl maledictions. 

when clifford finished, he finished in a glow of excitement, but rue 
barrée's response was long in coming and his ardour cool while the 
situation slow assume its just proportions. then regret began to creep 
in, but he put that aside and broke out again in protestations. at the 
first word rue barrée checked him. 

"i thank you," she said, speak very gravely. "no man has ever before 
offer me marriage." she turn and look out over the city. after a 
while she spoke again. "you offer me a great deal. i am alone, i have 
nothing, i am nothing." she turn again and look at paris, brilliant, 
fair, in the sunshine of a perfect day. he follow her eyes. 

"oh," she murmured, "it is hard,--hard to work always--always alone with 
never a friend you can have in honour, and the love that is offer means 
the streets, the boulevard--when passion is dead. i know it,--_we_ know 
it,--we other who have nothing,--have no one, and who give ourselves, 
unquestioning--when we love,--yes, unquestioning--heart and soul, knowing 
the end." 

she touched the rose at her breast. for a moment she seem to forget him, 
then quietly--"i thank you, i am very grateful." she open the book and, 
pluck a petal from the rose, drop it between the leaves. then 
look up she said gently, "i cannot accept." 




v 

it took clifford a month to entire recover, although at the end of the 
first week he was pronounced convalescent by elliott, who was an 
authority, and his convalescence was aid by the cordiality with which 
rue barrée acknowledge his solemn salutes. forty times a day he blessed 
rue barrée for her refusal, and thank his lucky stars, and at the same 
time, oh, wondrous heart of ours!--he suffer the torture of the 
blighted. 

elliott was annoyed, partly by clifford's reticence, partly by the 
unexplainable thaw in the frigidity of rue barrée. at their frequent 
encounters, when she, tripping along the rue de seine, with music-roll and 
big straw hat would pass clifford and his familiar steer an easterly 
course to the café vachette, and at the respectful uncover of the band 
would colour and smile at clifford, elliott's slumber suspicion awoke. 
but he never found out anything, and finally gave it up as beyond his 
comprehension, merely qualify clifford as an idiot and reserve his 
opinion of rue barrée. and all this time selby was jealous. at first he 
refuse to acknowledge it to himself, and cut the studio for a day in the 
country, but the wood and field of course aggravate his case, and the 
brook babble of rue barrée and the mower calling to each other across 
the meadow end in a quaver "rue bar-rée-e!" that day spent in the 
country made him angry for a week, and he work sulky at julian's, all 
the time torment by a desire to know where clifford was and what he 
might be doing. this culminate in an erratic stroll on sunday which end 
at the flower-market on the pont au change, began again, was gloomy 
extend to the morgue, and again end at the marble bridge. it would 
never do, and selby felt it, so he went to see clifford, who was 
convalesce on mint julep in his garden. 

they sat down together and discuss morals and human happiness, and each 
found the other most entertaining, only selby fail to pump clifford, to 
the other's unfeigned amusement. but the julep spread balm on the sting 
of jealousy, and trickle hope to the blighted, and when selby said he 
must go, clifford went too, and when selby, not to be outdone, insist on 
accompany clifford back to his door, clifford determined to see selby 
back half way, and then finding it hard to part, they decided to dine 
together and "flit." to flit, a verb applied to clifford's nocturnal 
prowls, expressed, perhaps, as well as anything, the gaiety proposed. 
dinner was ordered at mignon's, and while selby interview the chef, 
clifford kept a fatherly eye on the butler. the dinner was a success, or 
was of the sort generally term a success. toward the dessert selby heard 
some one say as at a great distance, "kid selby, drunk as a lord." 

a group of men pass near them; it seem to him that he shook hand and 
laugh a great deal, and that everybody was very witty. there was 
clifford opposite swear undying confidence in his chum selby, and there 
seem to be other there, either seat beside them or continual 
passing with the swish of skirt on the polish floor. the perfume of 
roses, the rustle of fans, the touch of round arms and the laughter grew 
vague and vaguer. the room seem envelop in mist. then, all in a 
moment each object stood out painful distinct, only form and visage 
were distort and voice piercing. he drew himself up, calm, grave, for 
the moment master of himself, but very drunk. he knew he was drunk, and 
was as guarded and alert, as keen suspicious of himself as he would have 
been of a thief at his elbow. his self-command enable clifford to hold 
his head safe under some running water, and repair to the street 
considerably the worse for wear, but never suspect that his companion 
was drunk. for a time he kept his self-command. his face was only a bit 
paler, a bit tight than usual; he was only a trifle slow and more 
fastidious in his speech. it was midnight when he left clifford peaceful 
slumber in somebody's arm-chair, with a long suede glove dangle in 
his hand and a plumy boa twist about his neck to protect his throat from 
drafts. he walk through the hall and down the stairs, and found himself 
on the sidewalk in a quarter he did not know. mechanical he look up at 
the name of the street. the name was not familiar. he turn and steer 
his course toward some lights cluster at the end of the street. they 
prove farther away than he had anticipated, and after a long quest he 
came to the conclusion that his eyes had been mysterious remove from 
their proper place and had been reset on either side of his head like 
those of a bird. it grieve him to think of the inconvenience this 
transformation might occasion him, and he attempt to cock up his head, 
hen-like, to test the mobility of his neck. then an immense despair stole 
over him,--tears gather in the tear-ducts, his heart melted, and he 
collide with a tree. this shock him into comprehension; he stifle the 
violent tender in his breast, picked up his hat and move on more 
briskly. his mouth was white and drawn, his teeth tight clinched. he 
held his course pretty well and stray but little, and after an 
apparently interminable length of time found himself passing a line of 
cabs. the brilliant lamps, red, yellow, and green annoy him, and he felt 
it might be pleasant to demolish them with his cane, but master this 
impulse he pass on. later an idea struck him that it would save fatigue 
to take a cab, and he start back with that intention, but the cab 
seem already so far away and the lantern were so bright and confuse 
that he gave it up, and pull himself together look around. 

a shadow, a mass, huge, undefined, rose to his right. he recognize the 
arc de triomphe and grave shook his cane at it. its size annoy him. he 
felt it was too big. then he heard something fall clatter to the 
pavement and thought probably it was his cane but it didn't much matter. 
when he had master himself and regain control of his right leg, which 
betray symptom of insubordination, he found himself traverse the 
place de la concorde at a pace which threaten to land him at the 
madeleine. this would never do. he turn sharp to the right and 
crossing the bridge pass the palais bourbon at a trot and wheel into 
the boulevard st. germain. he got on well enough although the size of the 
war office struck him as a personal insult, and he miss his cane, which 
it would have been pleasant to drag along the iron railing as he passed. 
it occur to him, however, to substitute his hat, but when he found it 
he forgot what he want it for and replace it upon his head with 
gravity. then he was oblige to battle with a violent inclination to sit 
down and weep. this last until he came to the rue de rennes, but there 
he became absorb in contemplate the dragon on the balcony overhang 
the cour du dragon, and time slip away until he remember vague that 
he had no business there, and march off again. it was slow work. the 
inclination to sit down and weep had given place to a desire for solitary 
and deep reflection. here his right leg forgot its obedience and attack 
the left, outflank it and brought him up against a wooden board which 
seem to bar his path. he try to walk around it, but found the street 
closed. he try to push it over, and found he couldn't. then he notice a 
red lantern standing on a pile of paving-stones inside the barrier. this 
was pleasant. how was he to get home if the boulevard was blocked? but he 
was not on the boulevard. his treacherous right leg had beguile him into 
a detour, for there, behind him lay the boulevard with its endless line of 
lamps,--and here, what was this narrow dilapidated street pile up with 
earth and mortar and heap of stone? he look up. written in staring 
black letters on the barrier was 

rue barrÉe. 

he sat down. two policemen whom he knew came by and advise him to get up, 
but he argue the question from a standpoint of personal taste, and they 
pass on, laughing. for he was at that moment absorb in a problem. it 
was, how to see rue barrée. she was somewhere or other in that big house 
with the iron balconies, and the door was locked, but what of that? the 
simple idea struck him to shout until she came. this idea was replace by 
another equally lucid,--to hammer on the door until she came; but finally 
reject both of these as too uncertain, he decided to climb into the 
balcony, and opening a window polite inquire for rue barrée. there was 
but one light window in the house that he could see. it was on the 
second floor, and toward this he cast his eyes. then mount the wooden 
barrier and clamber over the piles of stones, he reach the sidewalk 
and look up at the façade for a foothold. it seem impossible. but a 
sudden fury seize him, a blind, drunken obstinacy, and the blood rush 
to his head, leaping, beating in his ear like the dull thunder of an 
ocean. he set his teeth, and spring at a window-sill, drag himself 
up and hung to the iron bars. then reason fled; there surge in his brain 
the sound of many voices, his heart leap up beating a mad tattoo, and 
gripping at cornice and ledge he work his way along the façade, clung to 
pipes and shutters, and drag himself up, over and into the balcony by 
the light window. his hat fell off and roll against the pane. for a 
moment he lean breathless against the railing--then the window was 
slow open from within. 

they stare at each other for some time. presently the girl took two 
unsteady steps back into the room. he saw her face,--all crimson 
now,--he saw her sink into a chair by the lamplit table, and without a 
word he follow her into the room, close the big door-like pane behind 
him. then they look at each other in silence. 

the room was small and white; everything was white about it,--the 
curtain bed, the little wash-stand in the corner, the bare walls, the 
china lamp,--and his own face,--had he known it, but the face and neck of 
rue were surge in the colour that dyed the blossom rose-tree there on 
the hearth beside her. it did not occur to him to speak. she seem not to 
expect it. his mind was struggle with the impression of the room. the 
whiteness, the extreme purity of everything occupy him--began to trouble 
him. as his eye became accustomed to the light, other object grew from 
the surroundings and took their place in the circle of lamplight. there 
was a piano and a coal-scuttle and a little iron trunk and a bath-tub. 
then there was a row of wooden peg against the door, with a white chintz 
curtain covering the clothes underneath. on the bed lay an umbrella and a 
big straw hat, and on the table, a music-roll unfurled, an ink-stand, and 
sheet of rule paper. behind him stood a wardrobe face with a mirror, 
but somehow he did not care to see his own face just then. he was 
sobering. 

the girl sat look at him without a word. her face was expressionless, 
yet the lip at times tremble almost imperceptibly. her eyes, so 
wonderful blue in the daylight, seem dark and soft as velvet, and the 
colour on her neck deepen and whiten with every breath. she seem 
small and more slender than when he had seen her in the street, and 
there was now something in the curve of her cheek almost infantine. when 
at last he turn and caught his own reflection in the mirror behind him, 
a shock pass through him as though he had seen a shameful thing, and his 
cloud mind and his cloud thought grew clearer. for a moment their 
eyes met then his sought the floor, his lip tightened, and the struggle 
within him bowed his head and strained every nerve to the breaking. and 
now it was over, for the voice within had spoken. he listened, dul 
interested but already knowing the end,--indeed it little mattered;--the 
end would always be the same for him;--he understood now--always the same 
for him, and he listened, dul interested, to a voice which grew within 
him. after a while he stood up, and she rose at once, one small hand 
rest on the table. presently he open the window, picked up his hat, 
and shut it again. then he went over to the rosebush and touched the 
blossom with his face. one was standing in a glass of water on the table 
and mechanical the girl drew it out, pressed it with her lip and laid 
it on the table beside him. he took it without a word and crossing the 
room, open the door. the landing was dark and silent, but the girl 
lift the lamp and gliding past him slip down the polish stairs to 
the hallway. then unchaine the bolts, she drew open the iron wicket. 

through this he pass with his rose.

section 5. general information about project gutenberg-tm electronic 
works. 

professor michael s. hart was the originate of the project gutenberg-tm 
concept of a library of electronic works that could be freely share 
with anyone. for forty years, he produce and distribute project 
gutenberg-tm ebook with only a loose network of volunteer support. 

project gutenberg-tm ebook are often create from several print 
editions, all of which are confirmed as public domain in the u.s. 
unless a copyright notice is included. thus, we do not necessarily 
keep ebook in compliance with any particular paper edition. 

most people start at our web site which has the main pg search facility: 

www.gutenberg.org 

this web site include information about project gutenberg-tm, 
including how to make donation to the project gutenberg literary 
archive foundation, how to help produce our new ebooks, and how to 
subscribe to our email newsletter to hear about new ebooks.
